# The Register of Tides

# Chapter 1

By the time the loudspeaker found the hut, Halima had two fingers inside Rokeya and the baby's head jammed hot and hard against her knuckles.

The voice came thin at first, dragged over water and tin. People on Kadam Char were used to voices arriving warped from the river, traders calling fish rates, the muezzin from a farther bank when the wind tilted right, boys shouting from boats as if the whole char were one yard. This one had the flat-bellied force of office Bangla. It kept repeating a number, a memo, a date. Then it said the name of the island cleanly enough that every person in the hut went still for one wrong beat.

"Don't stop now," Halima snapped.

Rokeya had stopped anyway. She lay half on her side on the jute sack they had spread over the packed floor, one knee sliding open, one heel grinding mud into the weave, her glass bangles shoved high up her left arm because she refused to take them off even in labor. Sweat had pasted her hair to her cheeks. She was nineteen and vain enough to worry, between pains, whether the baby would flatten her nose. Halima liked her for that. A girl who could think about her face with a child halfway out of her had some spite in her yet.

Outside, the mother-in-law started wailing to God before there was anything yet worth wailing over. The old woman had come into the room three times already and had been pushed out three times already. She had a wet sandal tied at the strap with blue thread and a way of pressing both hands to her chest as if every event in the world had been designed to injure her personally.

Rokeya's younger sister Nasima, who still had silver wedding glitter stuck at one temple from a cousin's feast, hissed from the doorway, "Ma, quiet."

The amplified voice rolled past again, nearer now, the district launch circling slow in the channel where the char's edge broke into reeds and fresh-eaten mud. All residents. Immediate assembly. Government order regarding erosion resettlement. Attend at the primary school. Bring existing papers. The words struck the tin roof and came down dented and metallic.

Rokeya made a sound like she was trying to retch and cry at once. "Dai-ma, it's stuck. I told you it's stuck."

"It's not stuck. You're holding it like a miser holds coins." Halima braced her palm low against Rokeya's belly, feeling the baby's shoulder tilted wrong beneath the skin. Heat slicked her forearms. The hut smelled of iron blood, wood smoke, mustard oil, and the old damp that never fully left anything in late monsoon. A fly kept returning to the bowl where Halima had set the boiled razor and black thread. She blew it away without looking.

"Listen to me. When the pain comes, you don't push into your own throat. You push down. Here. Into my hand. Into the floor. If you bite the sound off again I'll slap you."

Rokeya laughed once, a raw little bark, because Halima had slapped women before and everybody knew it. Then the next pain took her. The laugh broke. Her fingers clawed for the rolled quilt behind her back. Halima moved fast, hauling one leg higher, turning the baby's head the width of a thumb, coaxing the shoulder free with the patience she no longer had for most other things in life.

Outside, feet thudded in mud. Men were already running toward the school, carrying nothing, because men always believed the first trip to news should be made with empty hands. A child shouted, "They're saying we'll all be moved." Another child laughed because adults had gone strange.

Halima leaned closer until Rokeya had to see the clouded left eye and the clear right one both. "This child is not waiting for the government. Now."

Rokeya bore down. The tendon in her neck stood out like rope. Something gave. Halima caught the head in both palms, wet black hair slick against her skin, and felt at once the bad danger passing and the next smaller danger arriving. She checked the neck for cord. Clear. Good. One shoulder, then the other, then the whole narrow body slid into her hands with a spill of blood and fluid warm enough to seem almost alive itself.

At that exact moment the loudspeaker said Kadam Char in the tone officials used when they had already put a place into a file.

The baby opened her mouth and nothing came.

Rokeya's eyes went blind with terror. "Why isn't she crying?"

"Because she's busy being born." Halima cleared the mouth with her little finger, rubbed the back hard, tipped the child, rubbed again. There. A cough first, offended and wet. Then the cry, sharp enough to cut through the loudspeaker, through the sandals splashing past outside, through the mother-in-law's fresh praise to God. A girl. Thin, furious, alive.

Rokeya started sobbing at once, from relief more than joy. "Girl?"

"Girl," Halima said.

The mother-in-law made a disappointed sound before she could stop herself. Halima did not bother hiding her glare. The old woman put on grief for the baby's sex only because there was an audience for it. By sunset she would be crooning over the child and swearing she had always preferred granddaughters.

Halima tied the cord with black thread in two quick knots and cut between them. Blood striped her thumb. The placenta had not come yet. She waited, one hand firm on Rokeya's belly, listening with half an ear to the launch outside as if it were an ugly insect that had found a way into the room.

The order repeated. Erosion emergency. Verified households. Compensation disbursement subject to documentary review. All residents to assemble. It had the oily confidence of language composed where the river could not reach.

Rokeya, still crying, said, "Dai-ma, what's verified?"

"Nothing good," Halima said.

The baby rooted blindly against the air. Halima put the child to the mother's breast and pressed again for the afterbirth. It came sluggishly, a dark collapse into the brass bowl, and with it the last of the morning's privacy. Once the placenta was out and she was satisfied nothing more was tearing than ought to tear, she washed her hands in water already pinking in the basin. Her bag lay open by the wall: old cloths, bottle of tincture, string, the tin of balm she had carried long enough for the label to wear away. Ordinary things. Work things. The kind of things a government notice never remembered existed.

When she stepped to the doorway, the char was already leaning toward the school.

People moved through the mud in streams that split and rejoined around puddles, chickens, tethered goats, children too young to understand why being shouted at should make them hurry. The launch sat off the bank with two men and a loudspeaker horn fixed to its roof. Its wake slapped the eaten edge of the char, and a slice of bank gave way with a soft chunking sound, as if the river were biting while the order was being read.

Babul, Rokeya's husband, came stumbling from behind the hut, still with fish scales silvering one forearm because he had been cleaning a net line when the labor turned bad. He wore one toenail black from some old engine accident and always rolled his sleeves twice too neat for a man his age. He peered over Halima's shoulder, afraid to ask first about the baby or his wife in case he chose wrong.

"Both alive," Halima said.

His face emptied and filled again. "Girl or boy?"

"Girl. Go in before your mother teaches the child disappointment."

That got a quick crooked grin out of him. Then he remembered the river, the launch, the school. "They're saying houses with no papers will get nothing."

Halima wiped her hands on her sari and felt the small catch under her ribs that came, lately, when worry and anger arrived together. "They're saying many things. Take me my fee when you have it."

"I've got two kilos of rice now," he said, ashamed before she even answered.

"Then bring two kilos of rice. I'm not marrying into your house."

She left before anyone could turn gratitude into a crowd around her. Her knees complained at the first stretch of open path. Mud sucked at her sandals. The char had that late-monsoon look of temporary survival: tin roofs dull with damp, bamboo fences leaning, courtyards lifted on fresh throwings of silt that would hold until they did not. Everywhere, people were pulling old packets from trunks and shelves because the loudspeaker had said papers, and once paper entered a village sentence it spread faster than fever.

She passed Rahima's western cousin running with a land-tax receipt held over his head under a steel plate. She passed two boys arguing whether voter card and identity card were the same thing. She passed Sufia's tea stall at the ghat already shuttered, though Sufia never shut early unless something worth hearing was happening elsewhere.

By the time Halima reached the schoolyard the notice was up.

It had been pasted crooked to the exterior wall of the primary school, the lower corners already lifting where the plaster held damp. Glue dribbled down like snot. Half the char seemed pressed toward it. Men stood in front because men always stepped between paper and the people whose lives it named, but women were pressing from the sides and behind, necks craned, chins up, children hoisted on hips. Somebody had tracked mud across the low concrete step. Somebody else was crying. A baby, not the one Halima had just delivered, was wailing under the whole human noise like a kettle forgotten on a stove.

Nura stood on the step with one hand braced against the wall to keep from being shoved off. She had tied her scarf back hard from her face and tucked the loose end into her waist so it would not catch. Even in that crush she had found a way to keep the paper flat with her fingertips, smoothing the lower edge as if a notice would behave better if handled properly. The old burn mark showed pale on the inside of her wrist. She was reading aloud in the school Bangla she used for forms and certificates, not because she liked the sound of her own literacy but because the boy who had tried before her had stumbled over every third word and turned the crowd mean.

"Read it properly," a fisherman in a faded football jersey yelled, the red stripes on it nearly gone to pink.

"Then learn to read it yourself," Nura shot back without looking at him.

Halima heard the line about compensation first because the crowd sucked in around it.

"Payment will be made," Nura read, her voice going flatter as the language thickened, "to verified households subject to matching across birth record, marriage record, land occupancy documentation, and national identity information. Mere current residence will not be treated as sufficient claim."

The words hung there, ugly and expensive.

Halima felt the dried blood in her cuticles wake again, as if the sentence had hands and had put them on her. Verified household. She could hear the tears in it before anyone else named them: widows folded into brothers' courtyards without paper, girls shifted between roofs so quietly no clerk had ever seen the move, boys eating in one house and sleeping in another because hunger had its own map. The launch had brought a labor after all. It only wanted names instead of a child.

A widow with turmeric ground into the cracks of her thumbs said, "What does that mean?"

Nura lowered the paper and answered in the char's own rough rhythm. "It means if your house only exists because we all know you live in it, that's not enough for them."

Women at the edges started first, and then the men at the back shouted over them.

"My husband is dead, whose name do they want now?"

Halima knew that voice before she found the face. Jahanara from the western edge, who had coughed through both pregnancies and buried the man who beat mud from his nets with a fever still burning in him. The question struck Halima low and mean. The state would call it inconsistency. On the char it was how people stayed fed after a man was lowered into wet ground.

"My father never had a card."

"The river took the tax slip."

Her palms slicked. She could hear the hidden arithmetic starting already: whose roof had paper, whose roof had witnesses, whose roof had only memory and the willingness of neighbors to swear. Worst of all was that quick turn in the crowd toward any man who might sell certainty. Fear always did that. It made even stupid authority smell like shelter for a minute.

"What about those who built after the last flood?"

"Ask the chairman."

Chairman Mofiz Uddin was already there, of course. He stood a little apart under a black umbrella somebody else was holding for him, though the rain had stopped hours ago. The edges of his beard were dyed too carefully for the char and always bled a rusty tint when sweat reached them. He had on a pressed panjabi the color of old cream and the patient expression of a man preparing to become everyone's bridge to power for a price. When he saw Halima he gave the sort of smile that showed public teeth and private calculation.

"Halima-dai has come," he called, as if announcing reinforcement he himself had arranged. "Make space. Let her hear."

The space they made was not generous. It was the kind made for a person everyone needed at once. Halima felt shoulders, elbows, breath, damp cloth. Her bag thumped against her hip. Blood from the birth had dried in the cuticles of her right hand.

The district men were easy to pick out. They had the mainland habit of carrying paper away from their bodies as if sweat belonged to a lower class of problem. One of them, younger than Halima's youngest dead, held a stack of folders under his arm and kept shifting his grip so the cardboard would not touch his shirt. Trimmed moustache, pressed pale-blue shirt already darkening at the spine, sandals too clean for the schoolyard. He had the weary caution of a man who had learned that one careless sentence could live in a complaint file for years.

He stepped forward when he heard her name. "You are Halima Khatun?"

Nobody on the char used both names unless there was paper involved. Halima looked at him and said, "Who wants to know?"

"Aminul Haque. Upazila records office." He gave a small nod, polite, measured. "We've been told you assist with births here."

"People have been saying that for thirty years. It keeps turning out true."

A few in the crowd laughed because she had given them something harder than panic to hold for a second. Aminul did not laugh, but the corner of his mouth admitted the line had landed.

"Then I need to speak with you." He glanced at the notice, at the crowd, at the chairman, already calculating what could be said in the open without losing control of the day. "Not privately. Here is fine. The initial household lists we received don't reconcile."

"With what?"

"With each other," he said. "Union rolls, voter information, old relief lists, the names attached to homestead plots. One person appears in three houses. Another appears in none. Dead men still anchor active households. Women shift surname spellings from one paper to the next. Children are present without traceable parent links. If we process compensation from this as it stands, half the char will challenge the other half and the district will freeze the file."

He spoke plainly for an official, but the plainness did not make it kinder. Around them, people had gone quiet enough to hear the shape of their own danger.

Halima said, "People move. Land moves. Husbands move. Why are you surprised the paper limps behind?"

"I'm not surprised." His tone stayed even. "But I still need a list I can defend."

That was the first moment she disliked him properly. He was not cruel; men like Mofiz were cruel when it paid. Aminul was worse in a narrower way. He could look at a broken thing with sympathy and still demand it line up in columns before he would touch it.

Mofiz spread one hand toward the crowd. "Our people are simple, Officer-saheb. They do not understand these technicalities. If you explain what documents are required, we will help gather them."

We, Halima thought. He was already stepping onto ground he had not been invited to own.

But Aminul's attention had stayed on her. He had noticed what the others had noticed: that when Nura's voice faltered over a clause she glanced not to Mofiz but to Halima; that women in the back had started pushing forward not to ask the chairman but to catch Halima's eye; that the old schoolmaster, useless on any question after 1989, had just muttered, "Ask Halima-dai, she knows whose child is whose."

The officer shifted the folders under his arm and lowered his voice only slightly. "I will explain the requirement now. Verification sessions begin tomorrow morning in the schoolroom. Each household must establish continuity through whatever records exist. Birth. Marriage. Land occupancy. National identity where applicable."

"Where applicable," Nura repeated from the step, dry enough to make several women snort.

Aminul let that pass. "Where a record trail breaks, I need local attestation from a responsible person who actually knows the household composition over time."

He could have said witness. He could have said elder. He could have said village assistance. Instead he said responsible person and looked directly at Halima.

A shoulder drove into Halima's back, and somebody else's elbow banged the wall hard enough to rattle the notice.

Halima knew, before he made the next sentence, exactly what it would be.

"Chairman sahib tells me you remember most births on the char," Aminul said. "The schoolmaster says you know which women came as brides and which arrived as widows. Others say that when the government nurse did not come, you kept your own dates. If that's even half true, I need you at the table."

There it was.

She could have denied it. She could have laughed and said old people were always credited with memory because it saved everyone else the trouble of keeping any. She could have cursed Mofiz for talking. She did none of those things, because the crowd was full of faces she had seen slick with first blood, first milk, fever, miscarriage, shame. Faces that would be erased cleanly enough if she chose pride now.

The more dangerous truth moved beneath that one. He did not only need her memory. He needed a form of memory that could survive being spoken aloud in front of clerks. He needed fathers repeated that had never lain with the mothers who bore those children. He needed marriage dates nudged forward over already-swollen bellies. He needed dead sisters turned into legal mothers and living grandmothers turned into aunts. He needed the small evasions by which girls had stayed in school, boys had stayed out of bonded labor, widows had stayed attached to a cooking fire instead of dropping off the edge of a household entirely.

He needed the island's private mercy translated into evidence.

Nura had stopped reading. She was watching Halima now with the sharp, fixed stillness that meant her mind was already running ahead and listing consequences. When frightened, the girl became more practical, not less. Halima could see it happening in the set of her mouth.

From the crowd a woman with one side of her scarf dark from nursing cried, "Dai-ma, you'll sit, won't you? They won't leave us to that chairman's mercy."

Another voice: "If she doesn't sit, we're dead."

Mofiz turned his palms outward, all modesty. "No one is speaking of mercy or death. We are only trying to ensure orderly administration."

Halima looked at him. "Then stand aside and let the paper breathe."

That drew a few harsher laughs. Mofiz's smile held, though it tightened at one corner.

Aminul waited. He did not rescue her from the decision by softening it. Halima respected that more than she liked it.

"If I sit," she said, "people will hear things they don't enjoy hearing."

"If you don't sit," Aminul said, "people may lose claims they won't get back."

The answer was simple and defensive, and it was ugly because it was true.

Sandals scraped the step. Paper crackled. Bodies leaned in close enough for Halima to smell wet cloth, old fish, betel. The crowd had already named one bad choice duty. The launch passed again on the river, loudspeaker now carrying toward the eastern side. Somewhere beyond the yard a goat screamed because it had tangled itself in rope. Somewhere closer a child asked whether verified meant they were being counted like cattle. Nobody answered him.

Halima wiped her palms again on the end of her sari though they were already dry. "Tomorrow, then."

A rough exhale moved through the crowd. It was not relief exactly. It sounded more like a hundred people loosening their grip on the same branch at once.

Aminul nodded once. "At eight. Before the room fills."

"It will fill before that."

"Then come before the full room fills."

That, at least, nearly made her smile.

He pulled one form from his folder, glanced at the name on it, then back to her. "One more thing. If you have notebooks, lists, older date books, anything contemporary to the births or marriages in question, bring them."

Nura's head turned toward Halima so fast the movement was almost violent.

Halima kept her face empty. "People on this char barely keep dry rice. You think they keep archives?"

"I'm asking what exists," Aminul said. "Not what should."

It was a better answer than most officials would have found. It still made cold sweat gather under her blouse.

Mofiz clapped his hands for order, already reclaiming the yard. "You've heard the officer. Households will prepare papers. Tomorrow the proper line will be maintained. No pushing. No rumor. We will proceed with discipline."

No rumor, Halima thought, while the man's whole trade was rumor with a receipt attached.

Nura stepped down from the wall and pressed through the crowd to Halima's side. Up close she smelled of sun-warmed paper and the cheap soap she saved for days when she had to read for other people. "You shouldn't have said yes in front of them."

"You think I didn't know that?"

"Then why did you?"

Halima looked past her toward the notice. Verified household. Mere current residence not sufficient. The paste at the bottom had already caught dust. "Because they're not asking whether I like the knife."

Nura said nothing to that. Her gaze had gone back to Aminul, who was fielding questions with careful, maddening restraint, promising process and never outcome. Halima thought he looked young, too young to understand what it meant here when a father was written, when a wife was attached, when a dead man's name stayed alive because it fed the living.

Then again, he might have been old enough and only pretending not to know.

The crowd thickened again around the wall. Names were already being shouted, compared, defended. One man insisted his wife had two spellings because the schoolmaster drank. Another demanded to know whether a second marriage counted if the first husband had vanished but not died. Women started leaving faster than men, which meant the real work had begun. They would be digging in trunks by now, opening tins, lifting bedding, calling older sisters, cursing damp.

Halima turned toward home.

Nura walked beside her, faster than Halima wanted and slower than the girl wanted, the compromise of kinship. Mud pulled at the hems of their clothes. Behind them the loudspeaker moved farther off, announcing the same order to the eastern side where the ground held a little higher and men pretended land there would save them from the river's memory.

"Do we have everything?" Nura asked after a while.

"Everything for what?"

"Don't do that."

She did not say granddaughter, did not say guardian, did not say our household. She did not have to. The question had already put the house between them.

By the time they reached Halima's yard the afternoon had turned the tin roof white with heat again. Their house sat low and ordinary near the middle of the char, packed earth swept clean, one bamboo shelf high under the rafters for the things that should stay away from rats and children. Halima had always liked that the house did not advertise itself. Survival here often depended on letting other people think your best possessions were somewhere else.

Nura went straight to the trunk by the bed and began pulling out papers before she had even washed her feet. This was how fear used her: not crying, not praying, but sorting. She stacked things by size, then by purpose, her careful hands flattening each sheet from the middle outward. Old tutoring notebooks. A vaccination card. Saleha's death certificate folded so many times the creases had gone white. Three receipts for tin sheets. One electricity application that had never become electricity.

"Slow down," Halima said.

"Why?"

"Because frantic hands tear paper."

"And slow hands lose money?"

There was the sarcasm first, exactly as Halima had expected. Hurt always reached Nura wearing another face. The girl sat back on her heels and blew hair off her forehead with an impatient puff. "Tell me what they want. Properly."

Halima set down her bag, washed at the pitcher, and watched the water go pink, then clear, then pink again from blood hidden under the nails. She bought herself those few moments before answering.

"They want households they can trace," she said. "Who was born to whom. Who married into which roof. Who holds which strip of land. Whose names match."

"Match where?"

"Everywhere they can make them match."

Nura gave a humorless little laugh. "Then half the people out there are finished."

Halima did not answer, which was answer enough.

Nura held up Saleha's death certificate by one corner. "Will this matter?"

Halima's hand closed so hard on the wet end of her sari that her knuckles hurt. Somebody was pounding rice two yards over, and each strike seemed to land against the sore place at the base of her spine. A child coughed. The hen under the window scratched once, then burst its wings against the bamboo hard enough to shake dust loose. Saleha's name in Nura's hand put the taste of an old sickroom at the back of Halima's throat: fever, metal, stale milk.

"Put that back," Halima said.

"Why? It's a paper."

"It's not the paper for today."

"Everything is the paper for today." Nura laid it down, more carefully than before but not obediently. "They're saying compensation will go by verified household. Whose household are we, then? Yours? My mother's? The dead don't stand in line."

Halima sat on the edge of the bed because the pain had started in her lower back, the one that came when the char wanted too much from her in a single day. "Don't borrow trouble before it reaches the door."

"It came on a launch with a speaker."

That was fair. Halima hated when the girl was fair.

Nura kept sorting, talking as she worked because talk was easier for her with her eyes down. "If they ask for marriage paper, we don't have one for my mother. If they ask for land, we have the tin receipts and that old tax slip in your box, but the name is half gone. If they ask for father's line..."

She stopped there, not because she had forgotten the rest, but because now they had reached the place both of them knew and neither wanted to put a foot on.

Halima said, "Enough."

Nura looked up. Her face was leaner when she was angry, cheekbones sharpening under the skin. "Enough for whom?"

The question sat between them with too many possible answers. Halima did what she did under pressure when she could not yet bear the true line: she moved to task.

"Get the papers off the floor," she said. "The damp comes up faster near evening. And check the trunk lining. There may be an old receipt stuck in the seam."

Nura stared at her for a second longer, seeing exactly what she was doing. Then she clicked her tongue once in disgust and obeyed anyway. She always had practical blood in her, even when she wanted to set the house on fire with questions.

As the light thinned, they worked without much speech. Nura tied the papers into three packets with thread saved from rice sacks. Halima hung the bloodied cloths from the birth over the back fence to rinse in the coming rain. The char's sounds shifted from public alarm to domestic preparation. Lids clanged. Voices called across courtyards, counting documents now the way people counted children before a boat crossing. Somewhere Mofiz's name was spoken too often. Somewhere Aminul's careful voice was already being misquoted into promises he had not made.

After dusk they ate rice with green chili and salt because there had been no time for more. Nura asked nothing while they ate. That worried Halima more than questions would have.

When the lamp was turned low and the char outside settled into the layered noise of insects, distant argument, and river water worrying the bank, Halima waited. Nura lay on her mat with one arm over her eyes. Whether she slept or only wanted Halima to think she slept, Halima could not tell.

The loft shelf ran along the back wall above head height, bamboo slats bowed by years of use. Birth cloths dried there in bundles. Old brass bowls. A broken hurricane lamp. Two sacks of lentils. An enamel basin with a chip sharp enough to cut a finger. Behind those, wrapped inside a piece of faded oilcloth darkened by years of rubbing with mustard oil against damp, was the ledger.

Halima had hidden it badly if the measure was cleverness and well if the measure was habit. No thief ever believed the old midwife's shelf held anything except old midwife's things. The book had survived rats, flood splash, one roof leak, and her own several attempts not to touch it for months at a time.

She stood on the low stool and reached up. Her back pulled. The oilcloth was heavier than its size deserved, as if thirty years of names had weight beyond paper.

From the mat Nura said into the dim room, not moving her arm from her face, "What are you taking down?"

Halima could have lied. She had built whole shelters out of smaller lies. Tonight she did not trust her voice to do it cleanly.

"Something older than your questions," she said.

Nura let her arm fall and looked straight at her. The lamp made her eyes seem darker than they were. "Then it's probably the answer to them."

Halima carried the bundle to the bed and sat. The knots were stiff. She had tied them years ago after the last time a union man came asking for birth dates and left with money from three households tucked into his pocket. She remembered the feel of each knot. She had tied cords on newborn stumps with less care.

The ledger had not begun as a grand thing. At first it had been names in a school exercise book because the government register boat came late and children came when they pleased. Then one father vanished and another man's name had to be written fast before a debt holder claimed the boy. Then a girl's marriage month had to be moved back because her belly had moved faster than the ceremony. Then a widow's sister's child had to become her own on paper because the sister had died and the husband's people would have sold the goats, the cot, and perhaps the child with them. One correction called for another. One mercy made a future need. After that she kept dates, blood lines, whispered truths, the names that could be said in courtyards and the names that could not. She had not done it for power. Memory rotted if it stayed only in the mouth.

Sometimes ink could do what men would not.

She untied the first knot.

The room smelled suddenly of mustard oil, mildew, and paper that had lived too long too close to weather. The cover underneath was once red, now the brown-red of dried betel spit. Her own handwriting on the first page had changed over the years from awkwardly careful to brisk enough to be mistaken for certainty. The oldest entries were the worst to look at. Their age was not the reason. They still carried the innocence of a woman who had not yet understood that a private correction could harden into a whole public life.

Nura had sat up now, her blanket fallen to her lap. She did not come closer. That restraint, more than any plea, told Halima the night had turned.

"Nani," she said quietly, using the softer name she saved for when anger and fear had to share a mouth, "what is that?"

Halima laid her broad palm over the cover as if it might still be possible to calm the thing by touch.

"Trouble," she said.

By morning it would have to ride at the bottom of her bag, wrapped again and quiet.

Then she loosened the final knot and opened the oilcloth all the way, because tomorrow would ask it to speak.


# Chapter 2

The schoolhouse had two rooms, one blackboard, and no corner where a name could be spoken privately. By eight in the morning the concrete floor was already giving back the night's stored heat, and the tin roof had begun its noon work early, ticking and expanding above the crowd before the sun had properly climbed. Aminul Haque had pushed two student desks together for a table. He sat on one side with his folders lifted off the wood by a folded handkerchief so the damp would not crawl into them. Halima sat beside him on a bench made for children, her knees too high, her cloth bag between her ankles. At the bag's bottom, wrapped again and quiet under a towel and a packet of betel leaf, lay the oilcloth bundle she had sworn to herself she would not open unless the day turned vicious.

It turned vicious before the first name was called.

People had packed the doorway and the windows before Aminul could finish arranging his forms. Mud dried in fan-shaped splashes up the wall where sandals had slapped it. A baby wailed somewhere outside. Men leaned on the sill bars and breathed their fish breath and tea breath into the room. Women stood shoulder to shoulder in the yard, sari hems dark with wet earth, clutching tin boxes, plastic file covers, school certificates, yellow tax slips, laminated identity cards with the corners already beginning to peel. Nura kept to the wall with a slate pencil tucked behind one ear and a sheaf of blank sheets in her hand, her burned wrist flashing pale each time she passed a paper to Aminul. She looked less like Halima's ward than like a girl who had arrived early for an exam and discovered the examiners were idiots.

Chairman Mofiz Uddin stationed himself in the doorway and claimed the school by posture alone, more completely than the government or the children who used it ever had. His white panjabi was too well ironed for that room. The dyed edges of his beard had gone the color of rust where the sweat touched them, and he kept pressing two fingers to the clove tucked in one cheek, managing even his own breath. He had brought a red plastic chair for himself and did not sit on it. He stood with one hand on the back and arranged the line.

"Let's keep dignity," he said, smiling at no one and everyone. "Women alone first. Widows. Deserted wives. Let them not stand in the sun all day. Then the older men. Then the rest. Officer-saheb has come from far away. We should show him we know how to do things in order."

Halima turned her good eye toward him. "Order was the line they made outside."

Mofiz spread both hands, all courtesy. "Khalamma, the line outside is one thing. Fairness is another."

It was the kind of sentence he liked: soft at the front, thumb in the throat at the back. Halima heard what sat underneath it. Start with the weak. Start with the ones whose men were dead or gone and whose papers would have holes. Let the room learn early what failure looked like. After that every small contradiction would taste like blood.

Aminul cleared his throat. He was a careful man even in this heat, his moustache trimmed, his shirt still tucked despite the journey from the mainland and the half-charred generator cable lying useless in the corner. He had a charged laptop open, a power bank fat as a brick beside it, and his phone propped in the window where one bar of signal came and went with the breeze. "We will proceed one by one," he said. "Please stay quiet enough that I can hear the claimant."

"Hear and be heard," Mofiz said pleasantly to the yard. "Otherwise people will say later that things were done in secret."

No one moved back. A man at the window hitched both elbows deeper over the sill, and the women in the yard rose on their toes behind him. The schoolroom stiffened into theater.

The first woman he called was Aklima Bewa from the western-edge huts. Halima knew her before the name was halfway out of Mofiz's mouth. Aklima had a left shoulder that always sat higher than the right from carrying water too young, and she stored everything important in a navy school satchel her dead youngest son had once used. Even today, with the whole island pressing behind her, she paused at the table to pull a few dried neem leaves out of the satchel flap. She kept them there against insects. Her hands shook so badly the leaves stuck to her fingers.

"Sit, khala," Aminul said.

"If I sit, I won't get up again," she said, and remained standing.

She laid out her life in damp pieces: her national identity card; her dead husband's card in a plastic sleeve split along one side; a union death certificate with one corner gone soft; a photocopy of a land-tax receipt so pale the ink had faded to a breath-thin ghost on the page. She kept smoothing each sheet flat with the side of her thumb, then looking up to see whether they had become more real.

Aminul began where officials always began, with the document in the best condition. "Your husband's name?"

"Kader Ali."

"Your father?"

"What use is my father now?" she asked.

Mofiz gave a short laugh for the room, turning her answer into something neighborly. Halima could have struck him with the heavy attendance register on the table.

Aminul did not smile. "For the record, khala."

Aklima named her father. Aminul typed. The phone signal caught and dropped. He waited for the screen to load. Outside, someone began shushing a child. Halima looked at the old widow's hands and remembered those hands twenty years earlier slick with mustard oil, bracing against the bamboo post while Halima lifted her second daughter out under a roof that leaked on the bed all night. Kader Ali had held the hurricane lamp then, his own fingers black with jute tar, and barked at everyone until the baby cried. A man who could hold a lamp through twelve hours of labor ought to have left something stronger behind than a half-rotten photocopy.

Aminul checked the death certificate against the identity card. "Marriage registration?"

Aklima blinked at him. "What?"

"Nikah record. Kabinnama. Union copy."

"Kader is dead."

"I understand. But to connect you cleanly to his household, I need the marriage trace."

She looked from Aminul to Halima and back, waiting for one of them to admit the ugly joke. "My clean connection is I went to his house bleeding at thirteen and stayed there thirty-two years. I buried two sons from that yard. Ask any person standing outside."

"Witness statements may help later," Aminul said. He had already reached the point in his voice where sympathy turned technical, where he tucked himself into the grammar of procedure because feeling would only make promises he could not keep. "But on this first pass I need the documentary link."

"There was no kazi here that week," Halima said. "The river had cut the southern bank. The men crossed two months later and did several marriages together."

Mofiz clicked his tongue softly. "If it was done later, perhaps the copy exists somewhere."

"Then go find it yourself," Halima said.

Somewhere near the doorway a cough snagged and turned into a laugh. Mofiz let it pass. He only tilted his head, the picture of injured civility.

Aminul kept his eyes on the papers. "Do you have any registered children whose records carry both your name and Kader Ali's name?"

Aklima produced two birth slips so damp they tried to curl back into themselves. One child had died. The other was married off upriver and not present. One slip had Kader's name. The other, from a different year and different handwriting, named only Aklima and left the father line blank. Halima felt the crowd lean in at the windows, foreheads nearly touching the rusted bars. For a second the blank father line seemed to sit on the desk like an object with edges.

"This one counts," Mofiz murmured, tapping the first slip with a clean nail. "That one causes questions."

"Don't touch my paper," Aklima said, and snatched it back with more speed than Halima would have thought remained in her.

Aminul's face tightened. "Khala, for now I'm marking the household pending due to missing marriage verification and inconsistent child records."

"Pending means what?"

"It means not rejected. Not cleared."

"And that means what?"

He took a breath. "It means you must return with any marriage witness, mosque record, older ration card, or child's full record that links you again to Kader Ali's household."

Aklima stared at the laptop. The insult had a screen now. Her mouth worked once before words came. "My husband had to die clean, then. He died too early. If he'd known he needed to leave more paper, maybe he would have stayed alive for you."

The widow's words dropped over the schoolhouse and put out every cheap laugh. Even Mofiz let them lie. Halima wanted to put a hand on the widow's forearm, to press something living against the administrative blank opening under her feet, but the table had already taught everyone there what touch could not do. Aminul wrote a case number on the top sheet and handed it over. Aklima did not take it at first. When she did, her fingers pinched the paper so hard they shook.

She left without looking at Mofiz. The crowd opened and closed around her. Outside, somebody asked, "What happened?" and somebody else answered too loudly, "No kabinnama."

The morning began not with a rejection, which people might have fought, but with a suspension. A family could dangle by one missing line.

After that the queue changed its own posture. Women opened and shut their document packets again. Men in the yard sent boys running home for trunks that would hold nothing useful. Mofiz kept smiling and kept choosing the order. He did not bring up his fertilizer cousin with the thick file. He did not bring the eastern-side men whose walls were high and whose papers had been dried each year in proper tin boxes. He called Selina, whose husband had left for Bogura three years earlier and continued sending instructions through other people's phones but no traceable address. He called old Nur Islam, who had three tax receipts and no identity card because the photo truck had come on a day he was out netting. He called a girl barely sixteen carrying her mother's marriage papers and her own baby on one hip. Each case taught a new way a life could become unofficial in front of witnesses.

Halima tried to keep people moving by hand where speech would not do it. "Put that under the dry one." "Read the year again." "No, that is your uncle's card, not your husband's." "Stop crowding. If you breathe on the paper any harder it will melt." Publicly she was brisk because softness would have broken the room open. Privately she was furious enough to taste it. She had known most of these people first by the wet weight of them, by which shoulder had bruised in labor, by which husband fainted at blood, by which grandmother slapped a newborn's soles before Halima had to. Now each life had to pass through father fields, husband fields, household head, occupancy trace. Whole years of feeding one another and sleeping wall to wall counted only if someone long ago had carried a stamp across water.

Near noon the heat thickened until it seemed the roof had sagged lower. Sweat slid down Aminul's temple and dropped onto his collar, defeating all his care. Nura brought a steel glass of water and set it beside Halima without being asked. She had stopped writing on her loose sheets. She was listening now, following the logic of the wound. Each time a form stalled she glanced toward Halima, measuring how close the trouble had moved to home.

Mofiz looked out into the yard, squinted in a show of fairness while he worked a knife between ribs, and raised his voice. "Now let us take a straightforward file. Kashem Sheikh. Bring your papers, bhai. Show everyone how a man comes prepared."

Kashem had not been next. Halima knew because she had seen him arrive barely ten minutes earlier, shoulders still glittering with the fine scales that clung after morning fish sorting. He came in grinning anyway, not about to refuse a public summons or let anyone think he feared paper. He was broad through the chest, darkened to teak by river sun, his palms ridged white where rope had burned them over the years. He kept hooking one thumb into his lungi knot and tapping his chipped front tooth with the nail of his other hand, a little tic he had when he needed to look looser than he felt.

"Straightforward costs extra now?" he said, and the room rewarded him with a few grateful laughs.

"Sit and be glorious," Mofiz told him.

"I can be glorious standing."

Kashem dropped a laminated identity card on the table with a flourish, then a folded land slip wrapped in polythene, then a ration booklet swollen with damp. "See? I didn't come naked."

Halima had delivered him before dawn in a hut where the fire would not catch and Rahima kept bleeding through the torn quilts under her hips. He had come feet first, left heel blue from the jam of bone, the cord looped under one armpit so tightly Halima had to hook two fingers under it before his lungs opened and he let out his first furious bawl. Rahima had reached for him with both hands while blood ran down her thigh to the mat. Halima remembered the third night too: Abed Sheikh's widowed sister arriving with a clean gamchha, lifting the boy off Rahima's breast, and making Rahima stay behind the hanging quilt while the child was carried into the front room and given the safer household line before the men tied to her debts could count what work his body might one day owe.

Aminul took the card. "Kashem Sheikh. Father: Abed Sheikh."

"That's what it says when the print is clear too," Kashem said.

Nura suppressed a smile without looking up. Halima almost admired him for not shrinking yet.

Aminul entered the number. The phone signal hung on. He waited, clicked, frowned, clicked again. Then he unfolded the land slip. The paper had once been yellow and was now the color of old teeth. Halima saw the moment the mismatch reached him. His whole face did not change; only the muscles beside his mouth drew in.

"This holding is under late Jalal Mondol's household chain," he said. "How are you claiming it?"

Kashem's grin stayed in place half a second too long. "By living there."

"That's not enough on the first pass. Your card places you under Abed Sheikh. This land slip runs through a different line."

"Abed lived there."

"Not according to this paper."

Kashem leaned across the table until the fish smell on him reached Halima. "Then the paper is stupid."

"Perhaps," Aminul said. "But stupid paper is still what I have to submit."

There was another laugh from the yard, meaner now because it had found a man to bite. Kashem heard it. His shoulders lifted a fraction.

Mofiz stepped in exactly where a snake would have put its head. "No need to excite yourself, bhai. Khalamma will clear it. She knows whose child came from which mat. That's why she's sitting there."

He made it sound like honor. He meant exposure.

Halima said, "If you want this done, let the officer ask and let the man answer."

Kashem slapped his ration booklet open with the flat of his hand. "Ask, then. My father was Abed Sheikh. Dead six years. My hut is mine. My nets are mine. My wife's cooking pot is in that yard. What else?"

Aminul checked the screen again. "The database trail on Abed Sheikh links to a mainland ward code, not this char. There's also no inheritance transfer here from Abed to you. On the paper holding, Jalal Mondol's widow and sons appear. Your name doesn't."

Kashem blinked. The sentence had reached him in a language he should have known and did not. "Mainland what?"

"Ward code."

"I don't live in a code. I live by the canal bend."

Mofiz made a sympathetic noise. Several men in the back echoed it, not because they pitied Kashem but because they enjoyed the shape of his confusion. Halima felt the whole room turning toward her before Aminul did. It was happening already: the state had said no, so the island looked to the woman who had once made yes possible.

Aminul turned then. He kept his voice polite, but the politeness had thinned. "Khalamma, do you know the original household entry here? Was he entered under Abed Sheikh from birth, or by later correction?"

The question was careful enough for an office. In that room it landed like a slap.

For one beat Halima heard nothing except the roof ticking and the ferry horn drifting from the river. Her fingers tightened around the edge of the desk. In her bag the oilcloth bundle seemed to push up of its own will. The names inside had been waiting thirty years for air like this. She saw herself on that third night, copying Abed Sheikh into the father line while Rahima sat behind the hanging quilt with milk soaking the front of her blouse and no standing to speak over the household taking the child. If Halima said any of it now, Kashem would learn in front of the whole yard that the name on his card was borrowed, Rahima would be dragged from the doorway into open claim, and Mofiz would turn the room into a fight over whose compensation share had just grown or vanished.

The pause was not long. It was long enough.

Somebody at the window said, very softly and therefore perfectly audibly, "She knows."

Mofiz's eyebrows lifted with theatrical surprise. "If there's some older register, we should all benefit from its wisdom."

Nura's head came up so fast the pencil fell from behind her ear. Aminul glanced toward the doorway, finally understanding the scale of what he had asked in public, but he did not have the authority to pull the words back.

Kashem looked from Aminul to Halima, and for the first time the swagger drained enough for something younger to show through it. Not suspicion yet. Only the first sting of being made foolish in front of other men. "Khalamma?"

Halima answered the way she answered when a birth room was close to panic: not with the truth people wanted, but with the next task. "The old tax bundle from your house. Bring the earlier receipts. And the ration entry from before the card lamination if you still have it."

"That's all?" Kashem said.

"Bring them."

"I brought what didn't rot."

"Then bring what's left that still has ink."

He laughed once, sharp and joyless. "You want my father's bones too? I'll dig those if it helps."

Two men by the doorway barked laughter and then swallowed it. Mofiz did not laugh this time. He put on concern like a shawl. "No one is insulting you, Kashem bhai. We are only trying to protect legitimate households. If Khalamma recalls something, let her say it clearly. Better one moment's shame than a big mistake later."

The knife showed cleanly now: shame dressed up as civic duty.

Halima turned on him. "You talk like mistakes arrive from the river by boat and not from men's mouths."

Mofiz's smile thinned but did not break. "Then let us trust the mouth that remembers."

Kashem slapped his identity card back into his palm. "Do I stand verified or not?"

Aminul did not hide behind terminology this time. The balance in his sentences had frayed. "Not yet."

The words seemed to hit Kashem physically. His hand closed over the card hard enough to bend the lamination. Behind him, through the bodies at the door, Halima caught one glimpse of Rahima Bewa's smoke-dark shawl and the outline of her small underfed frame. Rahima had stopped outside without entering. She watched from the edge, one hand holding the throat of her cloth bag closed. Then somebody shifted, and she was gone.

"Fine," Kashem said too loudly. "Write pending. Write sky-fallen. Write whatever pretty word you people use when you can't answer a straight question."

He shoved the land slip back into its polythene and stood. The bench scraped. "But next time, don't call me up like some circus goat if all you want is to stare."

He walked out before anyone dismissed him. Outside the yard broke into low speech at once, that urgent thick murmur that meant news was already running ahead of facts. Halima could hear her own name moving through it.

The next claimant came and another after that, but the room had changed species. No one believed in simple checking anymore. Each person who sat at the table sat inside the shadow of Halima's pause. Men looked at her before they answered Aminul. Women tried to read her face the way they once read labor pain. An old mother with a split heel asked, before even putting down her papers, "If your memory says one thing and the card says another, which one wins?" A young husband who smelled of kerosene and pond water pushed his niece behind him and asked whether dependents counted if they had eaten from his pot for seven years but had another father's name somewhere. Aminul kept saying, "One at a time. One matter at a time." The words no longer governed anything.

At a short midday break he rubbed his eyes and spoke to Halima without looking at her. "I should have asked that differently."

"You should have had walls that worked."

"This room is impossible."

"This island is impossible. The room is only honest about it."

Nura handed Halima a piece of gur wrapped in newspaper. Her jaw was set so tight the muscle jumped near her ear. "He's doing it on purpose," she said under her breath, meaning Mofiz.

Halima did not ask who. There was no one else in the room worth that sentence.

Across the doorway Mofiz accepted tea from a boy and thanked him in the voice of a man blessing a donor plaque. Then he said, quite audibly, "We are fortunate. Most places do not have a living archive to help them."

Living archive. Three people near the door repeated it under their breath. By the time it reached the yard it already sounded official.

When work resumed, Halima kept her hands flat on the desk so no one would see her reach toward the bag at her feet. She did not open the oilcloth bundle. She would not give Mofiz that sight. Even so, the next woman in line laid down three papers and kept a fourth folded in her fist. Only when Halima said, "Open your hand," did she slide out a birth slip with the father's line smeared nearly white. Behind her, a man from the eastern high ground bent low enough for Halima to smell cardamom on his breath and murmured, "You'll remember who held your roof tin down last flood, khalamma," in the oily tone people used over small debts. Nura snapped, "Stand back," and three men near the window let out the thin laugh people used when they were already frightened.

By late afternoon Aminul's power bank had dropped to one blinking light. The schoolroom smelled of old sweat, wet paper, and the iron tang that came off damp rusting window bars. Cases were now piling faster than they could be sorted. He marked several pending, two provisionally matched, one disputed by absent heirs. Each new label sounded temporary enough to soothe and final enough to terrify. Halima kept hearing their practical meanings under the language. Come back with more proof. Bring a man who will stand for you. Produce a dead thing that does not exist. If you cannot, prepare to be thinned out.

At last Aminul shut the laptop because the battery would not hold another claimant. Mofiz announced they would resume the next day with the grand generosity of a man granting a festival. People surged toward the desk instead of away from it. In the morning they had wanted the officials. By evening their hands were reaching past Aminul for Halima's sleeve.

Aklima caught her first, gripping her elbow with fingers hard as dried roots. "Khalamma, you tell them I went to Kader's yard a virgin and came out a widow. You tell them that much. One missing kazi can't erase my whole body."

"Bring your married daughter tomorrow," Halima said. "And the neighbor who stood at the cooking fire that week."

"My daughter is two ferries away."

"Then send for her tonight."

Aklima searched Halima's face, trying to tell advice from mercy. Then she nodded once and let go.

Before Halima could stand fully, Majed Ali stepped in from the side, tall and slightly stooped, grease still lodged under his nails from some engine repair he had not finished. He held his cap in both hands instead of wearing it. That was the sort of man he was: mild in public, but his worry always took practical shape. "Khalamma," he said quietly, careful not to be overheard and therefore immediately overheard by everyone nearest him, "when our household comes, speak to what is useful. Not to every old corner of it. Children eat from the front story now."

There was no threat in the words. That made them worse. He was asking her to remember selectively for the sake of a household built on one of her old acts. He would not have named it that way himself. Still, the weight of the request sat exactly there.

Halima looked at the grease half-moons under his nails. "Bring the papers you have."

"I'll bring them."

"And decide before you come what you can survive hearing in public."

His face shifted just once, a tiny flinch, before he put the cap back on and moved away.

Then came a girl Halima barely knew, wanting a younger brother counted though his card named another village. She kept the boy half hidden behind her hip until Aminul asked to see his face. Then an old man asking whether a fostered grandson could remain tied to his cooking fire instead of being thrown back toward drunk uncles; while he spoke he kept one thumb over the corner where the grandson's other surname showed. Then Selina, abandoned by the husband who still occasionally sent voice messages from somewhere inland, saying in a flat hard tone, "If your memory can keep my boy inside my file, don't let men laugh him out of it." As Aminul bent over another form, Selina eased a betel tin toward Halima and clicked it open with her nail. Two folded hundred-taka notes sat under the lime packet. Halima shut the tin with the heel of her hand and shoved it back. Selina's ears went red before she snatched it away.

Kashem waited until almost all of them had gone. He stood by the school wall with his shoulder against a flaking map of Bangladesh painted there years ago for lessons no one remembered. The bravado had returned, but now it had a crack in it. He rolled his bent laminated card between both hands, trying to flatten it by force.

"Tomorrow," he said when Halima came out, "don't stop in the middle like that."

"Then don't come up before your turn when the chairman whistles."

"He called. I came. That's not the point."

"It's one point."

He gave a breath that was almost a laugh. "You know what I mean."

Halima did. Trouble was, she knew too much of what everyone meant now.

Kashem pushed off the wall. Up close she could see the salt dried white in the hair on his forearms. "If the problem is paper, say paper. If it's some old mess, say that too. Don't leave me standing there with all those bastards looking at my face."

The rawness in it showed for one second, then he covered it with a sneer. "A man can take bad news. What he can't take is being handled like a child."

Halima might have lied to soothe him. Once, years earlier, she had been willing to move whole names around to buy children time. Today the room had shown her the price of each such mercy when it matured. She said only, "Bring the older receipts."

"You really do want the bones."

"Bring the paper."

He shook his head, half anger, half bewilderment, and walked off toward the canal path, calling something crude to a friend so that anyone listening would hear only the crude part.

The schoolyard emptied slowly. Mud held prints the way wet paper held ink: briefly, then not at all. Nura came beside Halima with the cloth bag. Her voice was low. "They're saying you have a book that's truer than the government."

"They say many stupid things before evening prayer."

"They're not saying it like a joke."

No. They were not.

From the road toward the ghat drifted the day's final proof. A boy ran past carrying tea glasses in a wire rack and shouted to another, "Forget the laptop. Ask Halima-dai. She's the real office." By the time his sandals slapped out of hearing, two girls at the tube well had already taken up the phrase and were whispering it into their hands.

Halima stood in the school doorway and looked back once at the improvised table. Chalk dust had settled on the wood beside Aminul's neat stacks of forms. On the blackboard someone had written columns in a schoolboy hand: `Name`, `Father/Husband`, `Household`, `Remarks`. Beneath them, in smaller crooked chalk, someone had added: `Ask Halima`. Nura rubbed at it with her palm until the words blurred.

When she finally started home, the bag at her feet had become heavier than it had been in the morning though she had not taken the bundle out. At the first bend a woman from the north lane slipped out from behind a jute screen with a ration booklet tucked under her blouse and asked whether Halima could come after dark, when her brothers were asleep. Halfway down the path a man from Mofiz's tea circle called from his verandah, smiling too much, "Remember carefully, khalamma. Too much memory can drown a house." Near the western lane, Rahima stepped toward her with a folded paper in hand, saw Nura beside her, and tucked the paper back under her smoke-dark shawl before turning away.

By the time Halima reached her own threshold, three children were squatting across from the yard pretending to play knucklebones and watching to see whether her lamp would stay lit after supper. Inside, even with the door barred, the schoolhouse seemed to have followed her home.


# Chapter 3

At first light Halima stood in the doorway with the dented aluminum bowl tipped in both hands and let the last of the rice water slide into the yard. It darkened the packed earth by the threshold before the mud took it in. Near the fence, three knucklebones still lay where the neighbor children had squatted the evening before, pretending to play and watching for her lamp. A goat complained from somebody's yard. A child shouted for a missing sandal. From the western side came the thin metallic striking of someone beating mud off a cooking pot. Morning had resumed its house noises, but the schoolhouse heat from yesterday still sat in Halima's bones as if she had carried its benches home on her back.

Behind her, Nura sat cross-legged with the torn mosquito net stretched over her lap. She had found a spool of blue thread from somewhere and was darning the rip in small mean stitches, the way she did anything that had to hold. Her narrow face stayed bent to the mesh. Every so often she licked the thread, pushed it through, and pulled hard enough to pucker the cloth. The little burn mark on her wrist flashed when the light caught it.

"You're making a knot there," Halima said without turning.

"I'm making it stay shut."

"A mosquito will still find that ridge and laugh at us."

"Then let it laugh. It won't bite through thread."

Nura would argue with an insect if the insect sounded certain of itself. Halima set the rice pot on the stove ring and crouched to feed the flame with a twist of dry jute and a chip of bamboo. Smoke licked her eyes. The clouded left one watered first. Inside, the room had already gone wet and close. The island was giving them a few ordinary minutes before it remembered what the schoolroom had done to it.

Nura held the net up, checked her seam against the light, and said, "Sufia apa said Nazma's baby came out with a full head of hair. Thick as jute fiber."

Halima snorted. "Babies come out looking like old men and frogs. Hair doesn't improve them."

"You say that after you pull them out and hand them over."

"I say it because I've seen enough of them to know. Give the mother three days and she'll call the frog moon-faced and beautiful."

The line pulled a quick breath of laughter from Nura. The sound was small but enough to make the room feel inhabited instead of merely occupied. Halima took that as a good sign and reached for the onion. She peeled it with her thumbnail, split it with the small blackened knife, and scraped the pieces into mustard oil with two green chilies and last night's dried fish mashed fine. The smell rose sharp and honest. It was the kind of breakfast that made a house feel poorer and steadier at the same time.

Nura said, "Nazma's mother-in-law already wants to name the boy after her dead brother."

"Then the child is unlucky from the start."

"Nazma wants a film-name."

"Worse."

This time Nura laughed properly. She bent her head as though to hide it, but Halima heard the laugh go loose in her chest and took another small comfort from it. The girl had come back from the school yesterday with her mouth flat and watchful. She had eaten in silence, stacked the plates, and lain down without asking the questions that had been waiting in the rafters since Aminul had started calling fathers aloud in front of the whole char. Halima had been grateful for that silence in the way one is grateful for a storm that has not yet reached the house. She had no right to ask more from the morning than rice, patched netting, talk of somebody else's baby, and the illusion that ordinary work could still outrun what had already started.

She set two tin plates on the floor mat and ladled the rice when it swelled soft enough. Nura folded the mosquito net aside, washed her fingers from the pitcher, and came to eat. She always sat with one knee up when she was thinking about something, ready to get up before the meal had properly ended. Halima saw the posture and knew at once that the quiet had an edge under it.

Still, they ate first.

Nura mixed rice with the fish and onion, blew on the steam, and said, "Lutfa khala's son came by at dawn. The school wall has another paper under the first one."

Halima kept her eyes on her plate. "Every day they'll paste another paper until the wall falls down."

"This one lists what people should bring next."

"Then people will bring half of it and swear the other half washed away."

"Most of it did wash away."

"And the half that didn't, rats have eaten."

Nura took another bite. "You sound cheerful."

"I sound awake."

They finished without hurry. Halima carried the plates to the water jar, rinsed them, and set them upside down on the shelf. Nura shook out the mosquito net and spread it over the rope line that crossed the room. One corner still held the smell of their sleep, salt and damp cloth. Halima reached for the needle to mend a loose hem in her old blouse, and that was when Nura went to the tin trunk under the bed.

Nura made no performance of it, and the carefulness tightened Halima's neck more than a complaint would have. If the girl had burst into it, there would have been something to push back against. Instead Nura opened the trunk, moved aside the folded kameez, lifted the biscuit tin where she kept her good things, and brought out a packet wrapped in clear plastic cut from an oil bottle. The certificates inside had curled a little from humidity but were still clean. Nura laid them on the mat with more care than Halima had ever seen her give a piece of jewelry.

"Eat some more if you're still hungry," Halima said.

"I'm not."

"Then hang the net higher. It'll catch the stove soot where it's hanging."

"Later."

Halima knew that word the instant it came back to her. Nura had used Halima's own delaying voice. She threaded the needle anyway.

Nura sat down beside the packet and flattened the top sheet with her palm: secondary school certificate, testimonial, birth registration copy. Halima recognized them by color before she read a line. Papers had their own skins.

"Shirin madam sent word from the mainland," Nura said. "At Nabinpara they'll open the clinic with trainee posts once the first relocation blocks are settled. Not full nurses yet. Assistant work first. Dressings, vaccine days, keeping the ward book, helping with births if the family welfare people come short. If we go there and I get in early, they can forward my name for the district intake later."

She said it fast, as if the plan had been said over in her head so many times that the words had worn a groove.

"You never told me that," Halima said.

"I tried. You kept saying we'd talk after the school."

Halima put the blouse down. "How many posts?"

"Three at the clinic to start. Maybe four if the NGO women keep their promise." Nura's mouth bent at that, not trusting promises even while she reached for one. "Shirin madam says the first names will go from the relocation list. Girls who can read, write, keep records, and stay on night duty without fainting at blood."

"You don't faint at blood."

"No."

"You don't sleep enough for night duty either."

"I'll sleep when they pay me to."

Pride flashed there, bright and quick, and Halima saw, suddenly and cleanly, the whole private structure of the dream: Nura in a washed white apron that would never remain white, Nura writing doses in careful letters, Nura earning a wage that came from work instead of pity or marriage, Nura walking through rooms where men had to move because she carried work in her hands. The dream was small enough to carry: an apron, a ward book, money of her own. Its smallness made it more dangerous.

Halima said, "The colony isn't built. The clinic isn't open. Promises made before relocation are worth the spit used to say them."

"I know that." Nura tapped the certificates. "That's why I want the papers ready before the people with the real houses arrive and fill the places. If I wait until we're already shoved there with everyone else, they'll say come later, come next month, come after the list closes. Later is for people with fathers and land. I need to go in with the file already clean."

On the mat, the papers had turned into a road map: timing, paperwork, one narrow road cut against the grain of the world.

Halima reached for the birth copy because her hand needed something to do. Nura pulled it back first.

"Don't smudge it," she said. "The ink's already thinning at the seal."

"I know what paper does in damp."

"Then you know why I wrapped it."

Halima looked at the packet, not at Nura. "What needs cleaning?"

Nura let out one dry laugh. "You know that too."

She slid the birth copy free and placed it between them. On the father's line sat Monir Hossain. The letters held steady as they always had. Ink had no shame. The paper did not blush because the name could not walk into a room.

"For the training file they want the SSC certificate, birth registration, and either father's NID copy or a guardian line that matches across everything," Nura said. "For correction they want the same. I asked twice."

"Use my NID."

"I asked that too."

"And?"

"And they said a grandmother can stand as guardian for residence, stipend, and ration card if the household list allows it. For birth correction they still want the parent line. If the father is dead, they want the death paper. If the father is living, they want his card, or him."

She spoke the last word flatly, like a pot set down.

Halima reached for the needle again though the thread had slipped free. "People say many things at counters to move a line."

"This did not come from a counter. I checked it on the phone form too. A red box came up: Missing NID. Then mismatch." Nura pointed to the father's line with her thumbnail. "A name isn't enough now. They want the number behind the name, and the number behind that, and the father of that father if they think they can get it. Yesterday at school I watched them do it to everybody."

The schoolroom humiliations from yesterday had lodged sharply in the girl. But Nura copied forms for half the char. Paper entered her faster than it did most people. She knew where the trapdoors were because she had spent years helping others step around them.

"The training isn't tomorrow," Halima said.

"No. Which is why I'm asking now."

"Relocation hasn't happened."

"No. Which is why I'm asking now."

Heat from the banked stove pressed against Halima's shins. On the mat, the birth copy's damp corners lifted and settled under Nura's hand.

Halima said, "Monir worked off-char. That much you know."

"I know what's written." Nura looked straight at her. "I know men at the ghat say he went with dredging crews. I know nobody here has seen him in years. I know whenever I ask a second question you start shifting pots or finding me a chore."

"A house gives a woman plenty to do."

"Not like that."

Halima stood and took the rice pot off the stove though it had already been moved aside. She set it on the floor, adjusted it, lifted it again, put it nearer the wall. Nura watched all of it.

"If he's alive," Nura said, slower now, "then help me find him. If he's dead, tell me where. If the name is wrong, then say the wrongness properly and let's see what can still be done."

Halima turned. "You think saying a thing properly makes an office kind?"

"No. I think not saying it keeps me exactly here."

The blue thread on the mosquito net had snagged in one place, pulling the mesh into a little puckered mouth. Halima stared at it because it was easier than staring at the girl she had raised. Nura was twenty-two, old enough to know the cost of questions and young enough to believe a clear answer might still open a road.

"You've got tutoring today," Halima said.

"At noon."

"Then go wash the lentils."

"Don't do that."

"Do what?"

"Turn me into a chore because you don't want the sentence."

Halima's mouth went hard. "Mind your mouth."

"Mind mine?" Nura leaned forward. Her quick hands flattened over the certificates as if someone might snatch them. "I spent all yesterday listening while that officer asked every man on this island to prove the father above him and the land under him. I came home and thought, fine, ours is bad but at least you're here, at least you know. But you keep making me talk around a ghost as if I'll break if I say the name."

"Lower your voice."

"Why? There's nobody here but us."

"There's always people listening on a char."

"Then let them hear that I need a paper."

Halima's patience tore there, not into shouting but into the dry edge Nura hated most. "A paper. As if that's all. You think I can pull a father out of a trunk the way you pulled those certificates?"

Nura went still, so still that even the papers under her hands seemed to stop rustling. Halima would have preferred anger.

"So there's nothing behind him," she said.

Halima heard the mistake she had made and could not take it back. She bent for the plate cloth she did not need and wiped at a clean place on the shelf.

"I didn't say that."

"You didn't have to."

"You're hearing what you came ready to hear."

"Then help me hear something else."

Halima threw the cloth down. "I put you in school. I kept you there when other girls were being dragged out to wash a husband's mother and spread their legs before they knew what a body could cost. That line on the paper did work. Don't stand here and talk as if it was nothing."

Nura's face changed at that. Hurt came first, then anger over it, the way shame always hardened too quickly in her.

"I never said it was nothing," she said. "I said it isn't enough anymore."

Halima looked away. Beyond the door the yard flashed with white heat. Somebody's hen scratched by the water jar. The ordinary world kept moving with obscene steadiness.

When Nura spoke again, her voice had gone lower and rougher. "Did my mother know?"

The question landed like a hand at the throat. Halima did not answer.

"Did she know his name was going on my paper?"

"Leave your mother out of this."

"How? She's in it before I am."

Halima could smell, with horrible clarity, the old sour iron smell of bedding that had held too much blood. It came and went so fast she almost thought the house had imagined it for her. She gripped the edge of the shelf until the wood bit her palm.

"Some things were done because there was no decent choice," she said.

"That's not an answer."

"It's the one I've got."

Nura made a sound in the back of her throat, half laugh and half disgust. "You always have exact answers for everybody else. Rahima's grandson needs an age shifted, you remember it. Majed's sister needs a witness name, you remember it. Half the island came to you before mobile forms and government seals and still you found a way. But for me you keep saying later, maybe, not now."

Each example struck because it was true. Halima had been exact for others, exact enough to ruin herself, exact enough to make people line up under yesterday's school veranda and wait for her mouth to place them in the world. She could not tell Nura that this case was harder because it was not a case. It was her daughter rotting backward through every paper.

"For you I'm thinking before I move," Halima said.

"Then think faster."

"Life isn't a form where you hit the next box and it opens."

"No. Life is you deciding for everybody and calling it weather."

Halima slapped the mat with her open hand. She had not struck Nura and never would, but the sound cracked through the room all the same.

"Enough."

Nura flinched once and hated that she had done it. The hatred showed immediately.

"Fine," she said. "Then I'll ask without you."

"Ask who?"

"The ghat men. The dredging crews. Sufia apa's customers. Whoever remembers where Monir worked last."

"You won't."

"Watch me."

Halima took a step toward her. "You won't stand at tea stalls with men rolling betel in their cheeks and ask after a father they've never respected enough to keep track of. You won't hand your name to every loose mouth between here and the mainland."

"It's already on the paper."

"Paper isn't a body."

"It becomes one when they block a door with it."

Halima almost said more there. She almost said You were a body first, slick and furious in my hands while your mother bit through a cloth and then burned from the inside out. She almost said I used his name because a dead girl leaves a child too naked in this place. She almost said I would have written the river itself as your father if the river could have signed. But chapter and verse rose in her throat and stopped there, too jagged to pass.

What came out instead was, "You think men will tell you the truth because you're holding a school certificate? They'll smell need on you before you finish the question."

"Then come with me."

"No."

"Why not?"

"Because I said no."

Nura stared at her a long time. When she spoke again, the speed had gone. The slowness frightened Halima more than the shouting had.

"You don't have to tell me the whole thing," Nura said. "I can see you won't. But give me one usable thing. An address. A union. Somebody's father's name who knew him. Tell me if the man on this line ever held me once. Tell me if he knew I existed. Tell me whether I'm supposed to wait for somebody who is dead, or vanished, or just written because you needed a blank filled."

Halima's hand slipped on the shelf edge. The tin spoon hanging by the stove knocked once against the wall, and the thin clang stayed in her teeth.

Halima said, very quietly, "Don't talk about blanks to me."

Nura's eyes flashed. "Then don't leave me in one."

Silence followed. It sat between them like a cut mouth holding blood.
