Outside, a burst of schoolchildren's recitation traveled from a neighboring yard where somebody had started lessons early: the sing-song climb of multiplication tables. From farther off came the cough and grind of a boat engine turning over at the ghat. Life moved in counts and departures.

Nura gathered the certificates with fingers that had gone careful again. She slid them back into the plastic, then into the biscuit tin, but did not return the tin to the trunk. She kept it beside her knee.

"I need the correction before the relocation list closes," she said without looking up. "If I miss the first intake, they'll give it to somebody from the mainland side or somebody whose uncle already knows a clerk. You know that's how it goes."

Halima did know. A chance did not disappear cleanly. It got taken up by another hand and then spent as if it had always belonged there.

"I'll see what can be traced," she said.

Nura looked up at once. "When?"

"Soon."

Nura's mouth twisted. "That's not a date."

"This week."

"Before the next school day?"

"I said this week."

"That means you're still hoping the week will decide for you."

Halima had no answer that would not sound like more smoke. Nura stood, pushed the biscuit tin under her arm, and crossed to the line where the mosquito net hung. For one second Halima thought she meant to tear it down again. Instead she only pinched the new seam between finger and thumb, testing its hold.

"The knot will keep," Nura said. "Ugly, but it will keep."

Then she took the tin and went out into the yard.

Halima did not follow. She listened to the movement instead: the dipper lifted from the water jar, the splash of a rinse, the scrape of Nura's sandal against the threshold stone. After a minute came the smaller sounds that told more truth than tears would have done. The trunk lid opened outside under the eaves. Nura was rearranging her papers again, straightening them, counting the copies, preparing for a road she had been told not to take yet and might take anyway.

Halima stood in the middle of the room with the needle still in her fingers until the thread cut a damp line into her skin.

By noon the heat had thickened enough to slow even flies. Nura had gone to tutor two girls near the mosque lane, carrying the biscuit tin in her cloth bag instead of leaving it in the house. That told Halima how much trust had been bruised. The house felt wrong without the girl's restlessness in it, too still, too much like a room that had been left to an old woman and her objects.

Halima shut the door against the glare and dragged the low stool under the loft shelf. Her back complained before she had one foot up. She ignored it, reached into the dark above the hanging pots, and found the oilcloth bundle by touch. It smelled of oil, dust, old paper, and the faint medicinal ghost of the herbs she once kept near it to fool casual fingers. She brought it down and sat with it on the mat.

For a while she only held it.

Yesterday at school, people had looked at her as if the ledger inside could still save them. She knew that look. Hunger had scraped them thin enough to believe in any bound paper. A book could not turn a river or soften a magistrate. But it could do what it had always done: put a line where life had refused one. Sometimes that had been mercy. Sometimes it had been only a cleaner shape for damage.

She unwrapped the oilcloth.

The notebook's spine had cracked years ago and been sewn back with black thread. Its pages smelled of damp, smoke, and the fat of hands. Halima turned them carefully. Old births filled the pages, names that had married, drowned, left, or turned into men who now stood in school verandas demanding proof of themselves. Her own script changed across the years: tighter when Nura was small and she wrote after everyone slept, larger in her middle age when her fingers had thickened, now broken here and there by the drag of the clouded eye.

She found Saleha's page before she found Nura's. She always did.

At the top stood Saleha, daughter of Halima. One margin from years before noted fever after marriage. Below it, a later entry cramped itself into space that had not been meant for grief. Halima's thumb rested beside the words and made them smaller.

She did not read the whole page at once. She took it as the body had come to her then, in pieces.

Her eye found the date first, the numbers still fixed to that lean wet season.

Her thumb slid down to the labor note and read what her own hand had written: girl child before dawn, cord loop once, loosened by hand.

Then she reached the line she had added later, after the bedding had been stripped and the women had gone home and Saleha's mouth had stopped needing water: Mother dead on seventh day. Child kept in Halima house.

Below that, she saw darker ink from months after, when school had first started to become imaginable instead of absurd:

For papers use Monir Hossain. Dredging line. No return to husband's people.

Halima shut her eyes.

The sentence had looked practical when she wrote it. Practicality was a powerful narcotic. It let a woman believe that if she used a steady hand on the page she had done something cleaner than desperation. Back then Nura had still slept with her fists tucked beside her face, mouth opening and closing for milk after the breast cloth had gone cold. Saleha's in-laws had already started asking what would happen to the child, who would keep her, what claim the dead girl's people imagined they had. There had been one cousin on the mainland whispering about a match for Halima's granddaughter as if the baby were a sack of rice to be promised forward. There had been one man, half-drunk on country liquor, laughing that a girl with no solid father could always be folded into some house later if the price was right. Halima had heard all of it. She had gone home, opened the ledger, and chosen a line that gave the child a father far enough away to be unreachable and respectable enough on paper to get her into class when the time came.

What sickened her was how well it had worked, year after year, class after class.

She turned to Nura's later school note, added in the margin when the first admission form had required consistency. The same name had been carried forward. Her age had been adjusted by months. Everything was neat enough to pass the headmaster's lazy eye. Halima remembered Nura at six, hair oiled flat, furious at the ribbon in it, holding a slate as if the slate had insulted her. She had walked into school because the form said she could.

Now the same line stood up like a bamboo pole across the road.

The page gave Monir Hossain, no NID known, no union certain: a man who had once been useful precisely because he could not be fetched. The world had changed around the old trick. Ghost fathers had worked when ink only needed another ink beside it. They failed when a machine demanded numbers and linked fathers to fathers to holdings to voter lists. Even if Halima started today, what would she bring back for correction? A tea-stall rumor? A dredging memory? A dead end on another char? Offices did not amend a girl into a future because an old midwife said please.

The pages blurred. The old cloudy ache had started again in her left eye.

She pressed the heel of her hand against it and sat listening to the roof tick in the heat. From outside came the faint cadence of Nura teaching. The girl was drilling spelling today, not arithmetic.

"Certificate," Nura's voice called from the lane.

Two small voices stumbled after her.

"Guardian."

Again the children echoed, wrong on the first try, then right.

Halima bowed over the book as if somebody had struck her between the shoulders. The child she had protected was out there teaching other girls the very words that were now barring her own way forward.

This week, she had said.

Her body knew better, with the same certainty by which she knew when a labor would turn bad hours before the mother understood. Delay always dressed itself as prudence first. On a char it called itself waiting for the rain to ease, waiting for the ferry to run, waiting for the bank to hold one more night. Then morning came and half the yard was in the river.

She looked again at the line she had written years ago: For papers use Monir Hossain.

Use: as if a man were a borrowed pot, as if the future would never ask for him back.

Halima closed the ledger and kept her palm on the cover. The house was hot, the mat rough under her legs, the smell of dried fish still caught in the air from breakfast. Nothing about the room suggested that a life could close inside it. But Nura's chance had already begun closing by inches: through dates, through lists, through a red box on a phone screen and a missing number no one could conjure by refusing to speak.

The papers would come to her mat next, damp at the edges, opened under this same heat. By the time the colony clinic called for names, waiting would already have chosen against the girl.


# Chapter 4

The noon heat sat under Halima's tin roof like another body, large and breathless, turning every paper damp at the edges. Since the school had started asking for fathers and houses in writing, her mat had not stayed clear past breakfast. She had spread Rahima Bewa's scraps of proof on the woven mat farthest from the cooking place: a widow certificate with the seal half eaten by mildew, a ration card folded so many times the corners had gone white, and a land-tax receipt yellow as old fat. Rahima herself crouched by the doorway with her smoke-dark shawl slipped off one shoulder, keeping a square powdered-milk tin upright between her ankles. She sold puffed rice from that tin at the ghat and polished the lid with ash every Friday, though the metal had long ago given up shining. Today she had brought it half full and laid two guavas beside Halima's knee, both of them worm-marked near the stem. She never came empty-handed, not even when hunger had made the bones in her wrists look separate.

Nura sat at the window with her good pen and a strip of old newspaper under her hand to keep the ink from taking on damp. She was copying names cleanly for whoever came because half the char could not write the same father twice. Outside, somebody was beating a wet lungi against a bamboo post. The river smell pushed in with the light: silt, fish oil, the soft rot of waterlogged jute. Every few minutes the tin roof gave a dry clicking sound as the sun shifted on it.

Halima squinted down at Rahima's receipt through her clouded eye and the good one both. The number was still legible if she tilted the page away from the glare. "This can stand if the rest of your line stands," she said. "Who can speak for the hut besides you?"

Rahima rubbed a cracked heel against her opposite ankle, scraping off a curl of white skin onto the threshold. "Who speaks for widows?" she said. "The goat, maybe. The grocer if he wants his money."

"Don't joke when your paper is this thin."

"I wasn't joking."

Nura did not look up. "Shirin's husband saw her roof go up after the last flood. He can say she's lived there."

Rahima sniffed. "Shirin's husband says whatever will get him a seat near a government man."

"He still has a mouth, and that's what counts this week." Halima flattened the receipt with the heel of her palm. The paper felt warm already, as if it were beginning to cook. "Come tomorrow before school. I'll see whether they let widows through before the men start shouting."

Rahima opened the tin and pushed it toward Nura without ceremony. "Take some before it goes soft."

Nura finally glanced over, and the quick hardness in her face loosened a little. "Keep it. You'll sell it."

"I've already chewed enough river dust today. Eat."

Nura took a pinch and put it in her mouth. At that moment feet slapped up the yard path, faster and heavier than any widow's. Kashem ducked under the low eave without asking and came in trailing the outside with him: fish scales stuck to his calf, wet mud drying pale on the hem of his lungi, sun caught in the chipped edge of one front tooth when he grinned. He always entered as if the room had been keeping his place warm.

"Dai-ma," he said, breath still up from the walk. "You've opened a court here now? Then hear a man's case before all the chickens start claiming they own land."

Nura clicked her tongue and moved the ink bottle farther from his hand before he could knock it over. "If you brought your papers, sit. If you brought your mouth, go cool it in the pond."

"Look at her. Two certificates and one pen, and now she's already the deputy officer." Kashem laughed for Rahima's benefit, because men like laughter when an old widow is looking at them. Then he crouched on his heels across from Halima and took a laminated card from the fold at his waist. He laid it on the mat with more care than he used on anything living. Under it came a folded tax slip and a photocopy so blurred the letters looked chewed. "I'm not here to waste your time. Aminul sir said I should bring whatever links the father's side to the house line. I brought what there is. You tell me what else is needed, and I'll get it."

Halima kept her hand off the card for one breath, then another. It was only plastic and paper, but the old mistake sat under the laminate as hard as bone. At the school two days ago, Aminul had asked the source of the father's name, and she had paused a fraction too long. The whole room had smelled it. Kashem had smelled it most of all.

She drew the card toward her. "I need to see the homestead receipt."

"You can see it after you tell me whether the name on the card is enough."

"Nothing is enough by itself."

"Fine. Is it the main thing, then?"

The chipped tooth was still showing. The grin around it had gone thin.

Halima unfolded the tax slip instead of answering. The ink had spread in one corner where rain had hit it years ago. "Who paid this last?"

Kashem let out air through his nose. "My father's cousin paid it when my father got sick. You know that."

She did know the cousin had paid. She also knew why the line had been made to bend that way, toward a quieter house and away from the other one, the one where a boy's body could be counted against a debt before he had learned to piss standing. She had written a safer name in the ledger on a night when the western bank was sliding in chunks and Rahima's labor blood kept trying to run under the reed mat and into the mud. A dead man without creditors had become father by the pressure of her pen. At dawn the baby had coughed mustard oil and river mucus and lived. That had seemed the larger fact.

Now the living man crouched in front of her, thirty-five and broad through the shoulders, waiting for paper to hold him the way arms never had.

"I know what I know," Halima said. "That doesn't mean the office will accept it because I say so."

"Then say it in the way they'll accept."

"Don't teach me my own work."

"I'm not teaching you. I'm asking you straight." He tapped the card with one finger. "At school you stopped when they said the name. Everybody saw it. If there's some clerical dirt, say it's clerical dirt. If the line is right, say it's right. I'm not a boy standing with an empty stomach asking for a sweet."

Rahima had gone still by the door. Even the tin between her ankles stopped making small metal sounds. Nura's pen hung above the paper without touching it.

Halima slid the card back across the mat. "Lower your voice."

"Why? So the roof doesn't hear?" Kashem showed the chipped tooth without smiling. "If the line is clean, why am I the one whispering?"

Outside, a child's running feet slowed near the yard and then moved on. Halima could feel sound leaking through the bamboo slats behind her. On the char a private question only had to survive one wall before it belonged to everybody.

"Bring the cousin tomorrow," she said. "Bring the old receipt bundle from your trunk. If the card and land line won't marry by themselves, I'll see what witness can stitch them."

"Witness to what? That my father was my father?" He leaned closer. Fish and sun and the stale sour of river clothes came off him. "Dai-ma, don't make me beg for my own name in front of widows."

Rahima said, "Widows hear plenty even when nobody speaks to them."

Kashem gave her a quick sideways look, not yet angry, only impatient, the way younger men looked at women whose poverty had become part of the scenery. "This isn't your matter, Buri."

Rahima picked a grain of puffed rice from the lip of her tin and crushed it between finger and thumb. "I held the bloody cloth. Don't tell me whose body is my matter."

Halima did not turn. "Rahima."

Kashem heard the warning in her voice and fixed on it at once. "What body? What matter?"

"The birth mat," Rahima said. "The one that took blood. The child that came blue on it. Now the school wants fathers stacked on the table like grain sacks."

The roof clicked again, dry and close. The broken cane end in the mat bit through Halima's sari, and Rahima's thumb made the tin lid tick once. "You came here about your own receipt," she said to Rahima. "Keep to that."

Rahima looked at her and then at Kashem. Her face never prettied itself for pity. Hunger had carved it too long for that. "He came here for certainty," she said. "That plastic's warm from his waist, that's all. It never touched the mat."

Kashem straightened. His performance fell away so quickly it was almost naked. "Then tell me what you make it from."

Halima spoke before Rahima could. "You make it from occupancy, witness, tax proof, and patience. The office wants a chain. That's all."

"That isn't all," Rahima said.

"Enough."

Rahima did not raise her voice. She never needed to. Her words came out as if she were laying tools in a line. "When this one came, the cord was around his neck twice. He slid blue and silent, and Halima had to hook a finger under the loop before he took air. The woman under him bled so hard the mat floated red at one edge. Three days later his navel still wept, and someone had to burn clean cloth and press it there. Someone tied black thread on his left ankle because the right one was swollen from the way he turned inside. You think a card knows that?"

Kashem stared at her.

Nura's chair gave a small scrape on the floor. She had not meant to move. Halima heard it and hated it because now the whole room had acknowledged the shift.

"Rahima." Halima's voice came out low and sharp enough to cut skin. "Stop."

But Rahima had waited too many years to be interrupted cleanly. "That name wasn't there when the child couldn't latch and sucked air till his lips split. It wasn't there when fever soured the first milk and the cloths went stiff by morning. That card came later, dry from a trunk."

Kashem's hand went to his own left ankle as if the black thread might still be there under the mud and hair. "How would you know that?"

Rahima's fingers closed over the tin lid. The metal clicked once. "I know what I know."

It was Halima's own line, thrown back at her and made filthier by precision.

Kashem looked from Rahima to Halima and back again. The room, which had held him since boyhood as something ordinary and legible, had suddenly grown corners he had never entered. He scrubbed the back of his hand over his mouth as if her words had left grit there. "What is this, then? Some joke old people kept for market days?"

"It isn't a joke," Nura said before Halima could stop her.

Kashem snapped toward her. "Then what is it?"

Nura's chin lifted. That was how tenderness first showed in her, as defiance. "I said it isn't a joke."

Halima put one hand on the mat between them. The woven reeds scratched her palm. "No one is laughing at you."

"That makes it better?" Kashem said. "If nobody's laughing, then what have you all been doing?"

Rahima looked at the floor. "Surviving."

Halima's tongue stuck to the roof of her mouth. Even the next roof-click sounded like eavesdropping. Survival was too close to explanation, and explanation would drag the whole old bank with it: the lender's man waiting outside a laboring hut, Rahima younger and wild-eyed with fear, the dead fisherman whose line could be borrowed because his widow had already remarried away. Those facts had flesh on them still. If Kashem got hold of one, he would tear until he found the others.

She pushed the card toward him again, harder this time. "Take your papers."

He did not touch them. "Tell me who that woman was."

Rahima's face changed by almost nothing. Only the skin beside her mouth tightened. "A woman who bled."

"Everybody bleeds."

"Not for you they didn't."

Halima shot her a look that would once have stopped younger women in the middle of crowning. "You'll shut your mouth now."

Rahima met it. "Will I."

Halima opened her mouth to cut her off and had to wet her lips before sound came. She had pulled babies out of women while husbands wept, while mothers-in-law prayed, while storms took the roof nails one by one. Her voice had held in those rooms. Here it stalled in front of an old widow with cracked heels and a puffed-rice tin. Age had thinned more than muscle.

Kashem snatched up the laminated card and bent it in his grip without quite meaning to. The plastic sprang back. "Was I sold?" he asked.

No one answered quickly enough.

He nodded once, as if the silence itself were a form he had learned how to read. "Good. Fine. So we're at that stage."

"You weren't sold," Halima said.

"Then what?"

"Watch your tongue," she said, because it was the only command that came first.

"No. You watch yours. Don't start ordering me now like I'm ten." He slapped the tax slip against his knee to flatten it, then failed, because the paper had taken its curl years ago and kept it. "At the school I thought maybe there was some mismatch in dates. Some stupid government problem. But you look like that when she talks. She talks like this. So tell me what hole I've been living over."

Halima saw again the storm night in fragments that never aged properly. The hut wall had bowed inward with wet. Rahima had bitten through the end of her sari and spat pink threads into her own hair. The baby had come feet first to the knee, stalled, then twisted, and Halima had pushed two fingers into a place no pain should have had room left in and turned him by force while Rahima made the sound of an animal caught under timber. Later, when the child finally took milk, Rahima had held him with both hands open under him, not cradling, just supporting, as if one wrong pressure might send him back where he had nearly gone. Halima had believed then that a boy saved from one line of men was simply saved.

She should have known better. Saved children grew into men with mouths, claims, and shame.

"There are things," she said carefully, "that were done because the time was bad and the choices were worse."

Kashem flattened the tax slip under his palm until the old curl gave a soft crackle. "That isn't an answer. That's a wall."

"It's what you're getting today."

"Today?" He leaned toward her again. "So there is another day. There is another answer."

She did not speak.

Rahima said, "Some truths don't ripen because men demand them."

"And some lies get old enough to stink from one end of the char to the other." Kashem turned on her fully now. The sociable swagger had burned off him. What was left looked younger, meaner, and far less steady. "Why are you talking about my navel and my ankles? Why do you know whether I bit air or milk? Tell me that much."

Rahima's underfed throat moved. She was not a woman who wasted gestures. Even swallowing looked costly on her. "Because some things happen in front of a person and never leave."

"That happened in front of you?"

She said nothing.

Kashem took one step toward her. Nura stood up so fast her stool tipped and hit the wall. Halima planted a hand on his wrist before he got another step.

His skin was hot from walking. The rope-burn ridges across his palm caught at her fingers. For one second the years between them folded the old way: she was the woman who had scrubbed mud off his knees after fever, who had slapped fish bones from his child's throat, who had once smacked him behind the head for throwing stones at a pregnant goat. Then his whole arm went hard.

He shook her off.

Not roughly enough to throw her. Worse than that. He removed her the way a man removed something dirty from his sleeve.

"Don't," he said.

The refusal stayed on her skin where he had shaken her off. Her next breath caught high under her ribs.

Nura moved around the stool and put herself between Kashem and Rahima without seeming to mean to protect either one. Her voice came fast, already revising itself. "You don't need to stand over her. If you want to shout, shout from there. Or go shout outside and let the whole char earn the story properly."

"You knew?" Kashem asked.

"No."

"You swear that?"

"I'd swear it if I thought you'd hear anything besides yourself right now."

He stared at her, then at Halima. "How many people know?"

Halima answered too quickly. "Not many."

His face changed again. The answer did what silence had not. It made the hidden thing countable.

"Not many," he repeated. "So there is a number. I walked around all these years and there was a number of people carrying my beginning in their mouths."

Outside, the woman beating clothes had stopped. Halima could hear only the soft tin crackle overhead and the goat tied near the back fence pulling at its rope. The whole afternoon seemed to lean in.

"Kashem," she said, keeping her voice level by force. "Listen to me. Your claim isn't dead. Do you understand? Paper trouble isn't the same as no claim. Bring the cousin. Bring whoever saw you raised in that house. I can still work on the line."

"The line." He said it as if it were something greasy he had found in his rice. "You want me to walk around gathering witnesses to a line when you won't tell me whether the line is mine?"

"Yours is the life you lived."

"Don't give me old-woman smoke."

It stung because she had nearly given exactly that. She pulled her sari end back over her shoulder, buying a second. "The house you grew up in is still the house you grew up in. The river doesn't change that."

"No. But pens do, maybe."

Rahima said, very quietly, "That pen didn't jump from the shelf. Somebody pinched it and pressed."

Halima rounded on her then. "If you have courage today, where was it twenty years ago? Ten? Last year? Don't sit in my doorway and grow a spine out of somebody else's deadline."

The words came out harsher than she intended. Rahima took them without flinching.

"I was where women like me stay," she said. "At the edge. Fed enough to keep moving. Silent enough to be useful."

Kashem let out a breath that sounded scraped raw. "Useful to who?"

Again the room failed him. Again nobody answered the whole way.

He looked down at the papers in his hands as if they might rearrange themselves if he stared hard enough. The photocopy trembled. Halima could not tell whether his hand was shaking or whether the air itself had thickened enough to make everything unsteady.

"I thought," he said. He didn't throw it at them. He said it down at the trembling papers in his hands, each word separate, with the tea-stall swagger knocked clean out of his mouth. "I thought I was coming here to ask which document needed a stamp."

No one had anything decent to give that sentence.

A torn laugh got out of him and died halfway. "Look at me. I even washed before coming because I thought I had to sit in front of paper." He scrubbed one palm against his chest and left a dark fish-oil smear on the cloth. "And all this time the paper was sitting in front of me."

Halima heard, under the words, every past kindness shifting shape in him. The extra ladle of rice Rahima had once pushed toward his plate at a funeral. The way she had turned away fast whenever he caught her looking too long. The way Halima herself had scolded him harder than other boys, then slipped him work mending net floats when fishing was bad. Mercy looked different once a grown man decided it had been management.

"Sit down," she said. "You're standing like a fool in a hot room. Sit, and we'll sort what can be sorted."

He looked at the place on his wrist where her hand had been and then at her face. "Was I always work to you?"

Her hand went searching for a cloth, a receipt, anything with an edge, and closed on bare mat. Behind that one question sat the whole ugly account of her life. Women on mats. Girls hidden in aunt-lines. Birth dates shifted to dodge one danger and land in another. Children becoming evidence. She had called it help because there had been no cleaner word and no cleaner act available.

"No," she said.

"Then what was I?"

She should have lied better. She should have used the brisk public voice that had gotten people through hemorrhage and fever and birth rooms full of bad husbands. Instead her private one rose, the one too full of touch and old weather. "A child I was trying to keep breathing in more than one way."

He flinched as if that were the worst answer yet.

"Fuck," he said softly. Then louder: "Fuck."

Nura shut the window panel with one quick movement. It did nothing for the sound that had already left.

Kashem stuffed the card and slip back into his waist fold badly, corners sticking out. "Don't come to my house with witnesses," he said to Halima. "Don't send anybody. If I need official help, I'll go to the officer. If I need lies, clearly this char is full of them already."

"Kashem."

"No." He stepped backward toward the yard. "And you." He pointed at Rahima without looking at her directly. "If you've got some story about me, keep it unless you're ready to say it with your full mouth. I'm not taking scraps."

Rahima's gaze did not drop. "Then don't take scraps."

That infuriated him more than pleading would have. He pushed out under the eave and nearly collided with the bucket by the door, caught it with his heel, and sent a sheet of water slapping across the yard. Then he was down the path, shoulders tight, walking too fast for the mud and not caring what the mud did to him.

Halima rose after him. Her knees complained, and the mat's pattern had printed itself into her skin. She stepped to the doorway and called his name once.

He did not turn.

She called again, louder. Halfway down the path he stopped. For one impossible second she thought he might come back, that she still held enough of him to bring his body around if not his trust. Instead he stood with one hand at the knot in his waist cloth, making sure the papers were still there, and looked over toward the western edge where the poorer huts sat lower and meaner against the sky.

Rahima's shawl, dark with smoke and age, was visible behind Halima's shoulder from where he stood. It was no more than a patch of cloth in the doorway. Yet Halima could see the thought crossing his back even at that distance: every old look, every shared silence, every piece of food pressed on him by the wrong hand had acquired a second meaning all at once.

Then he spat into the mud and kept walking.

Nura came up behind Halima but did not touch her. "He'll talk," she said. "Maybe not today. But he'll talk."

"Everybody talks."

"I didn't mean gossip."

Halima let the yard settle around her. The spilled bucket water carried a thin stripe of mud toward the ducks. A fly had found the cut side of one guava. From inside came the papery smell of damp documents and puffed rice gone soft in heat. She could feel the chapter of the day closing wrong, the way a wound closed around grit.

When she turned back, Rahima had not moved from the doorway. The old woman's hands were folded over the tin now, not protective, only tired. Up close the skin around her nails was split in half-moons from washing and ash.

"What did you think you were doing?" Halima asked.

Rahima answered without heat. "Opening it before they carry me out and the grocer comes to shake this tin for what's owed."

"You nearly named him."

"You've still got me with my tongue behind my teeth. That's your nearly."

Nura stooped, righted the fallen stool, and set it by the wall with more care than the act deserved. Her face had gone young in a way anger usually kept hidden. "Is what she said true?" she asked Halima. "Not all of it. The part that matters."

Halima looked at the mat, at the place where Kashem's card had lain and warmed under her hand. The space was empty now except for a smear from his thumb. If she answered Nura directly, one false father would begin speaking to another inside the same room. She did not have strength for both.

"Bring me the dry cloth from the shelf," she said.

Nura's mouth tightened. Stress made her practical before it made her soft. She turned, fetched the cloth, and said nothing more.

Rahima gave one short breath that might have been contempt and might have been pity. "There. Hand over a cloth, flatten a receipt, tell the girl to fetch this or that. That's how you cover a thing."

Halima took the cloth from Nura and used it to wipe the damp from Rahima's land receipt though the paper was not wet enough to need it. Her hands wanted work or they would start remembering too cleanly. "That covered thing put rice in his mouth. A dead man's name on a card got him into a yard with a rice pot and no creditor waiting at the door."

Rahima's voice stayed level. "Into a yard, yes. Not out of my body. Not through the fever that kept him on me three nights."

Halima bit the inside of her cheek and got the flat iron taste of blood. She had heard that same accusing steadiness before, in other forms, from other mouths. Jamila's perhaps, years later. Nura's soon enough. Halima was tired of being asked for the end of a road she had only ever built one dangerous step at a time.

"You think I don't know where he came from?" she said. "I wrote him into a house where he'd live long enough to grow that chipped tooth and that big mouth. That was the work in front of me."

"And now the big mouth has to stand in your room asking whose blood made him."

"Because paper keeps dragging him back," Halima said. She folded the cloth once, twice, until it was a hard small square in her hand. "Card, tax slip, school table, one after another. Don't put every stroke of that pen in my lap alone. You nodded when I wrote."

Rahima's eyes, dim and stubborn, settled on her. "I nodded because I was twenty-three and bleeding through the mat. You kept the same father on his forms after he had teeth, after he had legs, after he knew which doorway to run to."

Halima kept folding the cloth though it was already folded, pressing the corners into the web of her thumb until they hurt. Rahima had left her nothing easy to argue with.

Nura set the ink bottle back upright in the corner where it had been safe before Kashem came. "If he goes straight to Aminul with this mood, it's going to explode at the school."

"I know."

"Then what are you going to do?"

Halima looked out through the doorway to the path Kashem had taken. The mud still held the crisp edges of his heels where he had pushed off in anger. Beyond the middle houses the land sloped away westward, poorer with every step, until it gave itself up to scrub, huts, and the river that took what was least defended first. Somewhere down that line Kashem would be walking and rewriting his own childhood at speed, fitting fresh suspicion over old memory until nothing sat where it had sat an hour ago.

She had once thought the hardest part of cord-cutting was the blade: making one clean motion with a hand that knew it held the difference between attachment and breath. Age had taught her otherwise. The hard part came after, when the child kept living and the cut kept traveling.

"I'm going to school before him tomorrow," she said.

Nura frowned. "To tell Aminul?"

"To see what can still be held shut."

Rahima made a dry sound in her throat. "Nothing stays shut now."

Halima did not answer. She bent over the widow certificate again, though the letters had blurred in her clouded eye. Outside, the roof clicked once more in the heat. From far off came the thin engine whine of a boat turning somewhere in the channel. In the lane a pair of boys shouted, then quieted, then shouted again. Life on the char had not stopped. It had only changed its story about one man.

By evening, Halima knew, Kashem would remember every kindness done to him by the adults in this room and hold each one up to the light like suspicious paper. He would not yet know what the hidden thing was. He would know only that it had shape, that it belonged partly to his body, and that other people had been living around it for years as if it were ordinary. Sometimes that kind of ignorance bit deeper than truth. Truth at least arrived in words. Suspicion had to feed on whatever it found. By morning, if she failed to get to the school first, it would have official walls to lean against.


# Chapter 5

The new notice made the school wall look briefly official. Halima had got there before Kashem, just as she'd meant to, and found Aminul driving two rusted tacks through it and flattening its corners with both palms so the wind would not peel it loose. The sheet was official-white, thick enough to survive one rain if God was inattentive, and the Bengali on it came in fat government lines that made even the people who could read lean in suspiciously. Sandals sucked at the schoolyard mud behind her. The concrete wall sweated heat left over from morning. The storm had not arrived yet, but the air had closed up in the way it did before bad weather. The char held its breath and regretted it already.

Nura stood to one side of the crowd with her hair pinned back anyhow, though the damp kept pulling thin curls loose around her temples. She was reading silently before Aminul spoke, her lips moving over the lines faster than the others could track them. Mofiz had stationed himself near the doorway in a fresh white panjabi, beard edges dyed too evenly for a man who claimed not to care about appearances. He kept one hand lifted in a patient-chairman way. He wore the notice like his own idea and the fear on the faces below it like a small inconvenience he would manage for them.

Aminul cleared his throat. He did not shout. He never shouted unless the launch engine forced him to. He held a folder away from his shirt, careful even now about sweat, and said, "Please listen once before you start asking. Every household has until Friday noon to present whatever supporting papers it has. National identity cards, land-tax receipts, birth entries, school certificates, ration cards if that's all you've got. If a husband and wife are claiming one household, bring the marriage paper. If the paper is missing, bring two adult witnesses who can stand for the marriage and the household together. Names must agree across the file as far as possible. If you wait past the deadline, the first list may become the last list for anyone missing from it."

The yard broke at once.

"What if the card's with my brother on the mainland?"

"My husband never made an NID, what then?"

"The kazi died."

"The kazi ran off with the second wife."

"My marriage paper went in the flood three years ago."

Aminul raised his hand, then lowered it when he saw the hand did nothing. "Bring what exists," he said. "Bring witnesses who can be defended. Do not wait for the last day."

Mofiz turned half toward the women at the back. "Why's everyone panicking? We're not strangers to paperwork. Bring your files to me, and I'll see what can be arranged in order."

Halima felt the yard shift at that, bodies angling not toward the notice but toward whoever looked likeliest to own a way around it. She had seen that movement in labor rooms too, when the pain stopped being bearable and every woman searched the faces around her for the one person who might still know a trick.

She said, loud enough to cut the yard but not loud enough to sound like Mofiz, "Go home and open your trunks. Anything that smells of mildew comes out now. Anything wrapped in wet cloth gets unwrapped now. If your paper is under the bed, drag it out before the rain beats you to it."

Several heads turned. An old man near the front, Abdul Karim with the cataract milk in both eyes and the habit of nodding before he had heard the end of a sentence, called, "Dai-ma, what if the name is wrong in one place?"

Halima answered him in the tone she used on nephews who wasted her time. "Then you find the place where it's less wrong and you don't stand here talking while the sky darkens."

The crowd laughed because she had been sharp, and then they stopped because she had been right. That was the moment the fear changed shape. Before, the deadline had been a thing posted on a wall. After that, it became an afternoon's worth of reachable work and unreachable work. People began leaving while Aminul was still speaking, first one man, then two women, then a cluster of boys sent running toward houses with orders. Nura stayed long enough to read the notice aloud to the women who could not make the letters hold still, then came away from the wall with her jaw set.

"What did they write under the list heading?" Halima asked quietly.

Nura wiped sweat off her upper lip with the back of her wrist, leaving a streak of newsprint. "Preliminary verification for compensation eligibility."

"Meaning?"

"Meaning if you're not alive on their paper by Friday, they won't look hard for you after."

Aminul had read the notice in office Bangla. Nura had already pulled the blade out of it.

By noon courtyards that usually held fish baskets and sleeping children were crowded with documents weighted under enamel plates, brass tumblers, cracked stones, the wooden pestles used for chili. Women shook out old sari ends and spread land slips on them, turning each page toward the light with the same grim care used on a feverish baby. Boys were sent under beds with broom handles to hook out rusted trunks. In one yard a man made his daughter read the same date four times, then cuffed the back of her head when the numerals slid out wrong. Near the pond a woman held up a marriage deed gone soft at the fold and said to nobody, "If this drowns before I do, let them bury me with it." Only after seeing all that did the char begin to look like a place sitting an examination set by men who had never had to keep paper dry in a tin-roof room.

Halima turned first toward the eastern side, where the ground held a little longer before surrendering to rain. Jamila's house stood there, its yard swept even in monsoon, with two ducks complaining under an upturned basket and Majed's disemboweled engine parts arranged on a mat as neatly as prayer offerings. Today the parts had been pushed aside. Majed had laid documents over an overturned fish crate and was staring at them with the stubborn concentration of a mechanic counting missing parts.

Grease sat black in the half-moons of his nails. One small screw was tucked behind his ear out of habit even though there was nothing mechanical in front of him worth tightening. Jamila knelt beside a square biscuit tin wrapped in old blouse cloth. When she opened it, a faint sweet smell came up, cloves and stale sugar fighting with dried fish. She must have stored the papers with spice to keep insects off. The kabinnama lay inside, folded around their marriage date like something too embarrassed to be seen at midday.

"You came quickly," Jamila said. She wiped the stool with the one dry corner of her anchal and set it near Halima without quite looking at her. "Sit, Khalamma. Or don't. The stool's damp."

Halima remained standing. "Let me see what you've got."

Majed slid the receipts toward her. "Tax slips from three years. The newest one's half-eaten at the corner. My NID. Her card. Two children's birth cards. The marriage paper. My father's old occupancy slip, though the ink's running."

Jamila tapped the kabinnama. "And my name is one spelling here and another on the NID. The clerk wrote an extra letter. He said it didn't matter at the time."

"It mattered the second he took the pen off the page," Halima said.

Majed scratched his cheek with a knuckle. "Can they throw a whole household for one letter?"

"They can do whatever the file lets them pretend is reasonable."

Jamila gave a short breath that was almost laughter. "That means yes."

Halima went through the packet in order. Jamila had done better than most. Each paper had its own wrapping. The children's cards were tucked between school notebook covers so they would not crease. Majed had even found a length of plastic fertilizer sack to line the tin. Not one edge had been left to chance.

"Who can witness?" Halima asked.

Majed answered immediately. "The imam. My mother's cousin. Both know the marriage."

Jamila said, "The imam knows the ceremony. My mother's cousin knows the house."

Halima looked up. The correction had come too fast to be casual. Jamila's face remained smooth, but one of her thumbnails kept rubbing the edge of the biscuit tin until the metal sang lightly under the nail.

"And if somebody remembers more than the ceremony?" Jamila asked.

Jamila's thumbnail stopped on the tin rim. Halima unfolded the kabinnama and squared it against the children's cards, buying herself the space of one breath with her hands.

"Keep the packet separate from the children's papers. Find cloth that isn't damp. If the rain starts before evening, move the tin off the floor."

Jamila's mouth tightened. The question stayed between them, bright and flat as the marriage paper in noon light. Halima left it there. Any answer she gave today would only tear something open she could not stitch shut before Friday.

Majed grabbed the practical and held on. "I've got a plastic drum top. I'll set the tin on that. If water comes in, it won't wick up."

"Do that," Halima said.

When she left, Jamila was already repacking the papers by category, and Majed had gone to find the drum top. They were the sort of household the state preferred: a man who knew how to arrange objects, a woman who knew how to keep a face steady, children folded inside both their names. Even so, one wrong witness could unpick them.

The path west turned soft underfoot. By the time Halima reached the poorer huts the mud had begun taking sandals whole, belching when she pulled free. Everywhere she looked, paper had come into daylight that had not touched daylight in years. A girl with a runny nose held open three school certificates while her mother waved a hand fan over them. A man had spread yellow land receipts across a fishing net and was blowing on them through a gap in his front teeth. At another yard, two sisters were arguing over whether a dead husband's nickname counted as a legal alias if everyone on the char had used it longer than his formal name. Nobody was speaking about the river. Nobody was asking whether the administration could be trusted. The whole island had dropped from politics to inventory.

Rahima's hut crouched lower than the others. The ground under it had already made peace with submergence. One of her goats had dragged its rope around the threshold peg until the knot sat hard with mud. Rahima herself stood in the doorway with her smoke-dark shawl thrown back and a tin of puffed rice under one arm. She had opened the tin for the papers and forgotten to shut it; half the rice had gone damp and clung to the inside in a pale paste.

She said, not greeting Halima, "Look what passes for a file in my house."

Inside the tin lay two land-tax receipts turned tea-colored with age, a ration card with her dead husband's name half rubbed off, and a folded scrap on which someone had once written a date so faintly it looked like breath on glass. The papers smelled of old kerosene, rat droppings, and sweet stale grain. Halima touched the top receipt and felt the fibers rise under her fingertip. Another rain like the last and it would come apart in layers.

"Where was this kept?" she asked.

"Under the bedding. Where else? You think I've got a wooden chest with brass lock like Jamila bibi?"

On the threshold sat Noorjahan from two huts over, a narrow-hipped woman who never removed the cheap man's wristwatch she wore loose over her bangles, though the watch had been stopped at 6:12 for years. Every morning she set the hands back there by habit and wore the dead time all day. Her younger son had a wet cough that dragged from him nights like a saw through green bamboo. Halima knew the sound because it traveled through the lane. Noorjahan was shelling dried peas into her lap and pretending not to listen.

"I told her," Noorjahan said, "if she needs me to stand up at the school, I'll stand. But a witness loses a day too. My boy needs syrup. The shop won't take blessings."

Rahima did not look at her. "She wants two hundred taka."

"One fifty," Noorjahan corrected. "And I didn't say cash only. Rice counts."

Halima felt something inside her go flat and hard, like wet cloth slapped onto a stone. "So that's the price now."

Noorjahan lifted one shoulder. "The price of anything is what the frightened person will pay."

Rahima barked one humorless laugh. "Hear that, Dai-ma? Even my marriage needs rent."

"Do you have anybody else?" Halima asked.

Rahima's eyes moved toward the west, toward the lane where people preferred not to be seen going if they had other options. "Two men saw the burial when my husband died. One's half deaf, the other drinks. A woman from my old para remembers the wedding, maybe. If I go ask her, her daughter-in-law will make a face first."

"And you?"

Rahima pressed the heel of her hand to the tin and counted it out like debt. "I lived with the man fifteen years. I bled in that house. I buried two babies from that house. I kept his mother wiped when she lost her bladder. But none of that prints itself."

Noorjahan resumed shelling peas. "Printing costs extra," she said.

Halima wanted to snap at her. Instead she crouched, because the receipt edges were curling and needed flattening before they cracked. The packed-earth floor was cool and damp through her knees. "Find dry cloth," she said. "Not from under your bedding. Dry. Wrap each paper alone. And raise this tin. If water comes, it comes low first."

Rahima watched her hands. "You'll speak for me?"

The question carried more than widowhood inside it. Kashem's name lay between them unsaid, raw from the last chapter of daylight. Halima kept her eyes on the paper. "I'll speak to what I can defend."

Rahima gave one slow nod. "You have defended more dangerous things than a widow's roof."

The words landed cleanly because they were true. Halima repacked the tin and left before either of them could open the rest.

At Sufia's tea stall the whole char had narrowed into a queue of temporary bargains. The stall stood just above the ghat on bamboo posts gone black with spilled tea and river damp. Sufia moved behind the kettle with her usual slight limp, quick hands lifting glasses by the rims. Betel had stained the corners of her mouth a deep brick red. She kept the cash folded inside the sleeve of an old sweater no matter the heat, because years ago a customer had snatched coins off the counter and she had never trusted open surfaces since.

Today money was not the only thing changing hands.

"I'll stand for your sister if you stand for my wife," one man said.

"I've never been in your courtyard."

"You ate there on Eid."

"Eating isn't the same as witnessing."

A boatman with river mud up to both knees pinched a damp receipt between two fingers. "If you need me to remember land and marriage both, the price goes up. One memory is work. Two memories are business."

The man across from him spat betel into the ditch. "Then keep your memory. I'll find a cheaper uncle."

A woman Halima knew only as Shila, who braided her daughters' hair so tight the girls looked startled all morning, held a damp envelope to her chest and asked Sufia, "If the kazi's nephew swears he saw the register, is that enough?"

Sufia poured tea without looking up. "Enough for whom? For God, for the officer, or for the man who'll challenge you after?"

The woman did not answer.

Halima stood just outside the awning, listening. A boy too young for a beard and too old for innocence was waving cheap laminating sleeves and promising mainland shine by tomorrow if he was paid in advance. "Officers trust shine," he kept saying. On the bench, an old man repeated, "Seventeen years, not sixteen. Friday wedding, not Monday," while his nephew thumped the table in time so the lesson would stay in the body. By lunchtime even memory had rates.

Halima caught the laminating boy by the sleeve. "Enough shine. Run to my house. Tell Nura to clear the front room and bring out her copybook. Widows first. Missing marriage papers next. Name mismatches after that. And if anybody's paying for a witness, they come before they hand over the money."

The boy blinked at her.

Sufia slid a hot glass away from a reaching hand. "Repeat it."

"Widows first," the boy said. "Missing marriage papers. Name mismatches. No witness money till Nura apa hears the names."

"Go," Halima said.

Only then did Sufia look at her over the kettle steam. "You'll want tea."

"I'll want a second sky before I want tea."

"Can't sell that."

"You'd try if it came in glass."

One corner of Sufia's mouth went up. Then she nodded toward the lane. "Kashem's been prowling since the notice went up. He asked three men to stand for him. Two said maybe. One said yes too fast, which means he'll ask for something rotten later."

Halima did not thank her. She went on.

Kashem was by the boat sheds, exactly where Sufia had said he would be, moving too quickly for a man with nowhere useful to go. He had tucked his laminated identity card into the band of his lungi and kept pulling it out to check it, then shoving it back, then pulling it out again. The card would survive the rain. The lie sealed inside it could stay dry and shining in a man's hand while every path linking it to a household turned to slurry around him.

He saw Halima and barked a laugh that drew two other men closer just to hear what would follow.

"Dai-ma," he called. "Come bless the file. Maybe it'll start telling the truth if you breathe on it."

He laughed too loudly, enough to pull two men closer, and kept slapping the card against his palm until the plastic clicked. The chipped place in his front tooth flashed when he grinned. His palms were rope-burned and wet with sweat. The card had gone cloudy where his thumb kept rubbing.

"Show me the rest," Halima said.

"The rest is the joke." He slapped the card against his other hand. "This says one thing. Land says another. Old men say wait. Officer says bring witnesses. Everybody wants two names and a paper bed for them to lie in."

One of the listening men drifted away when Halima looked at him directly. The other pretended to adjust a rope.

"Who'll stand for you?" she asked.

Kashem shrugged with theatrical carelessness. "Depends what day they want honesty. For one day I can probably buy enough of it."

"With what money?"

"With fish, with labor, with the shame I've still got left. Same as anybody."

He leaned closer. His breath smelled of stale bidi and river water. The grin was gone. "Tell me one thing true, then. If I bring the card, the tax slip, two men who'll say they know the house, will they take me? Or am I supposed to stand there and let my life get laughed at again?"

Halima heard two questions in him. One belonged to Friday's bench and might still bend. The other belonged to the life behind it, and the char had been charging him for that one long before today. She said, "Bring the card, the tax slip, and any man who plans to swear for you to my house before Asr. If he won't say it in front of me and Nura, he won't say it straight at the school."

Kashem's face shut by increments. Pride came back into it like shutters closing. "That's your new medicine, then. Parade us through your front room first. Very good." He tucked the card back into his waistband. "Don't touch my papers, Dai-ma. They bite."

He said it lightly enough that the men behind him could pretend it was joking. The warning still landed with teeth. Halima left him with the river smell and the tarred hulls and the knowledge that a man she had once carried on her hip as a feverish child now treated her like contamination he had to manage in public.

If Kashem reached Friday with half-bought witnesses and names still fighting inside the same file, the school bench would eat him alive and call it fairness. It would do the same to Rahima, to Jamila, to every house whose life had never once lined up in a straight government hand.

At the mouth of the lane she pictured Nura hearing the boy hammer at the door, then shoving the lentil pots off the low table and opening the copybook flat. That would do. Let the broken files come to one room for once instead of rotting separately in a hundred damp tins.

When she reached home, Nura had already turned the front room into the sorting shed Halima had just ordered into existence. Mats were rolled up to keep them from damp. The low table had been taken over by stacks of other people's paper bound with thread. One pile held school certificates. One held ration cards. Another held copied name lists in Nura's neat hand. She had even chalked small headings on broken pieces of cardboard and propped them against clay bowls. The room still smelled of lentils, but she had made an office out of it.

A little girl from the next lane slept by the wall with her cheek on an atlas primer. Two teenage brothers waited in the yard, shifting from foot to foot because Nura had told them not to come in with muddy hems. Nura kept moving from stack to stack, burn-marked wrist flashing each time she reached.

"What's this?" Halima asked.

"Your order, improved." Nura did not stop moving. "The boy came panting from the ghat like you'd sent him to outrun death. He said widows first, missing marriage papers next, name mismatches after that, and no fools paying witnesses till we hear the names. So I started sorting before the yard filled."

She held up a tax receipt rolled tight as a bidi. "If one more grown man hands me paper like this, I'll push it back down his throat."

Despite herself, Halima felt the edge of a smile. It did not stay long. "How many households?"

"Twelve already. More coming after Asr. Jamila apa sent word asking if a different spelling counts as a different woman. Noorjahan wants to know if she can witness two marriages on the same day. Somebody asked me to copy a date cleaner than it was written the first time. I told him to get out."

"Who?"

Nura glanced toward the yard where the brothers waited and lowered her voice. "Not one of ours. A man from the western lane. I didn't even bother keeping the name."

People had always come to Nura for forms, school letters, phone numbers. This was different. A man had already asked her to make a date look cleaner than it had been the first time. Better penmanship, he seemed to think, could wash a lie.

"Did you eat?" Halima asked.

Nura made a face. "Did you?"

Neither had. Halima washed her hands at the pitcher and the water ran gray. She could smell old paper on her skin already, that mold-sour smell that sank into the creases beside the nails. Outside, the sky had turned the color of tin scrubbed with ash. The thin sun people had trusted all afternoon was failing.

She worked beside Nura for the next hour, not sitting either. They untied packets, aired cards, separated school documents from land slips so the cleaner paper would not stain from the older rot. Halima found one birth entry stuck to the back of a political leaflet and peeled them apart with the patience of a woman lifting gauze off a healing wound. She sent boys home with instructions shouted from the door: off the floor, out of wet trunks, each paper alone, each witness told clearly what name belonged to which house. Every solved stack uncovered two worse ones. They kept sorting.

When the first thunder rolled, it came off the school roof first, a great sheet-metal boom traveling across the char. Everyone froze for that one breath in which hope sometimes still survives. Then the wind came sideways through the yard and scattered two loose receipts off Nura's table. The teenage brothers lunged after them. One page slapped against the wet threshold and darkened instantly where the water touched.

"Inside," Halima snapped. "All of it inside now."

The rain arrived as a wall.

Tin roofs across the char broke into noise so total it seemed to erase distance. Houses vanished a few yards beyond their doorways. The yard turned from dirt to moving brown skin in seconds, water pushing around the brick edges and carrying chili stems, goat pellets, a child's lost pencil. Nura and Halima lifted the paper stacks to the loft shelf and then started receiving whoever reached the door first.

A woman came with three school certificates wrapped in her blouse and weeping because the ink had already begun to feather. Halima laid them between two aluminum plates and told her to stop crying and fetch dry cloth. Majed sent one of his sons with the biscuit tin high under the boy's shirt. The plastic lining had held, but the old occupancy slip inside was damp along one edge and would need air by morning. Noorjahan arrived not with Rahima's witness promise but with Rahima's kerosene tin, because water had come under the west wall and the goat had kicked the bedding loose. The tin leaked from one corner. Half the puffed rice had turned to paste against the paper.

Halima took one look and went herself.

The western lane in storm light was barely a lane. Water shoved at her calves where the ground dipped. The wind snatched her shawl back so hard it thumped against her spine. At Rahima's hut the threshold had vanished under the runoff. Rahima was inside on her knees, both hands pressed over the lid of the bedding chest, shoulders locked, pinning it in place. The goat screamed from the corner where its rope had tangled in a stool leg.

"Leave the chest," Halima shouted. "The papers first."

Rahima lifted her face. Rain had found its way through the roof seam and was running down her temple into the collar of her blouse. "The chest has the blanket."

"The blanket can get wet."

"So can I."

Still she let go of the chest. Together they hauled the kerosene tin onto the string cot. Halima opened it and saw at once what the water had done. The oldest tax receipt had wicked damp halfway up. The ration card had fused at one fold. The scrap with the faint date was bleeding into nothing, numbers losing their edges like bank under current.

Rahima watched Halima's hands with a concentration usually reserved for surgeons and money counters. "Can you save it?"

"Maybe."

"Maybe is a rich person's answer."

"It's the only one I've got."

They laid the papers on the cot slats one by one. There was nowhere truly dry. Rain rattled through the tin roof and dripped from two nailed holes near the wall. The room smelled of goat dung, wet ash, and paper coming apart. Rahima stood with her hands hanging useless because there were no more containers left to lift.

Then, over the rain, Noorjahan's voice came from the doorway. "I've brought my rice tin. It's higher than yours."

She stepped in without asking, dead watch flashing at her wrist. In her other hand she held not only the tin but a square of blue polythene cut from a fertilizer sack. Her son's cough medicine had not yet been bought. Halima could see the calculation still working behind her eyes. Yet she had come anyway.

"Put the good paper in this," Noorjahan said. "The worst one can stay out and curse the sky."

Rahima looked at her for a long second, suspicious even inside gratitude. "You'll still ask your one fifty tomorrow."

"I'll ask my one fifty whether you drown tonight or not. Take the polythene."

The exchange was so exact it almost steadied Halima. Even mercy had invoices now. At least that made the terms visible.

They got the driest papers into the new wrapping, raised the tin on two bricks, cut the goat loose and moved it to the doorway where the water ran less deep. By the time Halima left, her kameez clung cold to her back and her fingers were pruned from wet. The storm owned the whole char now.

Lanterns stayed lit all over Kadam Char later than oil should have allowed. Through rain and tin and wind, Halima heard Abdul Karim in the next lane drilling his daughters' birth years like a Quran lesson. Farther east a woman shouted, "Not Friday market, idiot, Eid day. If you can't remember the courtyard, remember the goat tied there." A man splashed past Halima's wall with a packet tucked under his shirt and yelled that Sufia wanted two witnesses at dawn, sober ones this time.

At home, the loft shelf bowed under the added weight of packets drying in borrowed safety. Nura sat cross-legged under it with a notebook in her lap, writing by lantern because two more families had sent names after dark. Her eyes were red from smoke. She did not look up when Halima entered. She said only, "Kashem bhai sent a boy. He wants to know if a man can witness a father he never met."

"What did you tell him?"

"That men do worse things every day."

Halima hung her wet shawl on the door peg. Water dripped off it onto the packed earth in a line as straight as writing. She listened to the rain hit the roof hard enough to shake dust from the rafters. On the shelf above her head lay Nura's certificates, Rahima's rescued tin, Jamila's carefully wrapped packet, three school cards from another lane, and a marriage deed so damp the seal had begun to smell of glue again.

Across the lane, two men who had not sat in the same courtyard in years were huddled under one sheet of plastic while a woman fed them dates from memory and slapped the wrong ones out of the air. Noorjahan's stopped watch flashed at Rahima's doorway as she argued witness price without lowering her voice. At Majed's house a lantern moved from one room to another and then held still, as if Jamila had planted it above the papers and dared the weather to take the house around them instead. Whatever else the rain spared, it had not washed the edge off the question Jamila had asked at noon.

Halima stood in the middle of her room with the smell of wet silt and mold rising around her and understood that the char had already begun to sort itself before any officer came back with a list: into the households that could gather enough paper, enough witnesses, enough swallowed insult to sound single and clean for one morning, and the households whose only honest shape was contradiction. The rain hit every roof alike. The files would not.


# Chapter 6

Jamila's noon question still had daylight on it when her boy came with one instruction: before maghrib, and not through the front if anyone was sitting there. By late afternoon Halima was crossing the middle path of the char with last night's mud drying in scales on her heels. The sky had gone the hard white that came when the monsoon began loosening its grip without offering any coolness in return, and the laundry she passed smelled of half-rinsed river water and the sharp rot of fish laid out too long. That alone told her the matter was not one for the school wall or the queue at Aminul's table. On Kadam Char people whispered for only two reasons. Either a girl was in trouble, or paper was.

Jamila's yard held both dried fish and engine grease. Strips of loitta hung from a bamboo pole under netting, silver skin gone dull in the heat. Near the duck coop Majed had spread the opened belly of a small ferry engine over a jute sack, every bolt laid in a line as careful as a prayer mat. He looked up when Halima came through the side gap in the fence, wiped his hands on a rag that only moved the grease around, and gave her the brief nod of a man already ashamed of what he needed to ask.

"Come in," he said. "Before people start circling."

Halima glanced toward the lane. "People already circle. They call it walking."

He almost smiled, then did not. The chipped enamel mug by his foot held kerosene gone black with use. Even his tools looked anxious.

Inside, the room had been cleared in the way households only managed when worry outranked comfort. The sleeping mat was rolled and tied. The children's slates had been stacked under a bench. On the floor, over a faded floral sheet, Jamila had arranged documents in separate islands: marriage paper, tax receipts, a death certificate in a crackling plastic sleeve, a folded photocopy of an identity card, two vaccination cards pressed flat under a steel plate so the corners would stop lifting. She sat with her back straight and her ankles tucked under her, her sari pinned so neatly at the shoulder it refused the room. There was always something disciplined in the way she occupied a room. Mess stopped at her skin. Let it touch her, and it began making claims of its own.

The children were missing. That was another sign. Jamila did not send them away unless she expected words that would stick to them.

Halima lowered herself beside the papers. Her knees complained against the hard floor. "Who has died now," she said, "that all this has been spread like a shroud?"

Jamila's mouth moved, not quite a smile. "Nobody has died. That's the difficulty."

Majed came in behind her and stayed by the door rather than sit. A tall man made himself look smaller by habit when he had bad news; Halima had noticed that about him years ago, long before he married Jamila. He kept one hand around the rag, twisting it the way other men twisted prayer beads.

Jamila touched the plastic sleeve with two fingers. "My mother came yesterday."

Halima looked up. "Your real mother?"

Jamila's eyes hardened a shade. "I only have real mothers, Khalamma. One is dead and made of paper. One is alive and hungry."

"Then say the alive one," Halima said. "Paper has ears."

Majed made a rough sound in his throat. "She's come three times in two days. This morning before sunrise. Again after noon. Karim has told her he won't take her into his line at relocation."

Jamila turned a receipt so Halima could read the aunt's name and the holding number beneath it. The plastic had a salt-stiff feel under Halima's fingers. "If I stay where you put me," Jamila said in the same tone she might have used for the price of lentils, "I remain my aunt's daughter. This house stays tied to her homestead. When compensation comes, it comes through that line. Our children stay clean on the page. If I go back to my mother's line, I can take her with me, or at least stand beside her when they load the boats. But then this claim becomes a lie in the open. The land goes soft under us."

Halima heard, from the yard next door, a hen protesting the hand that had found her legs. Somewhere farther off a hammer struck tin. All over the char people were prying ordinary life apart and pretending it was only carpentry.

She lifted the death certificate. The dead aunt's name sat there in blue ink, formal and stubborn. Halima remembered the woman as a narrow-faced widow with a laugh too sudden for her thin body, a woman who never stopped oiling the hinges on her trunk even after the trunk had been emptied three floods in a row. The claim was not large. A strip of higher eastern ground, a house space, enough legal weight to keep a family from sliding into the category of loose occupants. Enough to matter now.

"And your mother," Halima said. "What line does she have if you do nothing?"

Jamila gave a small shrug that looked more tired than careless. "A widow's name. A western-edge shack that belonged to a man she never married and nursed through fever until he died. Karim says he can't shrink his share for her. His wife says the colony houses aren't courtyards where anybody can sleep at the edge. They said it nicely until they didn't."

Majed came off the doorframe at that. "Don't put it as if this is only Karim's meanness. The colony will be tin boxes in rows. They won't let ten half-attached people stack under one roof the way we do here."

Jamila did not turn toward him. "I know what rows are, Majed. I can see a queue when it's coming."

Halima laid the certificate down and reached for the land-tax receipt bundle. The paper had that old dry softness that came before it broke at the fold. She flattened it with the heel of her palm. "Why now? Why not last month? Why not last year, when there was still time to lie with some room around it?"

Jamila let out a breath through her nose. "Because last year nobody was counting households as if God would be checking against a register. Now my mother is looking at boat lists and house numbers. She says if I don't go back to her, she will be put wherever there is leftover room or no room. She came today to be answered, not comforted. She's outside."

Majed said, too quickly, "And if the answer breaks wrong in the lane, what happens here? This roof gets called stolen. The marriage paper gets pulled crooked. Every bastard at the school table starts asking whether my children were born inside a household that exists or one Halima invented while the ink was wet."

He stopped. His own raised voice had startled him. Majed was not a loud man by nature. When volume came to him, the sound cracked under its own weight.

Jamila folded her hands in her lap. Her nails were short and clean. She had prepared herself even for this. "That is why she sent for you," Majed said to Halima, quieter now. "Because you know where the first cut was made."

He meant the first cut, and Halima almost told him not to speak of it that way. Then she thought of the cord knife in her box, the little curved blade she still rubbed with oil against rust, and kept silent. A cut could save a mother. A cut could also decide which blood got recognized and which got wiped from cloth.

She had not thought of that night in months. The crisis with Nura, Kashem, the schoolroom, the notices on the wall, all of it had dragged older memories out by their ankles, but Jamila's case had stayed under the lid because it had once felt finished. The girl had been moved. The marriage-for-debt had failed. A decent enough husband had been found later. The papers held. Halima had let herself count that one among the usable mercies.

The night itself had smelled of wet jute and old fear. Jamila had been fifteen, maybe not quite, with a braid heavy from cheap coconut oil and henna still fading rust-red from a cousin's wedding. Her mother had brought her after midnight, not by the front path but along the back where the goat pens stood, because the men of the household were drunk on rice liquor and bargaining courage. The story had already reached Halima through two houses before they did: flood loss, borrowed money, interest growing teeth, a widower from downriver willing to cancel part of it if the girl was sent before the next market day. He was old enough to have a married daughter. Someone had said, laughing, that he wanted young hips because his first wife had gone dry and mean. Someone else had asked whether the girl was healthy, as if they were discussing poultry stock.

Jamila had stood in Halima's doorway with both hands pressed between her knees. There was a bruise above one elbow where somebody had tried to steer her toward agreement by force. Her mother had not wept. That was what Halima remembered most clearly. The woman had spoken in a voice scraped flat by terror, saying only, If she stays under their roof they will send her. Take her name away from us. Take it tonight.

The dead aunt had provided the hole in the wall. She had died six days earlier with no living child to carry her small homestead claim. The formal death had not yet traveled beyond the union desk. On paper there was still looseness, enough for Halima to push a girl through if she moved before dawn. She had sat Jamila down by the lamp and made her say the aunt's name until it came without hesitation. She had changed the mother's field, shifted the household line, walked a folded note to a clerk who owed her for two births and his own sister's obstructed labor, and by the time the debt men came sober with relatives in tow, Jamila belonged somewhere else.

Jamila had not been safe. She had never been safe. But she had been elsewhere enough to spoil the bargain.

Halima remembered Jamila's mother standing with both palms flat on the floor before leaving, not in gratitude exactly, more in bodily surrender to the fact that a child had just been cut loose from one danger into another nobody could yet see. That woman had paid for the lie too. She had lived thereafter as the mother who could not publicly mother her own daughter without threatening the daughter's shelter.

Jamila was watching Halima now, reading memory across her face the way other women read weather. "You remember," she said.

"I remember you smelling of wedding henna and wanting to be sick."

"I was sick," Jamila said. "I threw up behind your duck shed. You told me to wipe my mouth and learn a new mother before sunrise."

Majed looked from one woman to the other. Whatever pieces of the story he had been told over the years, not all of them had been told in front of him. His shoulders tightened.

Halima said, "If I had not done it, they would have married you off by market day."

Jamila's voice stayed polite. That was always when it cut deepest. "Yes. You didn't pick me out of thin air for sport."

The words landed harder because they refused drama. Halima nodded once. "Then what are you asking from me now? Another trick? Or a witness?"

At that, Majed stepped fully into the room and squatted opposite them, his knees cracking. "If there was another trick, I'd take it," he said. "I wouldn't spit on it. But if there isn't, don't keep her outside while we talk around her."

There was a time, not even so many years back, when Halima might have found another path by instinct. A dead father's name left floating in one ledger. A cousin willing to call a widow his father's sister for a season. A birth date leaned three months one way or another until shame fit under marriage cloth. Kadam Char had always required looseness. The state had arrived now with laminated cards, cross-checked lists, men who typed a name once and expected it to behave forever. Every old soft place in the records had turned into a snag.

She looked at the papers again and hated how clean they seemed in Jamila's care. "Not one I can stand behind when Aminul starts matching households across the lists," she said. "I can move a lie between two people. I can't make the government forget it has three copies of the same question. Bring your mother in."

Jamila's face changed, not much, just enough to show she had hoped to avoid that. "She's by the duck coop."

Majed let out one sharp breath. "Of course she is."

Halima turned on him. "Then fetch her. This isn't only tidy people and clean documents."

He rose without another word. Through the doorway Halima heard his low murmur, then the scrape of a sandal against the threshold. Jamila's mother entered with her head bent not in submission but to clear the lintel. She was smaller than Halima remembered, or maybe poverty had pared her. The gray in her hair was not evenly spread; it clustered at the temples, thick where worry had worked on her longest. In the corner of her sari she had tied a brass key, rubbed bright by years of handling. Halima knew it before the woman sat down.
