It was the key to the dead aunt's old trunk, the trunk that had lost its lock to rust long ago. Some habits stayed alive longer than the objects they belonged to.

The woman did not come all the way onto the sheet. She sat on the bare floor at the edge of it and tucked one foot beneath her calf. "I heard enough through the wall," she said. Her voice had a dry grain to it. "No point pretending I didn't."

Jamila looked away.

Halima said, "Then speak with your mouth instead of your ear."

The older woman nodded. "I'm not asking to take her roof. If I had a roof anyone would honor, I'd sit under mine and die there. I'm asking not to be sent off as leftover. Karim's wife has already counted plates and sleeping bodies. She has counted me as too many."

Majed shifted his weight. "No one here called you leftover."

She turned her head toward him. "Men don't have to call a thing by its name before they do it."

The room stayed still after that. Outside, a goat knocked its horn against a post three times in a slow irritated rhythm.

Jamila's mother went on. "When my brothers fixed that marriage, I brought her to Halima-dai myself. Don't make me prettier than I was. I didn't fight them with a stick. I didn't run away carrying my daughter like a story heroine. I brought her here because they were measuring debt against her body, and I had nowhere else to put her. I'm asking now for the same reason. I've nowhere else to put myself."

Jamila flinched. The sentence landed in her body before it reached her face. Halima noticed because Jamila was usually too careful to let the body answer before the face did.

Majed crouched by the door again, but the fight had gone out of his posture. "If she returns to your line," he said, speaking mostly to the floor, "this house comes under question. You know that."

"I know it better than you," the older woman said. She lifted the brass key and let it fall back against her knee. "My sister's land saved my daughter. Now it can save her children. I know the arithmetic. I also know what it's like to stand in a queue where no one wants your name attached to theirs."

Jamila looked at her then, finally. "If you know the arithmetic, why did you come?"

Her mother's mouth tightened. "Because knowing arithmetic is not the same as swallowing it."

Halima felt the old urge rise in her, the one that had built half the trouble now pressing on the char: the urge to reach into the gap between need and rule and stuff something living into it before the gap could close. A cousin. A false dependency. A widow attached by service instead of blood. But every possibility she touched already belonged to another household, another fragile line. To solve this one cleanly she would have had to dirty three others.

She placed both palms on the sheet. "Listen. The deadline list is coming. Once a line hardens there, any correction becomes public trouble. If Jamila goes back openly to her mother's line now, she will have to defend why the aunt's name held her all these years. That means her marriage papers get looked at. The homestead claim gets looked at. The children may get looked at. If Jamila stays on this line, you remain weak unless someone else takes you in and means it, not just for the count. There's no decent road here. There's only choosing which damage you can carry."

Majed said, "I can carry shame. I can't carry the state striking our household off."

His fingers had gone to the rag again, working it so hard the cloth twisted thin as rope. Halima imagined the parts seller on the mainland, the waiting account, the humiliation of turning up to relocation with tools but no recognized place to set them down. Majed was not counting future profit. He was counting the exact size of disaster.

Jamila's mother heard it too. "You think I want your children out under plastic?" she asked. "I've seen enough rain on babies."

Jamila closed her eyes. When she opened them, the calm in her had become something stricter. "Everybody stop making me the road between two honest places," she said. "I'm not. I was shifted once because men wanted to settle debt with my body. Then I married under the name that kept me out of that bed. I made a household under it. I've sold eggs under it. I've stood in the market being called Majed's wife under it. My children know which grandmother is dead because I taught them from the story I was given. If I go back now, I don't return to some clean beginning. I tear up the only life that held."

Her mother said nothing.

Jamila kept going, and now the measured sentences began changing angle at the end, exactly where they hurt most. "If I step back into your line this week, I become that girl again for everyone watching. The one who was movable. The one who could be passed from debt to pity to duty depending on what solved a man's problem. I won't do that. Not for Karim's cruelty. Not for the government's list. Not even for you."

Majed looked at her with naked relief, then seemed ashamed of having looked that way in front of the older woman.

Her mother took the shame away from him by refusing to look at him at all. She watched only Jamila. "Then say the rest clean," she said. "Don't wrap it."

Jamila swallowed. The tin roof clicked once as the heat shifted overhead.

"I'll keep the line I'm living under," she said. "I'll defend the aunt's claim. I won't go back in the record."

There was no cry after it, no curse, no wailing fit for neighbors to gather around. The older woman only reached for the brass key at her sari corner and rubbed it between finger and thumb until the skin at her knuckle blanched. When she spoke, her voice stayed level.

"All right," she said. "Then I'll carry my bedding where I can and see who lets me set it down."

Majed rose halfway. "Aunty-"

She cut him off with a glance sharp enough to prove how much of Jamila had been her all along. "Don't call me aunty now. It won't sit right in my ear."

The words struck him silent.

She got to her feet slowly, using one hand on the wall. Halima noticed the tremor not in her knees but in the hand that pushed against the mud plaster. Hunger did that before age did. The woman had always been slight. Now she had gone down to wire and intention.

Jamila stood too, but did not move forward. Halima could not tell whether that was cruelty or the only discipline keeping her from collapsing into a different answer. The older woman adjusted the end of her sari over her hair, a gesture too ordinary for the size of the room's change.

At the doorway she stopped. "You didn't turn me away then," she said without turning. "I know that. But don't send mercy after me now. I can't swallow it."

Then she went out into the yard.

Majed took a step toward the yard, then checked himself. Outside, Halima heard the soft thud of sandals being found and put on, then the gate string lifted, then dropped. No one shouted. Next door the hen had resumed complaining about her fate. The world kept its small noises. Only the angle of a hand had changed.

Jamila remained standing, her face gone pale beneath its composure. Halima pushed herself up with a grunt and regretted, at once, being old enough that every serious conversation had to end with the body reminding everyone it too had terms.

"Those schoolbooks on the shelf, that trunk handle Majed wired back on, the duck eggs under the net," she began. "You held them here under this name. I'm not-"

Jamila turned on her so fast the movement made the papers stir.

"Please don't scrub it clean," she said.

Halima stopped.

Jamila did not raise her voice. Majed shifted back toward the wall. He would have borne shouting more easily than this low, exact voice. "Don't make it blessing because you can't fix it now," she said. "You moved me, Khalamma. Say that plain. You took me out of one house and put me in another name. It worked. Good. I married there. I buried my aunt there. I squatted on that floor and bled my children there." She pointed, not dramatically, just once, at the packed earth beneath the sheet. "So don't tie it up neat now. Don't tell me it's blood here and duty there. I picked who gets cut. That's what I did."

Halima felt the words travel through her with a steadier force than accusation. In labor rooms, women sometimes said the truest thing of the day while half-delirious with pain, then remembered none of it after. Jamila would remember this. She had been saving the sentence for years without knowing it.

Majed said, almost pleading, "She wasn't trying to harm you when she did it."

Jamila rounded on him. "And I'm not trying to hurt her now. See how little that helps?"

Majed's jaw worked once. "I'm hanging on to the roof, the claim, the children's names. I know what that may do to your mother. Spare floor if she's lucky, plastic if she isn't. I know it. I still can't tell you to throw these papers away."

His gaze dropped to the floor, to the marriage paper, to his own hands. Grease had worked so deep into the cracked lines of his fingers that no amount of scrubbing would bring the nails fully clean. He pinched the rag between thumb and forefinger and twisted until the cloth bit into the callus, shoulders pulled up in the old useless instinct to get smaller under a blow. Halima saw that Jamila had dragged his fear out of the place where it could pretend to be logistics and forced it to speak in front of the wound. He was hearing, all at once, the cost of his roof in another person's sleep.

Jamila lowered herself back to the floor and began, with maddening care, refolding the documents. She slid the land-tax receipt into plastic, smoothed the death certificate, and set the marriage paper between two old calendar sheets so the crease would not deepen. She moved like a woman preparing evidence and dressing a wound so it would stop leaking in public.

Halima said, quieter now, "I thought once the marriage paper held and your children came squalling onto this floor, the old cut would stay under the mat."

Jamila did not look up. "That let you sleep. It didn't put it under anything for me."

The sentence had no ornament on it. That was why it stayed.

Halima looked around the room: schoolbooks wrapped in old newspaper on the bamboo shelf, the tin trunk with one handle mended by Majed out of braided wire, the basket of duck eggs under a net to keep the rats honest, the engine head cooling in the yard, the dead aunt's name holding the walls upright, the living mother outside the fence reduced by hunger but not by confusion. The room would not let her sort the saved from the cost.

Majed finally spoke, his voice rough now that the shame had words under it. "I'll go to Karim tonight," he said. "Or the cousin near the ghat. Maybe if I keep at them, someone'll take her in proper."

Jamila stacked the papers and tied them with a strip torn from an old lungi. "Go," she said. "Push. Ask. Beg if you have to. I'm not stopping you."

It was not hope. It was labor handed to a man who still had somewhere to sleep.

Halima moved to the doorway. The air outside had thickened toward evening; cooking smoke drifted low, and somewhere on the western side a child was practicing the same two lines of a school lesson until they sounded like a curse. Jamila's mother was already at the bend in the path, smaller even than before, one hand pressed against the brass key at her hip, guarding the last thing that still answered to her hand. She did not look back.

Behind Halima, Jamila said, "When the school asks, I'll stand by the aunt's line."

Halima nodded, though Jamila could not see her face.

"And if later somebody tears it open in public, let them," Jamila went on. "But I won't open it myself just to prove I'm still somebody's daughter."

The words left Halima with one hard fact: the cut she had made years ago had not stopped with the girl.

Halima stepped down into the yard. Majed, still inside, had taken up the rag again and was scrubbing a wrench hard enough to make the metal squeal. Jamila knelt over the papers and pulled the plastic sleeves square with the same grim neatness that had held her together all afternoon. Outside the fence, the older woman kept walking toward the western edge where the ground softened and the houses thinned, one hand pressed to the brass key at her hip, and the key knocked against her with each step.


# Chapter 7

Aminul kept lifting his folder clear of the table before sweat from his wrist could creep into the forms, then setting it down again in the same careful square, as if neatness might spread by contact. Under the schoolhouse roof, late-Friday heat had softened every paper edge in the room. The wall behind him was already patched with old tape and newer notices. Outside, goats nosed the damp steps. Inside, the queue had thinned for prayers, but the air had not. It held body salt, wet jute, mildew from opened document bundles, and the sharp iron smell of people waiting to hear whether their names still belonged to them.

Halima had worked since dawn with a dryness in her mouth she could not wash away. She kept her thumb on one corner of each sheet while Aminul read the lines aloud, because the papers wanted to curl back into themselves and because her hands wanted work. When her hands were still, she heard too much.

People had begun lowering their voices when she looked up. The hush was new. The gossip itself was not. Kadam Char had always talked like a shared courtyard, one mouth picking up where another left off. But until this week the talk around her had still carried some ordinary need in it: a fever, a labor pain, a question about whether a baby ought to be nursing so little in this heat. Now the lowered voices carried appetite.

Mofiz Uddin came to the table with a widow from the eastern rise and two boys scrubbed harder than boys liked to be scrubbed. The widow kept pinching the corner of her sari over and over at the same place, worrying a tear she had already stitched once with blue thread. One of the boys had river mud still dried inside his left ear. Mofiz, in a cream panjabi that had somehow stayed clean where everybody else's cuffs had gone gray, rested two fingers on the receipt bundle as if he had brought not papers but calm itself.

"This one should be simple," he said. "Farhad Jalil's house. Wife, two sons, occupancy uninterrupted. If we move cleanly, Officer sahib, we can finish three more before the khutba."

Aminul adjusted the sheet in front of him. "The tax receipts are under Jalil, then Farhad. The younger boy is fine. The older one is not tracing to Farhad in the vaccine list. He is attached elsewhere."

Mofiz smiled without changing the set of his mouth. "Attached elsewhere by mistake, maybe. That happens on a char."

The widow's eyes flicked to Halima and away. She knew whose memory had been summoned into the room before anyone said it.

Aminul turned, polite as always when he needed something ugly from her. "Halima-dai, can you confirm the older boy's father line?"

Halima looked at the child. He was eight, maybe nine, with thin wrists and a scab on one knee that he had worried open again with his nails. She had seen him enter the world in a different house, on a night when the wind kept flattening the lamp flame and his mother bit a strip of old gamchha so hard she bled onto it. The boy had not come from Farhad Jalil's line. Everybody at this table knew why he had been brought under it afterward. He needed stability, food, and a roof on the higher side when the western edge started breaking off. But stability was not the same thing as truth, and Mofiz had been stacking that eastern ground with useful people for years, thickening one household into three and three into five so the better plots would land where his hand already rested.

If Aminul ever ran a real audit through births instead of tax receipts and public boasting, some of Mofiz's branches would fall clean off the tree.

Halima said, "The younger one was Farhad's. The older boy came before that marriage."

The widow shut her eyes. One of the boys started picking the skin beside his thumb until she slapped his hand down without looking.

Aminul made a note. "Then this claim needs witness support before inclusion."

Mofiz let out a soft breath that might have passed for amusement if Halima had not known how carefully he spent every expression. "Witness support," he said. "Yes. These days we're rich in witnesses. Poor in certainty, rich in witnesses."

He shifted his attention to the people standing closest behind the widow, letting them feel included. His real talent lay in that little turn of the body. He did not speak loudly. He spoke so that a crowd began to hear itself inside him.

"And of course," he went on, "some records are public, some are private. Some memories sit on the table where everyone can see them. Some stay wrapped away and come out only when the mood is right."

A few faces changed, though not much. Mouths tightened. One person leaned quickly toward another. The room did not need more than that. It had been waiting.

Aminul said, "If there is another document relevant to a claimant, it can be submitted through the same process."

"Submitted," Mofiz repeated pleasantly. "Yes, exactly. Through process. Not through favor. Not through gifts. We all want the same thing."

Halima turned her head. "Then say it with your full tongue. Don't lick around it."

The widow drew in breath through her nose. Aminul's pen stopped moving.

Mofiz gave Halima a small bow of the head, almost respectful. "People are nervous, Halima-dai. That's not slander. It's weather. They hear that a hidden book can make one child belong here, another there. They hear men have carried fish, cash, a duck, whatever they had, and walked away with a cleaner father than the one God gave them. Fear asks questions. I don't stop fear from asking questions."

Halima felt the old schooling in her body before she answered: she must not rise to theater, and she must not feed a crowd a shape it could take hold of. But she had spent too many years in birth rooms where a woman would die if nobody named the thing plainly. She had no gift left for varnish.

"Fear?" she said. "This is not fear. This is you seeing your own roof leak and pointing at mine."

The line hit the people nearest the table first. It was not hard enough to cut him, but it was hard enough to let them feel the contact. Mofiz's beard edges, dyed too dark and always a little rusty at the roots by the second week, twitched once at the jaw.

"Be careful," he said softly. "A respected woman should protect her own respect first."

"Then step away from it."

A boy laughed under his breath and stopped when Mofiz's head tilted half an inch. The room held still. From outside came the flat clank of a hand pump and, farther off, the first testing burst from the mosque loudspeaker, somebody tapping the microphone before the call.

Then Tuli came running in from the lane, braid half-open, slate-pencil dust still gray on the side of her hand from the sums Nura had made her copy that morning. She slowed only long enough to look frightened of the room she had entered, then pushed toward Halima anyway.

"Khalamma," she said, breath tearing in and out of her. "They're at your house."

The heat in the schoolhouse changed shape. Halima stood before she asked.

"Who?"

"Rasel-bhai and Biplob and more. Chairman sahib's boys. They're saying there's another book. Nura apa is shouting. They pushed the door."

Aminul came halfway out of his chair. "Who's conducting a search?"

Mofiz spread one hand, hurt by the word. "Search is a heavy term, Officer sahib. Some excited young men have gone to ask for reassurance. The island is strained. Let's not criminalize worry."

"You don't ask reassurance with hands," Halima said.

She had already moved clear of the bench. Her knees protested. She ignored them. The room parted for her the way rooms once had when she came carrying hot water and old knowledge. But nobody reached for her sleeve or asked what to do. People flattened themselves to the walls and let trouble pass.

Aminul said, "If private property is being interfered with, that is outside the verification procedure."

He shoved back his chair. "No one's authorized to touch that house."

He got as far as standing. The doorway had already jammed with men on their way to ablution, each suddenly anxious about the open files, the government seal, the unsigned witness slips spread across the table. Aminul had no constable to send and no clerk to leave in charge; if he walked out, half the morning's claims could come back challenged as tampered with before he reached the lane.

Mofiz laid his hand lightly on the nearest bundle. "Write the interference down first, Officer sahib. I'll send two men from the mosque lane to keep things calm."

"Then write that down," Halima said, and did not wait to see whether he would.

Tuli turned and ran again. Halima followed her into the white glare. Behind her, Mofiz's voice floated out in its smooth village-chairman patience.

"Please, everyone. We will settle this with dignity."

No scrape of hurried sandals followed the words.

The lane to her house took seven minutes in decent weather. In half-dried mud, with Friday gathering the men toward the mosque and all eyes already leaning ahead of her, it felt longer than the crossing to the mainland. Her sandals dragged. Mud climbed her hems and cooled, then warmed again against her shins. She passed the pond where ducks worried at algae. She passed Jamila's yard, where a row of washed egg trays had been propped in the sun and left. She passed two boys wrestling over a bicycle rim until they saw her face and fell apart from each other at once.

From the mosque came the full call at last, long and practiced, the muezzin's voice sailing over the tin roofs and the fish racks and the wet clotheslines. It would have sounded beautiful on another day. Today it only sharpened the obscenity of what waited ahead: men rinsed for prayer putting their hands into women's trunks.

Halfway there she saw the first cluster of watchers. People had spaced themselves with village cunning, each body parked at a threshold or lane turn so nobody had to admit to gathering. Women stood at their thresholds. Two old men pretended to discuss a net weight by the lane turn. A girl with a baby on one hip stood without bouncing it, just holding still and looking. The lane stayed open for Halima, but the mouths along it stayed shut. Thin walls made privacy a lie on the char; public silence finished what the walls started.

Her gate rope hung loose.

Nura was in the courtyard, hair out of its braid, one side of her kameez yanked low where somebody had grabbed cloth and missed flesh by an inch. Her school certificate packet lay split open near the water jar, pages fanned in the dirt like pale fish bellies. Rasel stood at the doorway with a bamboo pole in his hand. He had tied his new white skullcap too high, so both ears stuck out red beneath it. Biplob was on the veranda, breathing through his mouth, his upper lip shiny with the effort of trying to grow a beard that only came in at the chin. A third boy Halima knew only as Selim-from-the-ghat had both hands inside a bedding roll, shaking it as if papers might fall out with the dust.

"Don't touch that," Nura shouted, and went for the certificate packet first.

Rasel swung the bamboo pole toward the loft shelf instead, jabbing at the stacked quilts, the old birth basin, the bundles of washed cloth Halima kept above rat height. A rain of small things came down: string, a cracked feeding cup, a packet of dried neem leaves, a rolled mosquito net. Something rusted hit the floor with a chime.

Halima crossed the courtyard and said, in the voice that used to stop girls from pushing too early and boys from fainting at the sight of blood, "Out."

No one moved.

Mofiz was not inside the house. Of course he was not. He stood just beyond the threshold shade, still in clean cream, damp at the temples from ablution, one hand resting lightly on the doorpost he had not technically crossed. He had arranged himself where he could oversee without touching. Behind him two men from the mosque lane slowed and stayed.

"Please," he said. "No one wants a public insult."

Halima did not look at him. "Then call them out."

"If there is no hidden ledger, this ends quickly. If there is one, people deserve clarity."

Nura laughed once, a sound too sharp to be laughter. "Clarity? Your pigs are in my clothes."

Selim let the bedding roll drop and went to the tin trunk instead. He was the one who always chewed fennel because he wanted to quit cigarettes and never quite managed it. Halima recognized the sweet stale smell when he lifted the lid and plunged both hands in among folded blouses, Nura's bras, the packet of washed cloths for bleeding days, the wrapped comb and mirror, the old yellow frock Halima had kept because Nura had once been small enough to disappear inside it. He held up a bundle and shook it. Menstrual cloths, sun-stiff and square-folded, opened in the air like slapped birds.

The women at the lane edge saw. Halima knew they saw because the whole line of them went soundless.

Nura flew at him. "You bastard."

Biplob caught her by the forearm. She twisted hard enough to leave her nails across the soft part of his wrist. He swore and let go, but Rasel had already come off the step, bamboo pole dropped, both hands on the next obstruction in front of him, which was Halima's granddaughter.

He shoved her once, impatiently, the way a man shoved a door that ought to have opened faster.

Nura hit the iron trunk's hasp with her mouth. The sound was small and mean. Then blood came, bright on her lower lip, then darker when she sucked air through it and split it wider.

For one half second Halima did not see the room. She saw only the blood crossing the brown dust on Nura's chin and the stupid looseness of one hand where Nura had tried to catch herself. It was the same hand that wrote forms cleanly, with the little burn scar at the wrist from tipping a kettle as a child. Halima had spent her whole life being first to blood. It had never once felt ordinary.

"You touched her," she said.

Rasel took one step back. "She came at us."

Nura wiped blood with the heel of her hand and stared at what the hand had become. "Try it again."

The call to prayer still hung in the air outside, not finished, the last notes dragging over the courtyard like wire. Mofiz said, more sharply now because the scene had developed edges he had not planned, "Enough. Conduct yourselves properly."

But nobody left. Properly, on that day, meant the boys kept searching and he kept his hands clean.

Rasel went back to the loft shelf, angrier now. He struck upward with the bamboo pole, once, twice. The second jab tore through the old reed mat Halima had pinned across the back beam. Something heavy shifted behind it.

Halima knew what before it fell. She had taken the ledger out two nights running and hidden it badly each time because exhaustion made fools of older bodies. She had meant to push it deeper after Fajr. Nura had asked for tea. Somebody had come with a witness slip. The morning had eaten the intention whole.

The oilcloth bundle dropped from the shelf and hit the floor between them all with a dense, wet sound.

For a beat the whole courtyard locked in place. The cloth was old blue once, then gray, then the color of river tin. One corner had been rewaxed so many times it held a different shine from the rest. No seal marked it, no office string. It had the greasy shine of something handled in secret for years.

Nura lunged.

So did Rasel.

His fingers caught one end just as Nura got the middle. The old wrapping gave with a ripping noise that Halima felt in her own teeth. A black notebook corner slid into view. Behind it came three folded sheets in Halima's own hand, copy pages she had kept tucked inside the wrap for quick checking, the names and dates crammed smaller than her eyes liked now.

Then everybody moved at once.

Halima was across the room before she remembered her knee. She drove her shoulder between Rasel and Nura, not to knock him down, only to make a wall where there had not been one. The notebook hit her palm. One of the folded sheets fell open just enough to show columns. Another skated under somebody's heel. Nura, blood on her mouth, bit down on a curse and clutched the torn oilcloth.

From behind Halima, Mofiz's voice came smooth again, because now the thing existed and smoothness could afford to return.

"Careful," he said. "If there is nothing improper, there is no need to panic."

Halima gathered the notebook and what sheets her hands found, then shoved the whole torn mass inside the front of her blouse, under the crossed sari edge, against the hot fold of skin below her breasts. She was sixty-three years old and broad-palmed and sweating. She had delivered half the island. If one of them reached there in front of the lane and the mosque call and the staring women, nobody on the char would ever pretend again that this was only a search.

Rasel stopped. So did Biplob. Even Selim, with a pair of Nura's underwear hanging from two fingers because he had forgotten to drop them, froze and looked away first.

The line of her blouse was the only boundary left to her, and she hated them for leaving her nothing else.

"Come take it, then," she said.

Silence answered her.

Nura spat a clot of blood onto Rasel's foot.

"Cowards."

Mofiz did not step inside. He folded his hands in front of him, chairman again, grievance settled into community language. "You see the problem," he said to nobody and everybody. "A private register affecting public claims. Hidden from process. Hidden from scrutiny. This is exactly why people are worried."

Halima's breath came hard enough to lift the notebook against her ribs. She wanted to go for his throat with words. She wanted, more stupidly, to go for it with her hands. What came out was flatter than either.

"You sent boys into a woman's room on Friday and found an old book. If God was measuring shame by weight, you'd sink through the char."

Somebody in the lane murmured. A woman by the gate looked straight at Mofiz for one beat, then smoothed her sari over her mouth and looked away.

Mofiz inclined his head as if receiving public insult was itself a public service. "No one is accusing you yet," he said. "But this will have to be explained properly."

At the gate, somebody repeated the word yet under a breath. Rasel's shoulders loosened, and the watchers kept staring.

He turned to the boys. "Enough. Leave the room."

Now they listened. Rasel set the bamboo pole aside. Selim dropped the underwear back into the trunk with two fingers, suddenly delicate. Biplob looked at the blood on his wrist where Nura had scratched him and seemed uncertain whether he was proud of it or ashamed. One by one they backed out into the courtyard, taking their muddy footprints with them only in the sense that feet always did. The prints stayed.

Halima stood until the last of them had cleared the threshold. Only then did she let her hand leave the front of her blouse and go to Nura's face.

"Sit."

"I'm fine."

"Sit before I put you there."

The command still worked on one person in the house. Nura dropped onto the edge of the trunk, wincing when the movement jarred her hip. Blood kept welling from the split in the lower lip, not fast, but steadily enough to stain the front of her kameez.

Outside, Mofiz was speaking to the watchers in his lowered public voice, the one that implied sorrow and administration in equal measure. Halima could not catch every word. She caught enough words to hear the shape of it: regrettable, necessary, community trust, hidden entries, proper review.

She could picture the schoolhouse clearly enough: Aminul pinned to his table by open files, the government seal, and a Friday crowd that would call any unattended paper spoiled. By the time he fought free of that room, if he fought free at all, the words hidden book would already be moving from yard to yard ahead of him.

Inside the room, the heat felt fouled. Male sweat, damp prayer cloth, the fennel stink from Selim's mouth, mud ground into the mat where Nura slept. Halima fetched the brass lota with shaking fingers, rinsed a clean cloth, and came back to her granddaughter.

"Hold still."

Nura did, but only from the neck down. The rest of her was all insult.

"Don't dab," she said thickly. "Press it."

"If I press harder you'll bite me."

"I'm already biting."

Halima folded the cloth smaller and put steady pressure to the lip. Nura hissed and caught Halima's wrist. The burn scar flashed white against her own blood.

"Teeth apart," Halima said.

Nura obeyed. Pink water ran over Halima's fingers and down into the hollow between Nura's collarbones. Under the blood smell lay the dry-paper smell of the certificate packet and the older smell of a house opened too roughly: rice dust, kerosene, stored cloth, things that should only know women's hands.

"He touched my trunk," Nura said after a moment. She named the trunk before the injury or the blood. "That fennel-breathed bastard put his hands in my trunk."

"I saw."

"And that one with the cap. He pushed me like I was a goat in the lane."

Halima looked at the swelling starting along Nura's mouth and at the darkening patch on the side of her hip where the cloth had tightened. Under the girl-angry, there was a tremor she would deny if named.

"Lie down after this."

"No."

"You may want a different answer. That one stays the same."

Nura laughed and immediately regretted it. "You should've let me take his eye."

"And then what? Let them drag you through the yard by your hair while the whole lane watched?"

Nura pulled her hand free and wiped the side of her face with her own palm. "They already had my bras in their hands. What's left to save?"

Nura's sentence went through Halima cleaner than a shout would have. She turned away long enough to wring out the cloth over the basin. The water pinked, then clouded.

She surveyed the room properly for the first time. The trunk stood open. Bedding lay torn loose. Birth cloth bundles were split. Nura's certificates were creased in the dirt near the water jar. The old basin lay on its side. The loft shelf hung open at the back where the reed mat had been ripped down. The house looked less robbed than translated into the wrong language.

Her hand went instinctively to the notebook hidden under her blouse. It was still there, though the oilcloth was torn and the copy pages sat uneven against it. She did not dare unwrap it yet, not with Nura bleeding and the lane still full of ears.

"We'll wash the papers after your mouth settles," she said.

"My mouth's settled. The whole char is what's not settled."

Halima gave her a look. Nura gave one back, lip swelling around the contempt.

Neither of them said what sat between them: the boys had found the book because the book existed. Mofiz had not created that part from rumor. He had only chosen the moment and the witnesses.

The first person to come after the courtyard emptied was not Aminul and not a neighbor bearing sympathy. It was Jamila.

She paused at the threshold as if measuring the distance from her sandals to the room's disorder. Jamila was dressed as carefully as if she were going to market instead of stepping into a violated house: sari pleats straight, hair oiled and parted cleanly, blouse pinned near one shoulder where the hook had loosened. She carried no basket. Her bare hands told Halima more than tears would have. People brought baskets when they meant comfort. They brought bare hands when they meant retrieval.

Majed waited just outside the gate with a screwdriver tucked behind one ear and grease still dark in the cuticle of his thumb. He did not come in. He kept one hand on the gate rope as though testing whether it would hold if the char got any softer underfoot.

Jamila's gaze flicked once to Nura's swollen mouth, once to the open trunk, then settled carefully on Halima's face.

"I heard there was a disturbance," she said.

The extra politeness made the sentence colder than accusation.

Halima said, "You heard right."

Jamila nodded. "I left my papers here after the other night."

There it was.

Nura gave a short laugh that broke into pain halfway through. "Of course."

Jamila did not look at her. "I thought it was safer. Now people are saying anything kept in this house will be looked at. I've got children. I can't let their file get mixed into another story."

Halima could have answered with anger. She had earned some. Jamila had sat in this very room two nights ago and told the truth of her own life in a voice so controlled it had felt like cut bamboo under Halima's palm. Halima had watched her choose one loyalty over another because the state had turned kinship into arithmetic. But the arithmetic did not stop because the numbers hurt.

"Top shelf of the smaller trunk," Halima said.

Jamila crossed the room without touching anything else. She bent, lifted the packet wrapped in old calendar paper, checked the twine knot, and held it to her stomach as if the papers could feel exposed too.

Majed spoke from the yard, not entering, not quite absent either. "Are they saying the officer saw the book?"

Halima said, "The officer was still pinned at the school with the seal, the files, and a Friday crowd. He saw nothing. The chairman's boys saw enough."

Majed rubbed the side of his nose with the back of his wrist, leaving a gray streak there. "That'll do."

Jamila still did not accuse her. When anger came, Jamila cut by sounding reasonable.

"Khalamma," she said, "if they ask, I didn't leave our packet here overnight."

Halima felt the shape of the request and all the filth packed inside it. Jamila was not only asking her to protect them. She was asking to be protected from being known as needing her.

"Take your papers," she said. "Tell whatever story lets your children eat."

Jamila's chin moved once, almost a flinch. For a second Halima thought she might answer from the softer place, the place that still remembered being fifteen and moved like contraband from one house to another to escape a debt marriage. But Majed shifted outside. The screwdriver behind his ear glinted in the light. Household came first. Household always.

"I didn't come to fight," Jamila said quietly.

"No," Halima said. "You came in time."

Jamila absorbed that without showing where it landed. She touched Nura's shoulder once, lightly, because she was not all caution yet. "Put alum on it," she said. "Not just water."

"We know."

"I know you know."

She left. Majed stepped aside to let her pass, then followed without meeting Halima's eyes. The gate rope rasped shut behind them.

Nura stared at the doorway a moment longer than the absence required.

"You going to tell me she was only being practical?" she asked.

Halima folded the bloody cloth and set it in the basin. "She was being exactly herself."

"That's a pretty way to say coward."

"Pretty has nothing to do with it. Those boys borrowed their nerve from a chairman."

Nura touched her lip and winced. "Then what is she?"

Halima looked at the empty threshold where Jamila had stood holding her packet to her own stomach as if papers could be injured there and nowhere else. "Fed by the same fire as the rest of us."

By late afternoon the house had been put back into enough shape to fool a passerby. The trunk was shut again, the cloths restacked, the worst dirt brushed aside. Halima re-folded cloths with hands that wanted to shake. She brushed dirt from the certificates and laid them under the least warped part of the trunk lid. She pinned the torn reed mat aside and told herself she would mend it at dusk, knowing she would not. The notebook remained hidden against her body until the heat of it became intolerable. Then she rewrapped it in another cloth, tied it tight, and shoved it under the rice bin for the moment. She still did not count the copy pages. Her head felt full of metal filings. Counting required a steadier mind.

When the cloth in the basin dried stiff with Nura's blood, she took the small brass bowl and went to Sufia's stall for alum.

The path to the ghat had not changed. The sameness hurt most. The same duck droppings dried in the lane. The same fish scales glittered near the racks. The same boys slapped cards onto an upturned crate. But the people in it had adjusted themselves around her by some quick communal intelligence that needed no meeting and no notice on the school wall. Conversations narrowed as she approached. Eyes moved, then moved back too late to pretend they had not moved.

Sufia was at the stall cutting lime with the little black-handled knife she used for everything from guava to betel nut. By afternoon her bad knee always stiffened, and she had the habit of leaning her hip against the counter between customers, one foot angled out to spare the joint. Her lips were red from betel, but not freshly. She had been talking before Halima came within earshot. Now she was not.

Kashem sat on the bench with one knee up, heel planted, chipped tooth showing where his lip had caught on it as he worried the inside of his mouth. He looked like a man who had come for tea and stayed for injury. Beside him were two others from the river, cups in hand, bodies turned the lazy way men turned when they wanted a thing to look accidental.

Sufia said, too evenly, "Tea's gone bitter. Want some anyway?"

"Alum," Halima said. "A lump if you've got it."

Sufia reached under the counter. Glass jars clicked. The men around Kashem held their tongues. The silence was worse than open insult for the length of one breath.

Then Kashem said, not to Halima and very much to her, "I used to think the old stories were mercy."

One of the men beside him muttered, "Leave it."

Kashem ignored him. He had the fisherman habit of talking in circles until the hook set. "You know. A name bent here, a date slid there. We all said it. Dai-ma knows how people survive. Dai-ma knows where a child belongs if the paper doesn't. Fine. Fine. That's what I told myself."

Sufia put the alum on the counter. She did not slide it toward Halima.

Kashem laughed once. It carried no pleasure in it. "Now look. A hidden book under the blouse like smuggled money. Boys pulling it out in daylight. So what was it, then? Mercy? Or business?"

Halima put coins on the counter one by one because her fingers needed something smaller than his face.

"Mind your tongue," Sufia said, but the words came late and weak, like a lid set on a boiling pot after the milk had already run over.

Kashem turned then. His eyes were bloodshot, whether from the river wind or shame or lack of sleep Halima could not tell. "No, let me ask plain. How much did a father cost in your house, Halima-dai? A fish basket? Two witnesses? A little cash tucked in the ledger?"

The bench men did not laugh. Their stillness made it worse. Laughter still left room for embarrassment. Their stillness meant the question had already become thinkable.

Halima picked up the alum.

"If money were what made fathers," she said, "half the men on this char would have bought themselves better children."

One of the bench men let out air through his nose. Kashem's face tightened, not because he had been defeated but because she had denied him the full public fight he wanted. His pride needed volume. She gave him a smaller wound.

"There," he said. "Hear that? Still talking like she's above the rest of us. Not a midwife now. A writer for hire."

Nobody reached for his tea. One man rolled his glass against the plank until it clicked. Another kept his eyes on Halima's face, trying the name on her there. Writer for hire was a thin insult; cruelty on the char usually came plainer. The words paid forger came from the man beside him a second later, almost thoughtful, as if he were testing how they balanced together.

"That's the trouble with a paid forger," he said. "You don't know when she was helping and when she was selling."

The bench men let it stand.

Sufia finally slid the alum closer. Her fingers were quick as ever, tea-stall fingers, good at handling heat without drama. "Take it," she said to Halima. "It melts if you leave it open."

Ordinary detail came first. Sufia always led that way.

Halima took the lump and the change due back to her and left without tea.

The sun dropped redder toward the river. Smoke from cooking fires thickened low across the lane. Somewhere a baby started its hungry evening cry, stopped, started again. She walked home carrying the alum and the new title with equal care because both would sting on contact.

At her own gate she paused. From two houses down came the slow animal sound of a young woman trying not to moan through the start of real pain. It was Nasima, on her first baby, narrow-hipped enough that Halima had been watching her belly ride low for a week. Usually the first strong pains brought a child running before the second wave finished. Miracle had nothing to do with it. Halima was close, and people trusted the distance between her hand and disaster.

She waited without meaning to wait.

Inside, Nura shifted on the mat and called, "Did you get it?"

Halima said, "Yes."

The moan came again from Nasima's yard. Then the slap of bare feet on packed earth, quick and small, heading down the lane.

The footsteps passed Halima's door without slowing.
Behind her, the loft shelf still hung open where the bamboo had ripped the reed mat down.
The air had gone metallic, the way it did before night rain came hard off the river.

At the lane turn a boy's voice rose, carrying clearly in the evening thinness.

"Go get Rokeya-khala. Not Halima. Ma said not her."

Halima stood with the alum in her palm until the damp from it started eating into her skin.


# Chapter 8

Nura had already dragged the charpoy into the narrow stripe of gray light and was laying documents across the rope weave one by one by the time Halima crouched beside her. Overnight the floor had gone from packed earth to black paste. The men who had searched the house had left trunks open, baskets overturned, and the loft shelf half torn from the wall; night rain had used those new gaps before the room had time to cool. Mud sat in the heel dents of strangers. A brass plate leaned against a bed leg, furred with silt. One of Halima's clean birth cloths had slid into the doorway and drunk up the runoff until it looked like something used on a slaughter day. The house smelled of wet tin, mildew, old lamp smoke, and the sour paper smell that rose when ink started giving up.

Her split lip had dried into a dark seam at the corner of her mouth. When she bent, Halima could see the small burn mark on her wrist flash pale against the wet brown of her skin, a little moon she had carried since she was thirteen and tried to lift the rice pot barehanded before it was off the stove.

"Not there," Halima said. "If the sun hits too hard, the paper will buckle."

Nura did not look up. "If the sun comes at all, we'll be lucky."

Her voice was flat from bad sleep and from holding pain in the mouth. She moved a marriage paper from the charpoy to an upturned tin trunk, then changed her mind and moved it back. The seal on it had already feathered at the edges. A yellowed land-tax receipt lay beside it, the numbers swelling loose from the page, ready to drift toward any drier house.

Halima crouched slowly. Her back had stiffened in the damp. Every time she lowered herself she felt the old work in it, thirty years of crouching between women's knees, of bracing one shoulder against mud walls while babies came ugly or sideways or too fast. She picked up the tax receipt with two fingers under it, the way she would have lifted a premature infant out of soaked cloth, and slid a dry newspaper scrap beneath. The paper shivered. A corner tore anyway.

"Easy," Nura said.

"I know what's easy."

"Do you?"

The girl kept sorting. The quarrel had gone quiet and settled into the drag on every useful word. Halima heard the accusation under it: you knew men were coming, you knew rumor was thickening, you still sent me to the doorway with my hands and my mouth and no protection stronger than your name. There was no answer she could give that did not sound either false or small.

Water still dropped through the loosened tin seam over the storage corner. Nura had set a dented aluminum pot there to catch it, and each drop struck with a hard tick that made the room feel tighter. Outside, the char had the washed-out quiet that came after a storm heavy enough to flatten arguments for a few hours. No children yelled and no goat bells cut through it, only a cough from somewhere beyond the lane, the creak of wet bamboo, and the river talking low under everything.

On the mat by the wall lay the night's damage in little ruined neighborhoods: Nura's school certificate packet under a brass bowl, three witness statements curled into tubes, two vaccination cards clotted together, a widow's affidavit that had stuck to a piece of old sari cloth and come away with threads embedded in the stamp. Halima recognized the households by folds and smells before she recognized the names. Jamila always wrapped her papers with dried neem leaves against insects. Majed had once brought a receipt with engine grease thumbed into the margin. Rahima's few scraps carried goat smell and the stale ash of her hut. The men who had come searching had not cared which life belonged to which bundle. Once a house was declared dirty, everything inside it became the same heap under a boot.

Nura picked up the certificate packet and glanced at Halima as if daring her to speak first.

"Open it," Halima said.

"I was going to."

"Then do it before the gum sets."

Nura slipped a fingernail under the damp flap. Her careful hands were what always undid Halima. Even in anger the girl handled paper the way people did when they knew a torn corner could mean a missed exam, a failed application, another year gone. Officials held files away from their bodies to save their shirts. Nura's care came from need. The packet opened with a wet sigh. The top certificate had blotched along the left side, but the name line still held.

"The serial number's there," Nura said. "The seal is ugly but maybe still visible."

"Maybe won't feed a clerk."

"Nothing feeds a clerk except another clerk."

The line would have been funny another week. Halima felt the shape of the laugh and let it die. Nura flattened the paper with the heel of her hand, then hissed when the cut in her lip split wider.

Halima reached automatically. "Let me see."

Nura leaned away so fast the certificate almost slipped from her hand. "It's fine."

"It's cracked open again."

"It's a lip, not a broken mast."

Halima let her hand fall. The skin around the girl's mouth had gone puffy in the night. There was dried blood on the edge of her chin that she had missed washing. Halima had seen worse injuries before first breakfast. She had also seen wounds that mattered because of who delivered them and who watched. This one had been made in her own doorway, by a man young enough to call her auntie if he wanted favor from her, and half the lane had seen it happen. The blow had finished quickly. The publicness kept it open.

She turned back to the papers because tasks were still the only shape care could take without begging permission. She spread old lungis near the wall where the air moved best. She pried apart a set of witness forms with the blunt side of a hairpin. She laid Nura's school papers under a cooking pot weighted with stones so they would dry flatter. All morning the house filled with the small sounds of salvage: paper lifted from paper, cloth wrung out, water tipped from bowls, Nura's short breath when a seal smeared, Halima's tongue clicking once in irritation and then stopping because irritation was a luxury for people whose damage was not already done.

By midmorning the light strengthened enough to show what was lost and what was only sick. Dawn had blurred the whole room into one ruin; this hour divided it. A land slip could be saved if the ink had stayed inside the fibers. A birth date could survive even when a father's name turned blue and soft. A certificate with one dry corner might still be copied later if they got it to the mainland before mildew took the rest. Hope returned in fragments, and fragments demanded judgment.

Nura held up a folded receipt with two fingertips. "Whose is this?"

Halima squinted. Her left eye clouded first in damp weather. "Turn it."

Nura turned it toward the doorway. "No, the name is gone."

"Let me smell it."

Nura made a face but passed it over. Halima brought the paper close. Fish oil, rust, and the faint medicinal stink of kerosene. "Majed's. He keeps his receipt tin near the engine parts."

"You can smell a mechanic through mud now."

"I can smell carelessness through mud. Write his name on the back while you still know it."

Nura found a pencil stub and did as told. The movement steadied them for a while. Halima named households from scraps. Nura labeled the backs in her neat school hand before the pages dried beyond question. A ration card with the edge chewed by mice. A widow's petition folded into eight exact squares. A copy of a national identity card blurred so badly the face looked drowned. Each item came with a body attached inside Halima's memory: who had sweated over it, who had hidden it in a rice jar, who had begged her to hold it for a few days because their roof leaked worse than hers.

The labor calmed her until she went to the storage corner and saw the fallen basket of birth supplies.

The men had shoved their arms through it looking for hard covers and hidden compartments. Cotton rolls had burst and gone gray with floor water. The glass thermometer case had cracked under somebody's heel. A spool of clean cord had unravelled into mud like pale gut. Halima stood over it a moment too long. What rose first was the handling: male impatience, arms shoved deep, the quick certainty that nothing in a birth basket deserved reverence unless it could be turned into leverage. She bent and picked up the cord anyway. Mud slid along it. She washed what she could in a bowl and set it aside for household use only. After a floor like this, she would never bring it near a laboring woman again.

Nura was watching from the charpoy. "Leave that. We have enough ruined already."

"Ruined for one thing is not ruined for another."

"That's your answer for everything."

Halima wrung out the cord and hung it on a nail. "Because it's often true."

Nura stared at her a beat longer. Then she said, "They knew where to push."

Halima kept her back turned. "Everybody knows where a house will give if enough men lean."

"I don't mean the wall."

The tick from the rain pot marked the silence. Halima moved past it, went to the loft shelf, and reached behind the tin rice jar where the oilcloth bundle had been wedged after the search. She had hidden it there herself in the last minutes of confusion, when the house was full of strangers and Nura was shouting and some fool boy was laughing because he had found nothing but women's old blouses. The bundle was still there, heavier now from damp. Water had not soaked all the way through, but the cloth felt cold and wrong in her hands.

Nura got up at once. "You're checking it now?"

"Now is when the light is enough."

"Or now is when you finally believe they touched more than the trunks."

Halima set the bundle on the driest corner of the charpoy. The oilcloth had been wrapped and rewrapped over years until it remembered the shape of the notebook inside. She noticed the knot first. It was still tied, but the short end had been pulled on top instead of underneath. It was a small thing, stupid to any eye but hers. She always finished the knot with the long strip tucked flat because loose corners caught dust and made the packet bulkier. Now the corner stood up like a tongue.

Nura saw her pause. "What?"

"Someone opened it."

"You're sure?"

Halima looked at the knot again. "I didn't leave it like this."

Nura's jaw tightened. "One of them."

"Or in the dark after they left. Or when I moved it."

"You don't believe that."

No, she did not. The knot was only the first warning. The oilcloth had a new smear along the inside fold, the kind left by a wet thumb pressed in haste. The packet felt slightly thinner on one side. And the search had not been blind rummaging by hungry boys after gossip, no matter how much shouting and kicking had dressed it that way. There had been plenty of boot and spit and male fun in it, but someone in that room had also been looking with intention.

She untied the cloth.

The ledger lay inside, its black cover bloated at the corners, the stitching along the spine damp but holding. A few loose papers had been tucked around it to disguise the shape years ago, then kept there because on the char even disguises became storage. Nura leaned in over Halima's shoulder. Halima could feel the heat of her, the impatience, the old scent of coconut oil under rainwater.

The first pages of the notebook were safe. Oilcloth and thickness had done their work. Halima turned them with a thumbnail, checking the older entries: births from seasons when the river sat somewhere else, births in houses long gone, births whose fathers were dead, false, absent, or never written. Ink had feathered near the edges, but the dates still held. The names still held. Her own hand stared back at her from younger years, firmer then, less interrupted by pain in the knuckles.

Nura let out a breath Halima had not realized she was holding too. "The book's still here."

Halima kept turning. "If they've gutted the middle, the cover won't save us."

Halfway through the notebook she stopped. Between two thick original pages there should have been three thinner leaves folded twice and tucked deep, newer paper than the rest, copied clean because the old pages they echoed had gone faint with handling. She had not made those copies. Years ago she had found them already there after one of those bad monsoon stretches when too many women, helpers, and borrowed hands had been moving through the house to track every touch. Halima had kept them because they were accurate and because on the char nobody threw away a version of a truth that might one day need proving. The leaves were school-exercise paper, blue ruled, written in a careful hand trying hard to resemble formal script.

Now there were only the pressure marks where they had rested.

She checked again, slower. Turned back ten pages. Forward seven. Lifted the ledger and looked for the leaves stuck to the cover. Nothing. Only the original sheets, damp at the edges and clinging.

"What is it?" Nura said.

Halima did not answer at once. She set her thumb in one of the pale rectangular hollows and turned back to the original sheets the missing leaves had shadowed. The first still carried her old place marker at the top margin, blunt and brown with age: broken tamarind stump, east rise. Under it Shahnaz's breech-born girl was legible down to the birth month, but the father line had burst into a thumbprint of black where damp and handling had worried it thin. Two pages later another margin word remained clear enough to sting: shed strip. Aklima Bewa's note was split along the fold, late Karim written on one side, Harun, sister's boy, on the other, and the joining phrase between them eaten out so cleanly that only the pressure of vanished letters remained in the paper nap. Farther on, beside mosque-path east, Faruk's birth line still wore Halima's red pencil cross from the year she had to check twice whose courtyard he slept in. The copied leaf that had traveled with that page was gone too.

"There were pages with it," she said.

"Original pages?"

"No. Loose copies."

Nura frowned. "Of what?"

"Selected east-side entries."

Nura understood fast. Quick minds never let consequence wait. "You mean his side on the higher ground."

"Say his name."

Halima shut the notebook. "Names are cheap. Proof is expensive."

She opened the ledger again before Nura could answer. The original entries were still there, but age had thinned them almost to tissue. Shahnaz's father line had become that black bruise. The fold through Aklima Bewa's transfer turned greasy and near-transparent under Halima's nail. Faruk's note clung to the opposite page as if the damp wanted the mosque-path claim shut forever. The copied leaves had mattered because they carried those weak points in clean script: the month, the courtyard marker, the exact kin word that made a boy belong to one yard instead of another.

The disguise papers tucked around the ledger had barely shifted. A clinic referral still lay crooked over the back cover. A dead woman's vaccine card was still there. So was Halima's list of due dates from the last good season. Whoever reached into the bundle had left the useless scraps and taken only the entries that could travel.

Nura wiped fresh blood from her lip with the heel of her hand. "He spreads a story that you've been selling names. Men come in here and tear up the house. Then the only loose copies missing are the ones that make east ground easier to steal. How much more proof do you need before you speak?"

"If I speak in this lane now, it turns into me accusing a chairman because my own house smells bad."

"Your house smells bad because they put their feet through it."

"And whose word carries further today, mine or his?"

Nura's glance moved over the cracked thermometer case, the damp witness slips, and the cut that still kept opening at her mouth. "Then tell Aminul. He can at least put a date on it before Mofiz builds another story over the top."

Halima looked down at the ledger. "Tell him what. That pages I can't show him are missing from a book I shouldn't even admit was here."

"He already knows you've got things you have no business having."

"Knowing rumor isn't the same as receiving a charge."

Nura set the brass bowl down hard enough to make the wet certificates shiver. "That's always your road. Wait till the proof is clean, wait till the room is safer, wait till the man gives himself away. Other people do the paying while you wait."

The words crossed more than the charpoy between them. Saleha's marriage stood up behind them. Rahima's long silence stood up. Nura's own papers stood there too. Halima could have cut back if the girl had been wrong. Because she was not, the answer came slower.

"We don't open the door on only my mouth," Halima said. "But we don't sit here empty either."

Nura did not blink. "Then what do we do?"

"We write exactly what was taken before another hand or another rain gets to it. We keep it separate from the ledger. If Aminul asks, I can put something in front of him besides my temper."

A boy called from the lane, asking whether papers had survived. Nura turned her head and shouted that people could collect after asr prayer, not before. The boy went quiet at once. When she faced Halima again, some of the fury in her had tightened into purpose.

"Good," she said. "Then write."

Halima took an old school exercise book from the trunk bottom, tore out the least mold-marked pages, and sharpened the pencil stub with a razor blade. Her fingers protested. She ignored them. Near noon they ate cold rice mashed with salt and green chili over the charpoy without closing the ledger. The rice tasted of metal from the bowl. Then Halima bent over the page and began with the plainest line she knew: household name as publicly used, mother as bodily known, father as officially claimed, actual tie where relevant, child birth season, location marker.

She worked the eastern households in the order she had first attended them, because memory sat in her by labor and weather, not by plot line or office column. She began with Shahnaz's breech-born girl above the tamarind stump; that one she could rebuild nearly whole because the month, the mother, and the courtyard marker still held even if the father line sat there bruised black. Then she moved to Aklima Bewa behind the fertilizer shed and stopped. When she touched the fold, the page gave a soft tearing sigh. Late Karim on one side. Harun, sister's boy, on the other. Nothing whole between.

"What did the copy call him?" Nura asked.

"That's the part they wanted."

"You remember the house."

"I remember the house," Halima said. "I don't remember whether the clean line called him nephew, dependent, or simply in the courtyard. In a room full of men, those are three different truths."

Nura stared at the broken fold. "Then write the truth you can stand on."

So Halima wrote: Aklima Bewa, shed strip behind fertilizer shed; Harun, sister's boy, sleeping in Karim's yard since cholera year; linking phrase lost from original, copy missing. She hated the note as soon as it landed. It looked weak because it was weak. She ended on Faruk by the mosque path, the cowlick boy Mofiz liked to count as Sheikh blood when visitors came. That page still carried Halima's red cross and the house marker. It did not carry the clean proof that had once made the claim portable. Whenever her hand cramped, Nura took the page and copied the entries into her neat school script, leaving a hard blank in the Aklima line and a margin note: kin word lost. By the time the first sheet filled, they had two versions of the theft and one wound they could not sew closed.

Nura read over her own fresh lines. "He doesn't want the whole ledger. He wants the parts that can stand up without the rot showing."

Halima nodded. "The whole book would drown him. Now the shed-strip line is back to mush and my mouth."

By late afternoon the house had the look of a body stripped after surgery, still ugly, still smelling of wet things, but no longer helplessly open. The trunks had been shut. The driest papers were rewrapped in separate cloths with charcoal packets and neem leaves where possible. The unsalvageable scraps had been stacked in one corner for burning later, though Halima hated to think of fire touching names at all. Nura's school certificate packet sat beneath the brass bowl with a brick on top. Majed's receipt and two witness slips had survived. A widow's affidavit was beyond saving. Half a vaccination card had become nothing more than the ghost of a seal.

The ledger went back into oilcloth, but not with the new pages. Halima retied the knot flat and tight, then slid the rougher reconstruction under the lamp base where even floodwater would have to rise high to reach. Nura folded the cleaner copy inside her school certificate packet between the least damaged papers and set the brass bowl back over it. Another search could still hurt them. Even untouched, the cleaner copy still carried a hole where Aklima Bewa's claim should have joined to late Karim's house.

Nura watched her hands. "If they come again?"

"They'll come for the book."

"And?"

"And they'll leave without the part I can least afford to lose."

Nura touched the side of the packet with one careful finger, almost reverent, almost angry. "I should've bitten that bastard harder."

Halima looked at the seam in the girl's lip, the dried blood she had finally cleaned off, and the steadiness she had rebuilt by force over the day. "Next time," she said, "save your teeth for when it changes something."

Nura snorted. "You saying this changes something?"

"It changes what they can take," Halima said. "Not what the damp already ate."

The girl's eyes met hers then, level at last, neither soft nor healed. It was the first truly level look between them since the search. Halima accepted it the way she accepted a dry corner on a wet certificate: small mercy, still usable.

Evening came early inside the damp house. The tin roof held the day's heat and gave it back slowly. From far off, toward the ghat, a ferry engine coughed and caught. Someone in the lane began reciting prayer in a hoarse voice. Piety had little to do with it. After a night when walls and weather had both gone loose, routine felt like protection. Halima sat for a moment with the ledger hidden again and the new copy hidden elsewhere, listening to the house settle around that division.

The injury had edges now, and one of them was sharp enough to name. If Mofiz reached Aminul first, he could choose the missing kin word on Aklima Bewa's shed-strip line and make the whole claim sound cleaner than it had any right to be. Their new pages could prove the hunt. They could not prove that broken join. That was why the first ferry mattered.

Then she called to Nura to start letting people collect what had survived. There would be questions. There would be faces measuring her while they took back their damp lives. She was not ready for accusation, and she had no interest in defense before she had something firmer to set on the mat.

When the first knock came on the post, Halima put her palm flat on the charpoy rope and felt how much water still lived inside the house. Behind her Nura had already begun repacking the market bag with the papers that had to travel dry. Then Halima stood, wiped her hand on her sari, and went to answer it thinking of east-side names, of the copy under the lamp, of the cleaner version lying inside Nura's school packet where a greedy hand would have to choose differently to reach it, and of the hole still sitting in Aklima Bewa's line like a tooth knocked out, because if Aminul was going to hear this from her he had to hear the gap before Mofiz named it for him.


# Chapter 9

Before first light the ghat was a long black shine of mud, scored with heel marks and the thin drag lines of baskets. The river had dropped a little in the night, enough to show the slick ribs of the bank. Halima planted her feet carefully and felt the clay try to keep one sandal. Aminul stood on the higher plank in a shirt so clean it seemed unreasonable in that hour, two file folders tucked under his arm and held away from his body as if sweat itself were a procedural error. Nura had packed and repacked their papers until the market bag bulged into hard corners: the school packet with the cleaner copy tucked inside, certificate packet, copies of copies, a cheap pen, safety pins, stale muri wrapped in yesterday's newspaper. Under strain she always prepared as if preparation could shame fate into obedience.

"If the ferry leaves full, we lose the data room before noon," Aminul said. He checked his watch though the sky was still colorless. "After noon they start telling people to come back tomorrow."

"Then don't stand there talking to your watch," Halima said. "Get us on."

The boatman at the plank mouth had one ear folded flat from an old cut and a voice worn down by shouting over engines. He looked once at Halima's mud, once at Nura's bag, and then past them to the men carrying feed sacks.

"Office people first," he said.

Aminul showed his ID card. "They are with me."

The boatman did not argue. He only shifted his mouth to one side in a way that said the category had been stretched past fairness and lifted the rope. A man behind them muttered that char cases now travelled like ministers. Nura's head snapped half around.

"Say it louder if you want a reply," she said.

Halima caught her elbow before the girl could turn fully. "Save your mouth. We haven't reached the place that will deserve it."

They boarded sideways, balancing on damp bamboo slats dark with old algae. The ferry smelled of diesel, fish scales, and the sour damp cloth of bodies that had come out too early and eaten nothing. A goat was tied under one bench with its legs folded miserably beneath it. Two schoolboys in town uniforms looked at Nura's bag, then at her sandals, then looked away with the fast politeness of children already trained in rank. Halima sat on the edge of the bench, knees wide for balance, and kept one hand on the market bag because papers had a way of sliding toward water the moment a person trusted stillness.

When the engine finally caught, the char loosened behind them by degrees. On Kadam Char distance had always remained human-sized to her: whose cough carried from which yard, how long it took a laboring woman to begin bearing down, whether rain on the western edge would reach her roof in ten minutes or twenty. Out here the river widened into something that did not care where one household stopped and another began. The mainland rose ahead not like a shore but like a wall of brick, signboards, and cell towers, a whole fixed world that had room to ignore them.

Nura crouched over the bag and checked the packets again. "I labeled the cases," she said. "Kashem bhai. Jamila bibi. The widow line from south para. Ours."

Halima looked at the last word without looking at the paper. "You didn't have to say ours."

"It's ours."

"Keep your voice down," Aminul said quietly. "Inside the office, let me do the first talking."

"If they ask a stupid question, I'm not nodding like a cow."

"No one asked you to be a cow," he said. "I'm asking you not to start with a fight."

The courtesy in his tone was real. So was the line beneath it. He could get them through a gate, to a desk, into a room with a computer screen. He could not carry them once the room decided what they were.

The ferry landed in a rush of elbows. Men with sacks moved first, then women with children cinched to their hips, then the rest of the loose human cargo. Halima stepped onto the mainland road and felt its hardness come up through her knees. The road gave her no soft give underfoot and no char mud to read. It smelled of dust baked yesterday and woken again by the first traffic. Rickshaws rang bells, buses coughed black smoke, and a tea seller slapped wet cups onto a tray with such speed it sounded like irritation.

The upazila offices sat behind a low boundary wall, several concrete blocks added to one another without grace. Fluorescent tubes already burned in the windows although morning had fully arrived. The gate guard had a little silver butterfly hair clip fastened to his breast pocket where a pen should have been, probably forgotten there by a daughter dressing him in the dark. He looked Halima and Nura over in one trained sweep, from wet hems to cheap plastic bag.

"Token?" he asked.

Aminul gave his name and designation. The guard checked a list, found nothing, and shrugged.

"Go if you want. Records, second corridor. Wipe your feet first. The cleaner shouted enough yesterday."

He did not say who the cleaner had shouted at. He did not need to. On the concrete by the steps lay the half-moons of dried mud left by people like them, already turning to powder under other people's shoes.

Inside, the building held heat the wrong way. Not the dense damp heat of tin and cooking smoke, but a stale electric warmth that came from old wiring, crowded bodies, and windows that opened only enough to prove they could. The corridor was jammed before office hours had properly begun. Men in tucked shirts clutched stitched file covers. Women in cheap synthetic saris held children and envelopes at once. An old man slept sitting up with his forehead on a land-tax bundle tied in red string. Every bench carried somebody who had brought a whole household in paper form and was waiting to learn whether paper could bear the weight.

On one wall a printed notice was taped crooked under a government seal. Nura read it first, lips moving.

"Cases without complete documentary chain may be disposed without recommendation," she translated.

"Disposed," Halima repeated. "Like rotten fish."

Nobody at the desks was shouting. Tea cooled in cups while pages turned. The violence here had no need to raise its voice. A woman with a swollen ankle stood for twenty minutes while a clerk finished his tea and turned the newspaper sports page with care. A boy no older than sixteen was sent away because the photocopy of his father's card had been cut too close to the edge, as if one missing border line had removed the man himself. A peon carried stacks of files against his chest the way women on the char carried sleeping babies: with practiced indifference to the weight.

Aminul brought them first to the data desk, where a woman with blue carbon stains on her right thumb was eating fennel seeds one at a time from the lid of her tiffin. She did not look unfriendly. She looked used up in advance.

"Case batch from Kadam Char," Aminul said. "Cross-verification and exception review."

She held out her hand. "Code."

He gave her the batch number. Her fingers moved on the keyboard. A thin green wool sleeve covered her mouse hand although the room was hot enough to sour milk. She clicked, waited, clicked again.

"Mismatch, mismatch, mismatch," she said, not with drama, only with the flat rhythm of a woman sorting lentils and flicking out stones. "One father line not linking to national record. One occupancy chain with no inheritable mutation. One widow household with marriage trace absent. One female dependent attached under a deceased uncle. One late registration correction blocked."

Nura leaned in. "Blocked by what?"

The woman's eyes lifted at last. "By absence."

"Absence isn't a reason. It's the problem."

"Girl, the computer does not separate those two things for me."

Halima watched the screen from too far back to read more than the colors. Green meant accepted, red meant halt, and white boxes sat waiting to be fed some exact word that could not be replaced with another. She thought of the births she had attended in rooms with one hurricane lamp and six women giving instructions at once, the child sliding out regardless of whether anyone had found the clean cloth in time. Bodies entered the world untidily and still alive. Here life had to arrive already sorted.

"Show me the late correction one," Aminul said.

The clerk clicked again. Nura's shoulders tightened before the screen turned.

"Father identification unresolved," the woman read. "Linked enrollment inactive. Reverification required in person."

"Inactive how?" Nura asked.

"Inactive as in not available for your use."

"Dead?"

"Missing record trail. Could be dead. Could be migrated. Could be entered wrong ten years ago and never matched. If you want me to know which, you will need a different office and probably a different government."

The fennel seeds snapped softly between her teeth. She said it without cruelty, which somehow made it land harder. A cruel person could be fought. A tired one only kept moving.

Aminul asked for a printout and received a look that counted the cost of paper. Then he asked for the land linkage screens on the disputed households. The clerk printed three pages, held them up to dry because the toner streaked when the machine overheated, and pointed them toward the land records room with the side of her stained thumb.

"You can chase inheritance there if you like," she said. "But if the father line is rotten, don't expect the land to smell good."

The land records room was narrower and somehow more crowded, two ceiling fans chopping the air into hot layers. Metal shelves leaned under bound volumes the color of damp tea. A senior clerk sat with one slipper off, rubbing the cracked heel of his bare foot against the chair rung while he read Aminul's printouts.

"This one," he said, tapping Kashem's file, "shows possession through a father who can't legally anchor the holding. This one," a tap on Jamila's, "survives only if nobody opens the earlier household tree. This widow claim has no marriage trace.
