This female dependent has schooling records but no stable paternal chain. What do you want from me, officer-saheb? Inheritance where there is no father? Mutation where the dead were never entered?"

"I want the route when chain reconciliation fails," Aminul said.

The clerk gave a short laugh and put his slipper back on. "The route is rejection."

He said it as if he were naming a direction on a road, with the dead certainty of north or south.

Nura's chin came up. "People lived in those houses. They ate there, slept there, repaired the walls there. You can't reject a roof because your paper is stupid."

The man's face did not harden. It barely changed. "I can reject compensation. The roofs do the rest by themselves."

Halima put a hand flat against the counter before Nura could answer. The wood was sticky with old varnish and sweat. She could feel the girl's anger in the room like monsoon pressure, building against tin.

"Enough," Halima said. "Let him finish being useless properly."

The clerk actually looked at her then, perhaps because age had spoken in the register he recognized. He flipped through one more ledger, not out of generosity but habit.

"There's no clean inheritance correction here," he said. "If you want sympathy, go to court. If you want entry, bring me an order I can cite."

Aminul's grip tightened on the printouts. At last the man had given him a sentence he could use.

He took the pages, thanked the man with professional smoothness that had grown strained at the edges, and led them back into the corridor. Nura walked too fast for the space, nearly clipping shoulders. Halima could feel the building close around them with its whitewashed walls, its humming tube lights, its windows too high to look out of. On the char even a quarrel had sky above it. Here every refusal came boxed.

By eleven the bag felt heavier than when they had left home though nothing had been added except printouts and bad news. They had gone to births, to land, to general administration, to a photocopy stall outside the gate where a boy with a cough that never fully left his chest charged extra for enlargements nobody needed. Each desk recognized a different portion of a person. None recognized enough.

Nura came back from the photocopy stall flushed and furious. "They're charging for two copies where one would do because they know people don't know the form."

"That's not unique to this office," Aminul said.

"No. It's the whole damn road."

Halima took the papers from her before the girl's hands could crumple them. "Stop squeezing. The paper isn't insulting you. People are."

They found half a bench outside the land acquisition cell. Across from them a woman from another erosion belt was loosening the knot of her son's school tie with one hand and fanning a baby with the other. The baby wore a wool cap despite the heat, its edge yellowed with old milk. The mother had written phone numbers on the inside of her wrist in blue ink and was reading them over like prayer. Halima looked at her and understood, with a tiredness that went under the ribs, that the office had built a long trade in people arriving already stripped to need.

Aminul stood instead of sitting. When he was thinking hard he seemed to preserve formal posture the way other men clutched prayer beads. He had just turned toward the next room when two men at the doorway behind him began talking over their tea.

"Send the embankment batch under occupancy affidavit," one said. He was broad through the middle, his collar dark with sweat, and he blew on each sip before taking it as if the caution belonged to his character, not the tea. "Blood line won't settle before notice period."

"Only if the magistrate sits," the other said. "Otherwise we'll be accused of manufacturing households."

"Let the magistrate hear them. I won't."

Aminul stopped so suddenly Halima thought at first he had forgotten something. Then she saw the change in his face: not hope exactly, but the tightening of a man who has heard a usable word and is already afraid it will vanish if he reaches badly for it.

"Excuse me," he said to the sweating man. "Which circular?"

The man looked him over, measuring rank, district, inconvenience. "Special erosion verification. Household occupancy. Last year's revision. Why?"

"Because I need the authority text."

"Then ask administration."

"That's what I'm doing."

Something in Aminul's tone, still polite but no longer yielding, made the man set down his cup. He jerked his chin toward a side room stacked with broken chairs and bound files. "Rust clip file. Displacement exceptions. If nobody has stolen it."

Aminul turned to them. "Wait here."

"We're coming," Nura said.

"No. If that file exists, three people crowding the room will only make me slower."

"Then be fast."

He was gone before she could sharpen the sentence further. Halima lowered herself onto the bench and felt the concrete wall at her back, warm and damp through her blouse. Nura stood fuming beside her, one hand over the burn mark at her wrist the way she did when she had forgotten she was doing it.

"If this is another dead end," Nura said, "I'm going to say what I think of their categories."

"You already are."

"To you. That's kindness."

Halima looked at the corridor, at the patient misery laid out in packets and human bodies. "No. Kindness would be eating before you collapse."

They bought tea and two stale singaras from the stall outside the gate because it was cheaper than the canteen and faster than waiting to deserve lunch. Oil had gone old in the singaras. The potato filling tasted faintly sweet from onions fried too far. Nura swallowed hers in three bites anyway. Halima could not finish hers. The tea came in glass cups thin enough to burn the fingers.

"Say it plain," Nura said once Aminul rejoined them. He had a photocopy folded inside one of his folders and dust on the cuff of his otherwise careful shirt. "Did you find something or just another room to be insulted in?"

"Both," he said.

They moved away from the gate so others could press in. Beside the boundary wall a neem tree made a ragged strip of shade over parked motorbikes and one handcart loaded with tied files. Aminul unfolded the photocopy against the folder, weighing the curling corners with his thumbs.

"This isn't lineage correction," he said. "That matters. If blood line, marriage trace, and land chain won't reconcile, I can push disputed displacement households toward occupancy affidavit instead. One consolidated batch. A magistrate, not these desk clerks. Affidavit paper, whatever proof still breathes, witness lists, local verification. Enough to make the state admit who actually lived under one roof."

Halima said nothing. She watched his mouth, the careful shape of the words. Lived occupancy was the phrase on his tongue, a government label trying to pretend it had discovered sleeping, eating, nursing, waiting through fever under one roof.

"And to do that," Nura said, "who gets to challenge it?"

Aminul met her eyes. "Anyone with standing. Neighbors. Other claimants. Local men who only remember kinship when money starts moving. The route is possible because it's public. That's also what makes it ugly."

Nura let out a short breath that was almost a laugh and had no humor in it. "So to prove a house, people have to stand up and let the whole char paw through their insides."

"That's one way to say it."

"Is it the accurate way?"

He folded the photocopy once more, then opened it again. The movement betrayed him more than his voice did. "Accuracy depends on how cruel the hearing becomes."

Halima took the paper from him. The Bangla was formal and cramped. She could pick out headings, seals, numbered clauses, but not their full machinery. Even so she could feel the shape of the thing. It offered no mercy; it was a gate cut into a wall by people who preferred walls.

"If a woman has lived ten years in her brother-in-law's house because her husband vanished," she said, still looking at the page, "what does this route ask her to say?"

"It asks her to say where she has lived, for how long, under whose care, with which dependents."

"In front of men."

"Most likely, yes."

"And if a child's father on paper is not the man who fed him?"

"Then if nobody objects, perhaps the hearing stays at occupancy. If someone objects, the history underneath may come out."

"May," Nura said.

Aminul's expression hardened a fraction. "I'm not going to lie to make this sound gentle."

The neem shade shifted. A bus took the corner too fast and scattered dust over the parked motorbikes. Halima held the photocopy with both hands and saw, not the clauses themselves, but the faces that would be made to stand beneath them. Jamila with her composed mouth going thinner and thinner as neighbors named the woman who had birthed her and the aunt who had papered her into safety. Rahima from the western edge, shawl smoke-dark and cracked heels white with dust, having to say milk and blood aloud before men who had never once asked whether she had eaten. Kashem hearing it. Nura beside her. Her own daughter dead for years and not done exacting payment.

"How much?" Halima asked.

Aminul understood the question immediately. "Stamped affidavit paper. Photographs. Copies. Travel. Workdays. If district refuses a camp, repeat trips until people start borrowing just to keep a file alive. More than many can spare."

"And if a household can't pay?"

"Then they borrow. Or they drop out. That's true of every route in this building."

He did not soften it. She respected him for that and resented him in the same beat.

"Can it work?" she asked.

"Yes."

Nura leaned forward. "Can it work for us?"

He was silent long enough for the bus dust to settle. "Possible is the honest word," he said. "It isn't safe, and it isn't guaranteed."

"You say possible when you mean maybe no."

"I say possible when maybe yes would be irresponsible."

The answer was so exactly him that Halima almost smiled, not because it comforted her but because it was defensible. He had made a life inside offices by never promising more than paper could bear.

"What do you need from the char?" she asked.

"Enough disputed households to stand together. Witnesses who won't vanish when they're called. Someone on the char has to explain the route before rumor gets there first and turns it into straight poison."

"May again."

"Yes."

Nura looked from one of them to the other. "And if the char refuses because nobody wants their dirty linen counted in public?"

"Then the neat cases get through," Aminul said. "The rest remain disputed."

"You mean the men with fathers, land, and louder mouths."

"I mean the households the paperwork can recognize without being taught anything."

The line rode back with Halima through the rest of the building, clean as a stamp and just as blind. The char had survived by teaching itself forms of kinship no office had bothered to learn: sister's child as daughter, widow as second mother, dead aunt as legal mother, false father as shield, a man rubbed off one paper so a child could stay in school. Households patched from leftovers, pity, barter, shame, one woman's decision in a birth room that a baby would live better under a different name. The office did not deny any of it. Denial would have required looking up from the desk. It simply had no slot for them.

By the time Aminul brought them back inside to have the circular noted against the batch, the afternoon had already begun its slow closing. The carbon-thumb clerk read the reference number, raised her brows, and entered it without comment. A supervisor signed one margin with a pen that leaked blue onto his finger. Another desk stamped RECEIVED across Aminul's forwarding note so hard the table shuddered.

"Special review request," the supervisor said. He had a nicotine cough and spectacles repaired with white thread. "If district agrees."

"And if district doesn't?" Nura asked.

He was already reaching for the next file. "Then district does not."

Somewhere behind them another stamp came down. If not, then not.

They came out too late for the return ferry without sprinting the road, and there was no point sprinting because the road itself was clogged. Buses had begun to load for evening, men hanging from doors and calling destinations in bored singsong. The sky over the bus stand held a dirty gold light that made every wall look older. Aminul checked the last launch time, then shook his head.

"You'll have to stay tonight," he said. "There's a lodge near the stand. Not good, but usable."

"Usable," Nura said. "Another kind word."

"Would you prefer I call it comfortable and be caught lying twice in one day?"

That almost got her. Halima saw the corner of the girl's mouth move before she flattened it again.

They walked toward the stand through a lane crowded with tea stalls, tire shops, and photocopy signs bleaching in the sun. A butcher was hosing blood toward the drain with more success than the drain wanted. Men sat shoulder to shoulder on benches too narrow for dignity, discussing politics and diesel prices while one radio fought with another. The mainland's abundance was not abundance for them. It was only more places to spend money proving they existed.

At the lodge counter a boy with brilliant orange henna on his hair and a schoolbook open behind the register asked for names without looking up from his algebra. Aminul handled the talk, the rate, the deposit. Halima stood beside Nura in the smell of old bedding, soap, and the frying oil from the stall downstairs working its way through the stairwell. She could feel the day's road dust crusted at the back of her neck. Her calves ached from concrete. On the char, tiredness usually came with some completed labor in it: a child delivered, rice cleaned, a roof patched before rain. This tiredness had been purchased mostly by waiting to be measured by strangers.

When the key was finally slid across the counter, Aminul did not leave at once. He stood with one hand on his folder as if there were one more clause to add.

"I need you to hear tomorrow clearly," he said.

"We've been hearing it all day," Halima said.

"Tomorrow morning I either file the special review request or I leave those households to die at separate desks. If I file, Mofiz will hear by noon that disputed names are moving together instead of rotting one by one."

Nura crossed her arms. "You mean he'll start chewing on the witnesses."

"Witnesses first," Aminul said. "Then whichever file lets gossip run fastest. A girl's school packet in that batch will draw questions. Jamila's marriage papers will draw different ones. Rahima's claim will draw the oldest kind. Mofiz doesn't need the truth. He only needs the sore spots. Tonight is the last quiet you'll get before the char starts deciding who will stand up and who will hide."

Halima looked at him properly then. Sweat had finally reached his collar. Dust marked the edge of his cuffs. He was still holding the folders away from his body on instinct, though the battle to keep them immaculate had already been lost. He was useful, yes, and limited too, but he was not false.

"Bring it," she said.

Nura turned. "You decided that fast?"

Halima took the key from the counter. The brass was warm from another hand. "No. I decided it years ago, piece by piece, every time I put a child where paper could not kill them at once. Today only showed me the bill."

The boy at the desk looked up then, curious despite himself, and pushed his algebra notebook farther from the damp patch on the counter. He had ink on the side of his little finger and the soft mustache of a child hurrying toward manhood because wages required it. Halima wondered what household he belonged to on paper and what household actually waited for him after the shutters came down.

Aminul gave a small nod, whether in agreement or resignation she could not tell. "I've taken the room across the corridor," he said. "If there's word about an early bus, I'll knock. Tomorrow we talk again before the first bus. Rest if you can."

He left them to the stairs.

Upstairs, Halima did not unpack. She set the market bag against the cot and left her hand hooked through the plastic handles while Nura went still by the wall. Possible, Aminul had said. She could already see the other room: stamped affidavit paper going damp under elbows, Jamila holding her face still while neighbors named the woman who had borne her and the aunt who had signed her into safety, Kashem listening while other people opened his life like a fish on a block, Rahima walking in from the western edge with dust whitening her cracked heels to say aloud what milk and sleepless nights had said thirty-five years before. Nura would be in that room too, hearing names still being withheld from her. More households might stay on the list. The char would pay by hauling its hidden stitching into public light.

"So that's the road," the girl said.

Halima kept her hand where it was. "That's the road," she said. "Now we see who will walk on it once they know what it costs."


# Chapter 10

Now there was nowhere for the day to go. Upstairs, Halima kept one hand hooked through the plastic handles of the market bag until the plastic bit her fingers numb, then set the rolled bedding on the nearer cot and let her back unhook one knot at a time. Under her palm the iron frame gave a small answering tremor as another coach backed out of the bus stand below. The window glass rattled in its frame. The lodge room was above a tire shop and beside the stand, and the whole building kept shuddering under other people's departures, as if it were ready to climb down and leave with them. The plaster had once been painted a clean blue. Sweat, handprints, and years of damp had turned it the color of old dishwater. A fan with one bent blade churned the hot air without improving it.

The day had been all ferries, office benches, one clerk's face after another, Aminul's careful voice, Nura's silence stretched so tight it had begun to feel like a second strap cutting across the room. She had thought the waiting itself would save her. People on the mainland were always making them wait. A waiting day ate questions. A missed launch, a missing signature, a file sent upstairs and not returned, all of that could carry a person until evening without the person having to say the thing she meant not to say.

The office had shut. The last boat back had gone. Aminul was across the corridor because the cheaper rooms downstairs rented by the hour and he would not leave two women in those. That had been plain in the way he did not look at the desk clerk while he counted the notes out. He had only said, with that same office politeness, that he would knock if there was word about an early bus.

Nura stayed by the wall under the fan, her school packet clamped to her ribs against any hand that might reach in from the corridor and take even that. She had taken off her sandals and tucked them neatly beneath the cot. Mud had dried in fine gray flakes around the straps. Her face looked sharpened by the tube light, all edge and intent. The small burn mark on her wrist showed when she shifted the packet from one arm to the other. Halima had seen that wrist wrapped around a bamboo pole during the search, seen the lip split open under a stranger's hand, and even now the girl held herself like someone still braced for another shove.

"Put the papers down," Halima said. "Nothing's going to grow legs in here."

Nura did not move.

Halima took the aluminum lota to the corner basin, found a thin brown thread of water, and washed her face. The basin smelled of rust and harsh soap. Somebody before them had left hair caught around the drain. She wiped her neck with the end of her sari and listened to the bus stand below: conductors barking districts, engines coughing, a radio somewhere trying to sing over diesel. On the char, night sounds spread out: water, frogs, somebody's pan lid dropped in a yard three houses over. Here every sound hit another wall and came back meaner.

She turned. Nura was still standing there.

"Sit," Halima said. "You've been on your feet since before sunup."

"You too."

"Then let an old woman sit first."

Halima lowered herself to the cot. The springs complained under her. She reached for the food parcel Aminul had left, opened the newspaper wrap, and found two parathas gone leathery, a folded omelet, a wet pinch of salt in paper. Food had its own weight now: salt, oil, chewing, swallowing. All day she had been waiting for those simple tasks to build a wall. She tore a piece and held it out. Nura took nothing.

"Eat now," Halima said. "You can be angry after."

"I'm already angry."

Nura said it without blinking. Her thumb worried the split edge of the folder until the plastic gave a thin, ugly squeak.

Halima put the piece back in her own mouth because there was nowhere else to put it. The paratha tasted of old iron and cold oil. She chewed until it would go down.

"We have to go back tomorrow," she said. "You'll need your head clear."

"My head is clear enough."

"No. Right now it's hot."

"Don't do that."

Halima looked up. "Do what?"

"Talk to me like I'm a pot boiling over. I'm not some woman from the queue you can cool with water and send home."

There it was, not loud, not even rude in the plain market sense, but aimed. Nura came off the wall and sat on the far cot facing her. The packet stayed in her lap. The girl's fingers had found the split in the plastic folder and were rubbing it back and forth.

"At the office," Nura said, "when that clerk asked for the father's NID trail, you looked at the floor. When Aminul said there might be another route, you told me to wait. On the ferry, you told me to wait. Here too, wait. Wait for what? Morning? Another office? My hair turning white?"

"Lower your voice."

"Why? The wall will learn I don't know my own father? The wall probably knows more than I do by now."

A bus horn split the air below, long and ugly. Halima waited for it to pass. Nura did not. That was part of youth. Youth thought its own anger could outrun noise.

"Sleep," Halima said. "We'll talk in the morning when your mouth isn't running ahead of your ears."

Nura laughed once. There was no humor in it. "No. You don't get to put me to sleep and call it patience. You've done that all my life."

Halima's hand tightened on the food paper until grease soaked into her palm. She wanted to answer with a task, with a correction, with any of the sturdy little fences that had served her for years. She could fold the clothes, check the door bolt, count the money. None of them would hold. Nura sat bent over the folder, rubbing the split seam with her nail, while the bent fan knocked hot air from one cot to the other.

"You think I kept this because I enjoyed it?" she said.

"I think you kept it because it suited you."

That landed harder than Nura probably knew. Not because it was fully wrong. It landed because it held enough truth to hurt.

Halima set the food parcel between them and wiped her greasy hand on the underside of the cot where no one would see. "Your mother's name wasn't spoken in this house for nothing."

"Then speak it now."

The bus stand roared again, and for one wicked second Halima wished the sound would last forever.

It did not.

Nura leaned forward. "Start there. Not with the papers. With my mother."

Halima looked at the girl and saw, not Saleha's face exactly, but Saleha's way of fixing on a thing until the whole room had to admit it existed. She saw Saleha at fifteen, with the braid she could never keep tight and red thread between her teeth because she always bit the thread before knotting it. She saw Saleha laugh at a crooked roti and eat the worst-shaped one herself before anyone could tease her. She saw Saleha standing in the doorway during storm season, listening for boats because the river might call her by name one evening and she would answer.

Halima lowered her eyes to the greasy newspaper on the bed. It was easier to speak to that than to the living face across from her.

"Your mother liked things that didn't belong on the char," she said. "Glass bangles in colors too thin for work. Perfumed hair oil. Sandals with a strap that would have snapped the first week in mud. She was always watching the launch people, the market people, the men who came from outside with shirts still carrying soap smell by afternoon. I used to scold her for staring. She used to say I was jealous because I had married the river and couldn't leave it."

Nura's fingers stopped moving on the packet. She said nothing.

"Monir came with a dredging crew first," Halima said. "Not as a groom, not as anything respectable. Just another man following work. He had one tooth capped in silver and he kept touching it with his tongue when he was pleased with himself. He could lift an engine cover with one hand and make girls stupid with the other. Your mother wasn't the only girl who watched him. She was only the one I had to bury afterward."

Nura flinched with a quick, involuntary jerk, the kind a bed spring's jab could draw. Halima heard her own sentence and hated its hardness even while knowing softness would have been a lie.

"Did she marry him?" Nura asked.

Halima breathed in the room's damp lime smell, the cheap detergent baked out of old bedding, the corridor smoke seeping under the door. "Yes. No. Not in the clean way a clerk would mean. There was a mullah, two men from his side, one lantern that kept choking in the wind. He said he'd take her after Eid. He took her before. By the time I understood what had already happened between them, she had missed two bleedings and was swearing he would do right."

"And did he?"

Halima let the question sit. Nura's voice had gone smaller, which was worse than shouting.

"He did what that sort always does," she said. "He stayed till staying pinched him, then he was gone."

Nura looked away at last, not in surrender but so she could think without showing it. The window carried up a gush of frying oil from the stall below. Somewhere in the corridor a child started crying and was hushed by a slap of adult irritation against the wall.

"You let her go with him," Nura said.

"I did."

"Why?"

There was no answer that did not shame somebody dead. Halima chose the one that shamed herself first.

"Because I thought a bad marriage with a named husband would keep her safer than being a girl people could point at. Because gossip fattens faster than fish in a closed pond. Because I had delivered half the island's babies and still couldn't stop my own daughter from believing a man with oil in his hair and lies in his teeth. Because she begged me. Because I was tired. Pick one and then pick all."

Nura gave a short, angry breath. "That's not an answer."

"It's the only one there is."

The fan clacked overhead. Halima's knees were beginning to ache from the angle of the cot, but she did not shift. If she moved now it would look like retreat.

"He took her to the other char for a while," she said. "Then back and forth. Work there, work here, never enough money, always another excuse. When she started swelling with you, he told her not to make her face tragic over a normal thing. He said first babies make women foolish. He said there was time."

The old room in Halima's head opened the way bad rooms always opened: all at once, complete, with smell first. She smelled wet mat, kerosene, blood turned sweet at the edge where fever had already touched it. She had spent years keeping that door shut. The bus stand, the lodge, Nura's straight-backed waiting, all of it had shoved it open.

"When the pains began, he wasn't there," Halima said. "He had gone to look for work or drink tea or chase some next day's lie. Saleha was at his sister's place. They sent for me late because the baby was coming wrong and they were ashamed to admit they had waited. I found her on a jute mat soaked through to the floor. She had bitten her own lower lip nearly open. Every time a pain took her she clawed at the bamboo post beside her, and the post was slick where her hand kept slipping."

Nura's hand had crept from the packet to the cot frame. Her fingers were curled around one rusted bar. Halima watched them whiten and went on.

"I turned you inside her with my hand. There was no room, not enough oil, not enough anything. She screamed at me to cut her open. I told her to push and hated myself while I said it. You came blue, then angry. I slapped your feet and you filled that room with your voice. I thought for one foolish breath that maybe the worst had passed."

The corridor child had gone quiet. Even the buses seemed further away. Halima could hear the fan's bad bearing grinding, a small stubborn metal complaint.

"It hadn't passed," she said. "The afterbirth didn't come clean. It came in rotten strips. She kept bleeding. I packed cloth. I pressed. I got Rahima's old iron key hot and laid it against the cord stump because somebody in that room believed heat could frighten death away from a newborn. Your mother looked at me like she was already far and still trying to be polite about it. By dawn the bleeding had slowed and the fever had started. Her breasts had filled with milk by the second night. She was burning and shivering both. You were rooting at her blouse while I was trying to keep her mouth wet enough to swallow."

Nura shut her eyes. She did it tightly, not theatrically, like a person bracing under thrown grit.

"Stop if you're only doing this to punish me."

Halima's mouth went dry. "You asked."

"I asked for the truth, not..." Nura groped for the room, for the word, and found instead the plastic packet in her lap, crinkling it until the sound scratched the air. "Not that."

"That's the truth," Halima said. "Blood on the mat, milk on her blouse. I don't have a cleaner one."

Nura opened her eyes again, shining with anger more than tears. "And he?"

"Gone."

"Just gone?"

"When I sent a boy for him, first nobody knew where he'd gone. Then suddenly everybody did. That's how it goes with men like that. Once a woman starts dying, the whole bazaar remembers seeing his back."

Nura swallowed. "So Monir is real."

"Real enough to leave debts."

"Was he my father?"

There it was. It was not the paper question. It was the body question, the one with no stamp box beside it.

Halima looked at the blue wall because she could not look at Saleha's daughter while answering about Saleha's bed.

"I know he lay with her," she said. "I know she used his name when she still believed a name meant a man would stay. I know nobody else came forward to claim you. If you're asking whether I can swear by Allah and cord blood and my own old hands that no other man touched her in those months, no. I can't give you that kind of clean line. She was married in mouth more than on paper, abandoned in fact before she was buried, and you were left in my arms screaming. That's the straightest version I've got."

Nura stared at her. The rusted bar in her hand clicked once when her grip tightened. "So you didn't know. And you wrote him anyway."

"I used his name," Halima said. "The rest I built."

"Built how?"

There was no point stopping now. She had carried the shame this far. She had to let it walk on its own feet.

"First for school. Before that, for survival. A girl without a father's line doesn't stay only a child in people's eyes. She becomes a question they think they can handle with their own hands. The schoolmaster asked whose daughter you were before he asked whether you could hold a slate. The mosque women asked whose blood had made you before they asked if you'd fed. Men started saying poor thing in that tone men use when they're measuring future use. I had already buried one girl because a man could come and go and leave only his smell behind. I wasn't going to leave you with a blank space where every bastard in the market could write his own answer."

Nura's face twisted. "So you gave me a lie."

"I gave you a father's name that got you through the school gate and kept worse mouths off you."

"And now it's that same name choking my file."

"Yes."

The word came out before Halima could trim it. It was better that way. Nura deserved at least that much clean edge.

"Do you know what it did to me," Nura said, "sitting there today while they said father like it was a thing somebody could reach behind a cupboard and pull out? Do you know how many forms I've copied for other people? Father's name, father's NID, father's village. Always a line, always a box. I wrote those boxes with my own hand for women older than you. I wrote mine too. And all this time you were watching me do it."

"I was watching you stay in school."

"You were watching me learn a false thing so well I could teach it."

That sentence went in under Halima's ribs. She had no defense against it except the one she was already tired of carrying.

"Yes," she said. "I was."

Silence settled between them, but it was not empty. It was crowded with Saleha's last room, with school forms, with the cheap lodge bedding, with all the fathers Halima had written into other people's lives and the one she had written into her own house.

At last Nura said, "Did you ever mean to tell me?"

Halima thought of all the later days she had spent like savings: after exams, after monsoon, after the next harvest, after Nura got into nursing training, after there was some version of the world in which truth did not arrive carrying a knife. She heard how cowardly that sounded even inside her own head.

"I meant to tell you after I'd secured something first," she said. "A seat in training. A room in the colony. A document nobody could snatch back. I wanted to hand you the truth with one other solid thing in the same hand."

"So that I'd forgive it?"

"So that it wouldn't only be loss."

Nura laughed again, and this time the sound broke halfway. She put the packet down at last, but only so she could press both hands to her forehead. The cot dipped. The mattress squealed under her weight. She sat that way long enough that Halima almost reached out. She did not. Touch could be greed as easily as comfort.

A knock came at the door, three quick taps, Aminul's knock because even his interruptions were arranged.

Neither of them answered.

He waited, then said through the wood, "It's me. I brought tea."

"Leave it," Halima said.

"Come in," Nura said over her.

The latch clicked. Aminul opened the door only wide enough to slip through. He had changed out of his pressed office shirt into a thin white vest under the same trousers, and without the shirt collar and tucked pen he looked younger and more tired at once. He held two cloudy glasses of tea and a packet of glucose biscuits. The pale band where his watch usually sat was still visible on his wrist. He took in the greasy food paper spread between the cots, Nura's wet face, Halima's hand flattened hard against the bedframe, and stopped with the biscuits still raised. Nura did not wipe her face. Halima's fingers had gone white against the bedframe, the tendons standing hard at her wrist.

He set the tea on the metal stool. One glass slopped over and ran a brown thread toward the rim. No one reached for it.

"Should I come back?" he asked.

"No," Nura said.

Halima said at the same time, "Later."

Nura did not look at her. "No. Say it now. If the father's line breaks, what happens to the file?"

Aminul rubbed the watch mark with his thumb and sat on the threshold instead of the cot, the biscuit packet balanced on one knee.

"For your house?" he said. "The office wants one version it can write once and hear back the same way. Same father, same village, same witnesses. If the answers split, the file gets marked disputed and shoved aside."

Tea slid over the lip of the glass and ticked onto the stool. Nura's mouth stayed open a beat too long before she swallowed.

"And the affidavit route?" Halima asked.

"We still push that," he said. "That's the lane for households blood papers won't carry. I haven't given that up."

Nura looked at him, testing whether he would flinch. "So the father's name that got me through school can now kick me out of training."

"It can," he said. "A training board will spot mismatch before it asks how the mismatch got there."

He pushed the biscuits toward the stool. "Eat something if you can. Empty stomach makes bad answers. Earliest bus is before dawn. I'll knock again."

Then he stood and went out, closing the door softly behind him.

For a while neither of them touched the tea. A skin formed on the top. The fan beat the heat around. Somewhere in the building a man coughed until it sounded like he might turn inside out.

Nura picked up one glass at last and drank it cold.

"Did my mother know he would leave?" she asked.

"Not at first."

"Did you?"

Halima thought of Monir's silver tooth, the way he smiled with only one side of his mouth when he wanted to seem humble. She thought of Saleha smoothing the cheap new sari he had brought, green with a border too bright for her skin, and defending him with the full stubbornness of a girl who thought being chosen and being cherished were the same thing.

"I knew he was light in the hand," she said. "I didn't know how light."

Nura nodded. She was not forgiving. She was sorting.

"Which char did he work from?" she asked.

Halima looked at her sharply.

"Don't start lying again," Nura said. "Not tonight."

Outside, another bus ground its gears and pulled away, the whole lodge giving a long tired shiver after it. Halima told her the name of the neighboring char, the one tied to the dredging men and engine crews, the one she had kept folded away inside her own head because names were bridges and she had not wanted Nura walking over this one.

Nura repeated the name once under her breath, then pulled the packet into her lap, slid a pencil stub from the fold, and wrote the char's name on the inside flap before snapping it shut again. The sound was small, but Halima heard the decision in it.


# Chapter 11

The mosque courtyard still held the smell of wet foreheads and old jute when Halima came through the side gate. Men were shaking out the prayer mats and rolling them against the wall, slapping dust and damp from the reed weave. The noon sun had shifted far enough that half the yard lay under the mosque's shadow and half stayed hard with light. Women did not usually stand in the middle of that space unless somebody was in labor or dying. Today they stood under the low eaves and along the bamboo fence, saris drawn close under their chins, as if shade itself could count as modesty.

They had come straight back from the mainland, and Halima had walked up from the ghat without going home first. The mainland dust was still on the hem of her sari. Nura came beside her carrying the blue plastic folder Aminul had stuffed with photocopies and notes. The inside flap was still marked in pencil with the neighboring char's name Halima had finally given her at the lodge. She held the folder flat and high away from her hip as though the papers might bruise. The burn mark on her wrist flashed each time the folder shifted. Aminul followed two steps behind them in his pressed shirt, the collar already gone dark at the neck, his own file gripped away from his body to protect it from sweat. Mofiz was waiting on the low platform by the mosque wall in a white panjabi too clean for the char and sandals that had not touched mud all morning. The dyed edges of his beard had run a little in the humidity, leaving a faint tea-colored stain at his jaw.

The assembly had been his idea, which was one more reason Halima distrusted it. He had sent a boy to the ghat before their boat had even tied up, saying the men needed to hear what the mainland office had offered. The men, as if the women whose names would be pinched, stretched, erased, or dragged into public were only rain on the roof, not the roof itself.

She had nearly refused. Then she had pictured the school wall with the list still wet-pasted on it, names straight and official and wrong, and she had come.

Kashem was already in the yard, broad shoulders filling his cheap checked shirt, laminated card tucked into the breast pocket as if it were a talisman instead of a problem. He kept talking too loudly to the men beside him, laughing after things that were not funny. The chipped edge of his front tooth showed every time his mouth opened. Jamila stood farther back with Majed near the women's side, her sari pinned sharp and even, one hand closed around a packet of folded papers inside a clear polythene sleeve. Majed's nails were black with engine grease that never washed out completely. Sufia leaned near the fence post with her bad leg set sideways and a betel tin in her palm, watching everything the way she watched a ferry unload: not for spectacle, for inventory.

Rahima was not there yet. That did not surprise Halima. The western edge was a longer walk, and Rahima's cracked heels did not cover distance fast.

Mofiz spread one hand, generous already. "Since Halima-dai and Officer-saheb have brought word from the upazila office, let us hear it properly. We have enough panic on this char. What we need now is a path with some dignity left in it."

There were murmurs at that, men liking the word because it sounded as if nothing ugly would be asked of them.

Aminul cleared his throat. He had the careful face he wore when every syllable might later be quoted back at him. "There is a possible route for households whose records don't reconcile in the ordinary way. It isn't an amnesty, and it isn't guaranteed. A magistrate can accept household-occupancy affidavits where blood line, land record, or marriage proof can't be made internally consistent."

Blank looks met the phrase. Halima could almost feel the jargon drop into the courtyard like a metal bowl and sit there.

Nura said, before anyone else could pretend to understand, "He means the roof can count if the papers won't."

Several heads turned to her, annoyed at being helped by a girl. Mofiz kept his gaze on the men by the platform and let Nura's voice hang there unanswered.

Halima stepped forward because if she let Aminul go on, the thing would sound harmless until it bit somebody. "It means this," she said. "If the father on the card is useless, the house may still count. If the land slip belongs to a man who's been in the grave ten years but the people under that roof are alive and have been eating from one pot, that may count. If a widow has been keeping a grandson, or a second wife's daughter, or an old aunt who can't stand without a wall, those people may count if witnesses say so in front of the magistrate."

For one long breath the courtyard only rustled. Women shifted their feet under wet hems. Somebody coughed. A child outside the fence started crying and was hushed.

Then a man Halima knew only as Babul the rice trader's younger brother called out, "May count for what?"

"For recognition as one household," Aminul said. "For compensation purposes. For relocation entitlement. Subject to review."

"Subject to review," Kashem repeated loudly, making the phrase sound like something spitty and rotten in his mouth. A few men laughed.

Mofiz raised a palm toward him. "Brother, let him finish."

But others had already started. Voices rose from different corners of the yard.

"Who gives witness?"

"In front of everybody?"

"Does the magistrate sit with the women too?"

"What if one witness lies?"

"What if three tell the truth and one man gets offended?"

"What if the house itself is disputed?"

The questions came fast, but they all bent the same way. Nobody wanted principle. They wanted to know where the knife would go in.

Aminul answered as carefully as a man stepping across rotten planks. "Live testimony would be required. The claimant, household witnesses, and supporting statements. The magistrate would need to hear enough factual consistency to recognize dependency and occupancy."

Jamila's voice came from the women's side, level enough to quiet two louder men. "Factual consistency about which facts?"

Jamila stayed in the shade. Only the muscles in her jaw moved, once, before she locked them still. A string of green bangles was wrapped in the end of her sari so they would not clink against each other. Halima had seen women muffle glass like that before funerals and before visits to moneylenders.

Mofiz smiled as if he welcomed the clarification. "Yes, let us keep the discussion practical."

Jamila held Halima's gaze and left Mofiz speaking into the side of her face. "If a woman has lived in one house for fifteen years under one name, but there was another name before that, which facts do you want in a magistrate's ear? The name she survives under, or the one that can still spoil her daughters?"

The yard shifted around those words. Men who pretended not to know what she meant suddenly became intent on the ground. Women leaned closer.

Majed spoke without raising his voice. He never tried to fill a room he distrusted. "And how long does this route take? We have boats to load, debts to carry, work to find. A clean line moves. A disputed line sits in an office till the paper yellows."

His face gave away almost nothing, but Halima saw the strain in the way he kept one thumb rubbing the ridge of a callus on his other palm, like a man checking a stripped bolt. With Majed, worry usually came out dressed as timetables, debts, and things that could break.

"A clean line for whom?" Nura said.

Halima felt every head turn again. She wanted to tell the girl to let the old ones rot in their own caution for one minute more. She bit it back. Nura had the folder pressed against her ribs so tightly the plastic bowed.

Majed answered her, courteous because discourtesy to a young woman in front of everyone would look like weakness. "For children who need a roof at the colony. For a household that doesn't want to die standing in a queue."

"And the people who don't fit your clean line?" Nura asked. "They die quietly somewhere more respectable?"

Mofiz cut in before Majed had to answer. "We're not talking about letting anybody die. We're talking about whether this char needs a second humiliation when the first one has already arrived by government order. The officer has brought an option. An option is not a command."

The words dignity and option loosened two men near the platform. Along the fence the women tightened instead. One young wife worried the knot in her scarf until something hard showed under the cloth.

She said, "Silence is also a command if only one kind of household can survive it."

Mofiz turned to her with a patient tilt of the head he used on petitioners too poor to fight back. "Dai-ma, with respect, silence also protects women. It protects daughters. It protects whatever little standing a family has left when the river is already at the door."

Sufia spat red juice neatly into the dust outside the fence. "Standing," she muttered. "That and fifty taka buys tea."

Enough people heard that a dry ripple moved through the yard.

Mofiz pretended not to.

Men near the platform were nodding now. Along the fence, women kept their eyes lowered and counted on their fingers or on the knots in their saris.

"Listen properly," she said. Her voice cut sharper than she intended, and the courtyard answered with quiet. "This route is not clean. It means saying who actually slept where, who paid for whose rice, who kept whose child through fever, who moved into which yard after a husband ran off, who has been eating in a house without a paper right to it. It means saying in front of a magistrate what this char has managed for years with its mouth shut."

The words landed harder than Aminul's. She could see it on faces all across the yard. A young wife with henna still faded at the edges of her palms dropped her gaze so fast it looked like a flinch. An old man squinted at the wall as if hoping the plaster would open and swallow him.

The young wife spoke then, surprising even herself. "My elder girl was married with enough trouble already. If I stand up and start telling who was under my roof before the wedding month counted out, what happens to the younger one?" She was Hasina from the south lane, Halima remembered now, a woman who sewed torn school uniforms at night and always kept a brass thimble knotted into the corner of her scarf so she would not misplace it. The thimble bulged there now like a small hard fruit. "Will the magistrate marry her too?"

Laughter never came. The question had too much bone in it.

Jamila said, "That is not cowardice."

"No," Halima said. "It isn't."

Jamila's shoulders dropped a fraction. The young wife with the hidden thimble lifted her head. A man by the wall opened his mouth, found nothing useful there, and spat into the dust instead.

Aminul adjusted the file under his arm. "You aren't under an obligation to pursue the affidavit route," he said. "But if a household's documentary chain is already unstable, remaining silent does not make the instability disappear. It only leaves the existing record to decide the matter."

"The existing record is what people have," Mofiz said smoothly. "That is not nothing. Let those with clean papers pass cleanly. Let the more complicated cases be handled with care, one by one, not by dragging half the char into a witness pit."

Kashem laughed again, but now there was no play left in it. "Complicated cases," he said. "Hear that? That's me. That's her. That's whoever makes the neat men uncomfortable."

He came out from where he had been standing and slapped the laminated card against his palm. The plastic crack carried. "This card has a father's name. The school list has another spelling. The land people tell me the homestead line doesn't sit right. Everybody's been calling me complicated since the table at the school. What I want to know is this: how many more years am I supposed to stand under a father that works on paper and nowhere else?"

The men around him shifted, some away, some closer. Kashem always liked an audience until the audience grew teeth. Today he seemed to need them anyway.

Mofiz said, still patient, "Brother Kashem, one man's agitation should not force every household to undress in public."

"One man?" Kashem barked. "You think it's one man? Half this char is stitched from somebody else's cloth. You know it. We all know it. You just want the stitches hidden because once people start showing seams, the big houses split too."

The shot struck closer than Kashem maybe meant. Mofiz's expression did not change, but a thickness entered his voice. "Be careful where your mouth goes."

"You first."

Halima saw the line just before it snapped. She had watched that exact narrowing in laboring women when the pain came sideways and took reason with it. She said, "Kashem."

He looked at her so fast it was almost a jerk. Hurt was uglier on a grown man than anger. It made his face boyish in the wrong places. "No, you talk now," he said. "You went to the mainland. You brought back this house route. Fine. Then say it plain. If the blood's wrong, do we say the blood's wrong or not?"

The courtyard had become a throat waiting to swallow whatever came next.

Before Halima could answer, a stir moved along the fence. People made room with the reluctance reserved for someone poor enough to embarrass them by existing. Rahima had come in through the side gap by the ablution tap. Her smoke-dark shawl was thrown over one shoulder, damp with sweat down the spine. She had a length of goat rope looped around her wrist, as if she had tied the animal and walked straight over without going home to change. One sandal strap had rubbed a blister raw, and she had slipped that foot half out so the cracked heel rode the leather edge.

She took her time. People who had already pushed her to the edge of things usually granted her that much.

"Say the house first," she said.

Her voice was not loud, but the yard had strained itself so tight that it carried.

Kashem turned toward her with open irritation. "Nobody asked you first."

"I heard that," Rahima said. "You don't have to ask. Your shouting reached the west side before I did."

A few men snorted despite themselves. Kashem flushed darker.

Rahima went on. "Say the house first. Say who ate there. Say who was kept there. Say who has been sleeping on a mat by the grain tin for six years because there was nowhere else to put him. Say which widow's hands fed a boy when the father's people wanted his labor more than his bones. Say which old woman pisses through the night and still gets turned so her hips don't rot. Blood can come after."

Rahima kept her eyes on the women when she said it, not on Kashem. Sufia's betel-stained hand went still. Kashem shifted as if she had stepped past him and taken the ground with her.

Kashem heard something else. He heard challenge. "Easy for you to say blood comes after," he snapped. "You already know yours."

Rahima's face changed so little that only Halima noticed it: the brief tightening at the mouth, like someone bracing a pot lid against boilover. "Knowing and being allowed to speak aren't the same thing."

That quieted him for one beat. Then pride dragged him forward again. "Fine. Then speak. Say it all. If this affidavit circus wants truth, let's have it. I'm sick of half-stories handed to me like leftover fish."

His use of the full form landed as deliberate, heavy. Halima knew it was not grammar. It was accusation put into shoes.

Rahima looked at him and then past him, to the women at the fence, to the men pretending not to need any of them. "I didn't come here to wash one man's face," she said. "I came because if this char clings to whatever papers already look respectable, the poor houses go first. The western edge goes first. The women who took in sisters' children, dead brothers' girls, second-wife sons, old mothers nobody wants on the colony count, we go first. Men with cards will still be men with cards. The rest of us will become weather."

Hasina nodded before she caught herself. Sufia stopped rolling betel leaf and just held it in her stained fingers. Jamila's grip tightened on her document sleeve.

Mofiz said, "Don't twist this into widows becoming weather."

Rahima turned her head toward him. "No. You only propose that we stay grateful while we disappear quietly."

The men nearest the platform stiffened at her tone. For a breath Halima thought one of them would tell Rahima to mind the mosque. Nobody did. Rahima was too old and too poor for easy scolding. To shame her would be to admit she had reached the level of a threat.

Aminul stepped in, flattening the file against his chest as if its corners could square the yard again. "Let us be precise," he said. "The affidavit route recognizes lived dependency where documentary proof fails. It's most useful in cases where existing legal documents either exclude actual household members or attach claimants to lines that can't be substantiated. That's the administrative value."

Nura muttered, "Listen to him wrapping a knife in cloth."

Halima almost told her not to be disrespectful. Instead she nearly smiled.

Majed looked from Rahima to Kashem to Halima and then back to the papers in Jamila's hand. He asked the question Halima had been expecting from him all along. "If we choose this route, can a witness story pull down a clean claim that's already standing?"

Jamila cut in before Aminul could answer. "Say it plain. If one house opens, do all the dirty winds start blowing through?"

The sound in the yard went thin. Jamila's polythene sleeve crackled in her fist. A woman by the fence tucked her papers deeper under her arm and took one step away from the women beside her.

Aminul waited a beat too long, and the answer showed its face before he gave it. "A competing statement can invite scrutiny," he said at last. "A magistrate would compare claims. Internal contradiction can affect credibility."

A hiss went through the yard. It was soft, mostly women, and far more frightened than any shout.

Jamila lifted her chin. Her voice came out level and polished, each word laid down as carefully as the folded papers in her fist. "Then I say this route is a fire. You may call it truth if you like, but it's still a fire. Some of us built our children's rooms out of whatever scrap would hold. If you set flame to every old wall because the magistrate wants a better map, don't call that courage afterward."

The words lodged in Halima's ribs because Jamila was not wrong. She had helped build some of those rooms. She had hammered paper over holes and called it shelter. Now she was standing in a mosque courtyard inviting people to tear the paper back.

Kashem pointed at Jamila with the card still in his hand. "Easy to talk about walls when you already have them."

Jamila looked at him at last, cool enough to cut. "Easy to demand truth when you think it will make you taller."

The men around him made the sound men made when a woman said something too accurate in public.

Kashem stepped once toward her and stopped when Majed moved, not dramatic, just enough. Majed's body said enough: not this.

The whole yard looked to Halima again. She hated that old feeling of authority when it came like this, after other people had already bled the room open and wanted her to make the wound lawful.

She thought of the bus-stand lodge, of Nura lying awake beside the wall, of Saleha's name finally said aloud between them after years of being stored like spoiled grain behind the teeth. She thought of how many lives on the char had been made by not saying the whole thing in the whole room. She thought too of the school wall and the notices and the office ink that would not care whether a silence had once been mercy.

When she spoke, the words came out bare of kindness.

"Jamila's right about the tearing," she said. "Rahima's right about who gets shoved off first. Don't stand there polishing the papers that already happen to shine. They put one child on the boat and leave another in the mud, then call the count settled."

The yard held still.

"If you want to ride the papers you've already got, say it straight," she went on. "Say whose mouth you can leave out. Say which widow can go knock on a brother's door with her plate. Say which girl keeps her marriage talk sweet and loses her rice. Say which boy watches a man board with the papers while he stays on the bank. Then say dignity if you've still got spit for it."

Her clouded eye watered in the heat, and she blinked it clear. "And if you want witnesses and roof-talk, say that straight too. Old filth will come up. Girls will hear what was done to keep them fed. Men will have to answer for children they kept at the edge of the mat. Some marriages won't hold. Some talks already half-promised will rot in the mouth. I'm not hawking a clean cure. There isn't one."

She heard the scrape of somebody's sandal against the mosque step. She heard a crow squalling from the tin roof. She heard her own breath, rougher than she liked.

"But I know what counts inside a house," she said. "The roof. The mat. The hand that kept buying rice when the papers wouldn't feed anybody. If the office will hear where a body was kept alive, I'll say the house before I say any father's name."

Mofiz smiled without warmth. "So Halima-dai now asks the char to divide itself in the mosque yard."

Halima kept her voice level. "The char was divided before I got here. Today you wanted names for the cut."

Kashem made a short, savage sound of agreement. "Then stop wrapping it. Father's name or the roof that kept you. Which one buys a place on the boat?"

From near the platform a man said, "And if my brother's widow says she ate from my pot, does she get my room too?"

From the fence a woman answered before anyone else could. "She cooked in that room three monsoons. What did you think kept her, your good name?"

Majed said, almost to himself, "That means a fight in every lane."

"One's already there," Nura said. "This just drags it into daylight."

Mofiz turned to the crowd and lowered his voice until people leaned toward him, as if he were bringing the heat down instead of banking it for later. "This heat is no place for drunken oaths. Go home. Speak inside your households. Those with clean documentary standing should not throw themselves into unnecessary scandal. Those who think they need this other route may consult further. We are not cattle to be sorted by shouting."

Rahima said, "Private is how we got here."

Mofiz spread his hands again. "And public is how we lose whatever little face remains."

The crowd broke then, not with resolution but with noise. Men started talking over one another, all of them suddenly urgent to sound reasonable. Women peeled off in knots, not leaving so much as carrying the argument with them into shade. Kashem stayed in the middle another moment, chest moving hard, then shoved his card back into his pocket and stalked toward the gate without looking at anyone. As he passed Rahima he slowed, not enough to call it stopping.

"If you've got something to say," he said to her, low but not low enough, "say it before the river takes the bank and saves you the trouble."

Rahima looked at him with a fatigue so old it seemed older than either of them. "You still think truth comes to serve you first," she said.

He flinched as if she had slapped him. Then he was gone.

Halima felt Nura move beside her. The girl said nothing. That restraint, more than any outburst, told Halima how tightly she was wound.

Women began approaching in twos and threes, not to make speeches now that the main crowd had started to spill out, but to ask the questions they could not ask in front of husbands or brothers. Hasina came first, twisting the corner of her scarf until the hidden thimble dug a square mark into her thumb.

"If I say my sister's daughter stayed with me those two years," she asked, "will they ask why?"

"Yes," Halima said.

Hasina swallowed. "And if I don't say it?"

"Then the girl doesn't exist for the claim."

Hasina nodded once, hard, and backed away like a person measuring pain before agreeing to it.

Sufia limped over next, her betel tin shut now. "You've made yourself party leader, dai-ma."

"I didn't ask for a party."

"Nobody does. They still get one." Sufia jerked her chin toward the tea stalls by the ghat, though they were too far to see. "By evening they'll have a name for this crack and swear they saw it first. Men love naming a split after they've spent years feeding it."

She said it almost lightly, but the lightness stopped at her mouth. Her eyes stayed fixed on the gate Kashem had used, then on the west-side path where Rahima stood alone, adjusting the goat rope on her wrist as though she had come merely to buy salt.

Jamila gathered her sleeve of papers and turned toward the eastern path. Majed hesitated, looking once toward Halima and once toward Mofiz, who was already drawing three men from the eastern high ground into a tight courteous circle. Jamila spoke to her husband without turning her head. "Not through my daughters' mouths." He went with her.

Aminul exhaled through his nose. "If this hardens into camps, the magistrate will hear every witness as a weapon."

"You say that as if it wasn't a weapon before," Halima said.

"Before, it wore official clothes," Aminul said.

Nura gave a hard little laugh. "Welcome to the char."

Aminul accepted that without answering. Sweat had bled a gray map across the back of his shirt. For the first time since the mainland trip he looked his age instead of his job.

By the time Halima and Nura left the courtyard, the argument had outrun the walls. Men clustered in the path under the tamarind stump where no tree stood anymore, talking in low urgent bursts about brothers' widows, daughters sent back from bad marriages, old mothers who slept in grain sheds and still had to be fed. At the tube-well a woman drawing water stopped mid-pump to ask another, "Will your husband's sister count then?" and the other woman said, "If she counts, my sons eat less," then drove the handle down hard enough to slop water over the pail. Down by the tea stalls, some fool had already compressed the whole afternoon into stupidity, calling one side the house page and the other the blood page.

They crossed the lane toward the school wall because Aminul wanted to collect himself before going back to his lodging space in the teachers' room. The posted list still hung there under a film of dust, names in straight official order. Someone had drawn a fingernail down one corner until the pasted paper curled away from the concrete like dead skin.

Nura set the folder on the school veranda ledge and opened it. The penciled char name on the inside flap showed for a moment before she pinned it under the exercise book. "If people are going to come," she said, "they need somewhere to come."

Her granddaughter's split lower lip darkened when she pressed it. She kept sorting the folder, flattening pages, lining the exercise book square to the ledge as if a cleaner surface might hold the afternoon still. After the mainland trip, any public talk of roofs and fathers had to be cutting her twice over. Still she pulled out a cheap exercise book and flattened it with both hands.

"Don't do it if you're doing it for me," Halima said.

"I'm not." Nura found the stub of a pencil tucked in the folder seam. "I'm doing it because if Mofiz gets the first line, he'll make this whole thing sound like decent people versus dirty kin, and then we're finished."

She drew a line down the page and wrote two headings in her small careful hand: NEEDS HOUSEHOLD WITNESS.

On the other side she paused, then wrote: STAYS ON CURRENT PAPERS.

"I hate both of them," she said.

"Write them anyway," Halima said. "The yard's uglier than the notebook."

Nura shot her a look, but she did not argue. Aminul watched with the expression of a man observing something irregular that would nevertheless have to be managed because no cleaner instrument existed.

The first person to step onto the veranda was not who Halima expected. It was old Kuddus Ali's daughter-in-law, Rizia, a narrow woman with a silver nose pin too big for her face and a habit of cracking roasted gram between her molars when she was thinking. She had three small sons and an aunt by marriage who slept in their grain shed during storms because no one had ever bothered to make room for her on the formal household sheet. Rizia stood there chewing nothing now, jaw working from habit alone.

"Put my name with the witnesses," she said. "If they leave old Amena off, then her medicine comes from where? Air?"

Nura wrote.

The second was Rahima.

She climbed the two broken school steps slowly, as if testing whether they would hold, and stood close enough that Halima could smell goat hair and stale smoke in her shawl. Up close the raw place on her heel looked worse, the skin peeled white at the edge of red.

"You know what they'll say if I stand witness," Rahima said.

"Yes," Halima said.

"They'll say I'm an old hungry woman trying to climb into somebody else's claim."

"Yes."

Rahima nodded, satisfied by the absence of comfort. "Write me there anyway."

Nura's pencil stopped for half a second above the page before she set Rahima's name under the first heading. She kept her eyes on the paper. The stillness in her was so complete it was almost visible.

Hasina came up the lane again before anyone else did, stopping one step short of the veranda. The thimble from her scarf was in her fist now instead of hidden at her neck.

"If I stand there," she asked, looking not at Halima but at Rahima's name on the page, "they'll ask about the months before my elder girl's wedding. All of it."

"Yes," Halima said.

Hasina pressed the thimble into her palm until the skin around her knuckles blanched. "Not today," she said, and turned away toward the west lane. She went home.

Across the schoolyard, near the path to the eastern ground, Mofiz had formed his own small queue without ever calling it one. Men stood with him holding document sleeves and tax receipts, their bodies arranged in a curve of caution around his white clothes. Jamila and Majed were there now. Majed held the papers. Jamila held herself.

Aminul said quietly, "If the magistrate asks later who encouraged which route, I'll have to answer truthfully."

"Then answer it," Halima said.

Across the yard, Mofiz tilted his head toward Jamila's sleeve. "You'd do better not to invite wind into a house that's still standing."

Jamila's answer carried because the yard had emptied enough for small sounds to travel. "My house isn't settled. It's only standing. Put us with the papers we have."

Majed handed Mofiz the sleeve.

Rahima stood at Halima's shoulder with the goat rope looped around her wrist. Nura's page lay open under Halima's palm, Rahima's name written first among the people willing to be witnessed into existence. Nura kept one hand on the blue folder, over the inside flap where the neighboring char's name sat in pencil. By dark the tea stalls would turn the whole schoolyard into house list and blood list. The names were stupid. The split was not. Across the yard, Jamila kept walking.


# Chapter 12

Nura left before Halima's first cough broke the dark.

Even in the dark she could still hear yesterday's schoolyard dividing itself: house list on one side, blood list on the other.

The house held that thin hour when even the tin roof seemed to sleep. The lantern had gone out long ago. What stayed was the smell of dried mud from yesterday's sandals, lamp smoke in the cloth hanging by the doorway, and the sour paper smell that never fully left the room now that certificates and copies had been spread, lifted, spread again. Nura stood barefoot by the shelf and packed as if she were going to an exam she had not been told the subject of. She took the folded slip with Monir's name on it, the copy of her secondary-school certificate because she did not trust herself not to need proof of her own existence once she began asking strangers questions, a little money from tutoring tied into the corner of her orna, half a paratha wrapped in yesterday's newspaper, and a pencil. She checked each item twice, then once more, not because the list was long but because fear made her practical before it made her wise.

Halima slept on her side facing the wall, one arm flung out of the sheet, palm open as if she had let go of something in a dream and had not yet realized it was gone. In sleep her face looked older and less defended. The clouded eye sat half-open. Nura had spent enough years beside that face to know that if she waited for permission, she would get either silence or a command to wait until after the magistrate, after the hearing, after this next disaster, after one more thing that needed enduring first. Her whole life had been an after. She took the key from the nail, lifted the door latch slowly so it would not click, and stepped into the gray before dawn without leaving a note.

At the ghat, Shamima was already there, squatting on her heels beside a stack of jute sacks and twisting the loose end of her scarf into a cord around her forefinger. She was Nura's cousin in the elastic char way, older by three or four years, married across one lane and two family arguments, and always in the middle of some repair that was not quite hers. That morning she carried a sewing-machine belt in her cloth bag because her husband's widowed sister on the neighboring char had found a secondhand machine and needed the belt before market day. She had agreed to take Nura because Nura had once filled out a clinic form for her son's chest medicine and because women on chars learned early that one errand was rarely worth a boat fare but two might be.

"If your grandmother asks me, I'm telling her you bullied me," Shamima said when Nura came down the bank.

Her voice was low, but the grin was real. She had one tooth capped in silver that caught what little light there was.

"Tell her you had your own work," Nura said.

"I do have my own work. I don't like mixing it with yours. Yours has fire in it."

The boatman, Mannan, spat into the water and told them to get in or stay home. He was a narrow-hipped man with a knitted skullcap he kept adjusting even when there was no wind, steering with one bare foot while both hands fussed with the engine rope. Nura stepped down carefully. The plank was wet and slimed with old algae. Shamima caught her elbow before she slipped and gave her a look that said she was already regretting this.

The channel between chars was not wide, but it had the authority of something that could still decide to take a person. The engine started on the third pull, coughing diesel over the river's stale-sweet smell. Water hyacinth drifted by in broken mats. Dawn widened, and Kadam Char pulled back into low shapes of tin and bamboo, the school wall a pale smudge, the mosque dome barely catching light. Nura kept her eyes on it until the bend took it away. Only then did she unfold the paper in her hand and look once more at the name she had copied the night before from her birth record. The name on it was Monir, just that, with a father's line that might have been as false as the rest.

Between what Halima had let slip in the bus-stand lodge and what Sufia had muttered later while pouring tea as if she did not care whether Nura heard or not, Nura had gotten three usable things: the name, the neighboring char, and dredger work. That was more than Halima had intended to give. It was enough for a morning's rebellion and maybe enough to kill a fantasy cleanly, which was more mercy than Nura had been offered so far.

Shamima watched the folded slip in Nura's fingers. "You still haven't told me what you'll do if you find him."

"Ask him some questions."

"You're crossing a river for some questions. You could ask questions at home and get shouted at free."

Nura looked at the current where the engine chewed it white. "At home they answer around the thing. I want the thing."

Shamima snorted, then tucked the sewing-machine belt deeper into her bag. "Men aren't the thing. They're the belt that snaps after you've already promised the blouse by market day."

Shamima pressed the newspaper around the belt with two fingers, checking for damp. Nura's mouth twitched at one corner, then flattened again. She rubbed the small burn mark on her wrist and kept her face turned toward the bend ahead.

The neighboring char woke noisier than Kadam Char. By the time their boat nudged into the mud, two boys were dragging a basket of duck feed up the bank, a woman was rinsing blackened tea glasses in a bucket gone gray with old sugar, and someone farther in was beating a rug against a bamboo rail with the rhythm of an argument. The ghat smelled of river slime, green chili frying in old oil, and the ammoniac edge of goat droppings warming under the first sun. A row of tea stalls had been made from tin, tarpaulin, and stubbornness. Men were already standing in lungis with their cups tilted from the saucers, talking about sand rates and fuel as if they had been at it all night.

Shamima jerked her chin toward the second stall. "Ask Momena. She knows who owes what before they know it themselves."

Momena was thick-wristed and quick, maybe forty, maybe older, with a faded orange shawl thrown over one shoulder and the top joint of her left ring finger missing as neatly as if a machine had bitten it off and then lost interest. She kept a carpenter's pencil tucked into the knot of her hair and wrote credit marks on the inside flap of a biscuit carton nailed beside the kettle. When Nura stepped up, Momena did not ask what she wanted. She looked once at Nura's face, once at the careful way she held her folded paper, and pushed a bench with her knee.

"Sit first. If you fall in this heat, I won't carry you."

Nura sat because refusal would have made her look younger.

"I'm looking for someone," she said.

"Everybody is. Tea?"

"No."

"Then you'll need a name that earns the trouble."

Nura unfolded the slip. "Monir. He worked here some years back. With dredger people, I heard."

Momena's hand stopped over the kettle handle. Her gaze moved over Nura with the quick inventory of a woman deciding which Monir, which debt, which ruin.

"You're late for him," she said. "Last story I heard, a propeller chewed him somewhere downriver."

Shamima made a noise through her nose. "And the story before that?"

"Southbound dredger. Before that, fever. Men die three ways before breakfast if they've left debt behind."

The dodge hit Nura harder than it should have. For one stupid second she saw herself going back across the water with nothing but stall talk and diesel on her clothes. Then fear did what it always did to her. It made her orderly. She pulled the certificate copy from her bag, opened it just enough to show the father line, and laid it on the bench between them.

"I'm not here for old tea debt," she said. "His name is in the father box. If there's a paper anywhere with his hand on it, tell me now so I don't waste more boat fare."

Momena's eyes dropped to the page. The carpenter's pencil in her hair tilted as she tipped her head.

"Paper," she said after a moment. "That's different."

She lifted the kettle, poured tea through a cloth strainer into two glasses anyway, and set one in front of Nura. "Drink. Your mouth's already gone dry. If it's that Monir, go behind the dead pond. Third house, blue drum outside. He slept there one flood season and ran his tab right down my carton: thirteen days' tea, four cigarettes he swore he'd pay after wages, one enamel cup. At Babul's pump shed he left a blue bag and kept bragging it had his voter slip in it. Don't start with government. Noorjahan will send you back into the lane. Start with the man."

Shamima took the second glass before Momena changed her mind. "Still writing everybody down?" she asked.

"What else would I do? If I stop marking, every man here starts calling old debt new tea."

She said it while marking something on the biscuit carton with the carpenter's pencil. A little boy came up to the stall carrying a hen upside down. Momena wiped his nose with the end of her shawl without breaking her stare from Nura.

"Go before Noorjahan leaves for the fish yard," she said. "And if she's skinning koi already, don't stand in the doorway talking like a police clerk. Sit and ask clean."

The path to Noorjahan's house cut past a pond that had dried to cracked green slime and mosquito skin, then through a strip of low huts where fish were laid on mats to salt. Dogs slept in the shade under bamboo racks. Women stepped around one another with basins on their hips, speaking in that fast half-shared shorthand Nura always heard among people who had too much to do and no time to turn themselves into speeches. One of them pointed with her chin when Shamima asked for Noorjahan, then called after them, "Tell her I kept the chilies inside. Rain's coming later." Another answered from a doorway that the sky said otherwise. They argued without stopping their hands.

Noorjahan's house had a blue plastic water drum outside and a line of school shirts drying from the eaves, all of them patched at the collar. She was sitting on a low wooden stool in the yard with a basin between her knees, scraping silver skin off tiny fish with the back of a spoon. The spoon must once have belonged to a better set. Its handle had flowers worn down to almost nothing. Noorjahan herself was not old, perhaps not even thirty, but hard use had taken the soft guesses off her face.
