Her hair was braided and coiled tight. One heel pressed the stool rung; the other foot kept time against the earth as she worked. A little girl beside her was trying to braid red thread into a doll made from rolled cloth and muttering instructions to it under her breath.

When Nura said Monir's name, Noorjahan did not drop the spoon or freeze or perform the shock of memory. She scraped one more fish clean, flicked the scales from her fingers, and only then looked up.

"Which Monir?" she asked.

Shamima, who had clearly decided she would rather finish this once than ferry Nura back half-satisfied, answered before Nura could trip over herself. "The dredger one. Thin. Used to drink tea at Momena's and sleep here sometimes."

"Then you've come to the wrong yard," Noorjahan said.

The little girl looked up. "Ma, is this about the man who stole the mug?"

"Go inside, Juthi."

"I'm braiding."

"Take the doll and braid inside."

Juthi scooped up the limp cloth doll and went, but slowly enough to hear more if she could. Noorjahan wiped her fish-slick hands on the edge of her sari. She still did not tell them to sit.

"I don't keep a clerk's shelf for men who passed through on bad weather," she said. "Momena likes stirring old pots she doesn't have to scrub."

The refusal made something inside Nura tighten. She could hear the scene tipping toward neighborhood quarrel, away from the one fact she had crossed for. She took the certificate copy from her bag again, opened the crease with her thumb, and held it low enough that only the women could see.

"I'm from Kadam Char," she said. "His name is on my birth record. I don't need a love story. I need to know whether he left a paper, a place, or a person who can still say he existed."

That changed Noorjahan's face, not into pity but into recognition. She nodded to the overturned bucket by the wall. "Sit. If this is going to be about papers, I don't want you fainting and making extra work."

Nura sat. The bucket flexed under her weight. Heat from the yard had already climbed into the plastic. She could smell fish blood, dish soap, and the cumin someone nearby had just thrown into hot oil.

"I needed to know if he's alive. If he has any papers. If..." She stopped. The sentence had run out into something she did not want to say in front of a stranger.

Noorjahan cut in before she had to finish it. "If he can stand up and call himself your father for the government. That kind of thing."

Nura hated how quickly her face warmed. "Something like that."

Noorjahan leaned back and let out a short breath through her nose. "Then you wasted your boat fare."

She pinched another fish by the tail and dragged the spoon down its side until scales stuck to her wrist.

"He stayed here one wet season," Noorjahan said. "Slept here when the rain got ugly. Worked near the dredger cut when there was work, disappeared when there wasn't. One day he had kin upriver. Next day he didn't. Every payday he was bringing sugar. You can hear how that ended. Fever took him down and I stood over a pot of coriander water while Momena let the tea run on credit. When the wages stalled he worked the lane house by house. Borrowed from me, from Momena, from Ruksana next door for kerosene, from anybody soft enough to unlatch a door. Last week he was here he made a show of handing Babul a blue bag to keep dry in the pump shed. Said there was a voter slip in it, two crew chits too, and if he lost them again the contractor would skin him. That's all of him I can swear to. The rest was appetite, lies, and one sickbed face."

Ruksana herself appeared in the doorway next door as if summoned by the sound of her name. She was broad across the hips, with a sunflower-printed housecoat under her sari and henna still clinging in orange ghosts to two fingernails. She had a baby balanced on one side, not her own by the look of how carelessly familiar the child's weight sat on her.

"He borrowed kerosene money off me and never brought the bottle back," she said. "Say that right if you're listing him."

Noorjahan clicked her tongue. "See? Women remember the real estate of a man. Bottle, bag, cup, debt."

Ruksana shifted the baby higher and studied Nura. "Who's she to him?"

Nura made herself say it cleanly. "He's on my papers."

Ruksana's mouth tightened with the look of a woman recognizing a misery she had seen too often.

"Come in out of the yard, then," she said. "If you're going to hear ugly things, hear them with water in your hand."

Inside Noorjahan's room it was darker and cooler. The packed floor smelled faintly of damp rice sack. A shelf held a steel plate, two schoolbooks with warped corners, a jar of cumin, and a cracked bottle of cough syrup with no cap. Juthi was on the bed platform, still braiding the doll's red threads. Ruksana set the baby down beside her and poured water from a jug into an aluminum tumbler, then put it into Nura's hand the way women handed one another necessary things.

This time Noorjahan stayed standing by the doorway, one hand on the frame.

"You're not the first girl to come through asking after a man's name," she said. "Just the first one this week."

Shamima barked a laugh before she could stop herself. "Only this week."

"I don't mean him," Noorjahan said. "I mean men. Let one rot in one yard and the stink blows through three."

She nodded toward the house across the lane, where an elderly woman was shaking water from a sari with both arms. "When Monir got fever, that one watched my girl while I went for tablets. When he took money and vanished, Ruksana told Momena before Momena sent her nephew to the dredger boys. When my roof tore in the storm after, Jharna slapped plastic over the rafters till my brother came. They saw the wet bedding, the fish turning in the heat, my girl standing in the yard. Then they put hands to it. That's what matters most days."

Nura drank the water. It was warm and metallic, and the tumbler sweated into her palm. Her thumb slipped on the rim. For a moment she saw Halima under a torn roof instead of behind the shelf of papers. Here survival moved yard to yard in cups of tea, watched children, borrowed kerosene, plastic slapped over a leaking roof.

"Did you ever see the papers in that bag?" Nura asked.

Noorjahan's mouth bent. "Only the plastic wrapper around them. He liked saying he had papers more than keeping them dry. When labor men came counting hands for the dredger contractor, he gave his name fast and his father's name faster. Could've been true. Could've been Tuesday."

Ruksana said, "Ask Babul at the pump yard. If the rats haven't eaten those papers, he'll know. He sees everything and keeps it because nobody else wants it."

Juthi, from the bed platform, said, "He whistles with his nose when he sleeps."

Noorjahan pointed at her daughter without turning. "That won't help her."

"It is if she's going to find him."

"She's not going to find him."

The tumbler clicked once against Nura's teeth before she lowered it. Juthi bent over the doll again, winding the red thread tighter. Ruksana lifted the baby and bounced him once on her hip until he hiccupped quiet. Shamima adjusted her scarf and did not look at Nura.

Nura put the tumbler down carefully. "Did he say where he came from? Anything true?"

Noorjahan scratched at a fish scale stuck to her wrist. "He said once that the river had taken his land. He said once that his brothers had. He said once there was a wife who wouldn't take him back. I never met the land, the brothers, or the wife. What I met was a man who could make a face like a hungry boy and borrow the price of tea with it."

"And you waited for him?" Nura asked before she could stop herself.

It was a rude question. She knew it as soon as it left.

But Noorjahan only shrugged. "For a while. A man says he's coming back with wages after you've already spent your own on his fever tablets, and you wait long enough to see what you bought. In my case it was a thief with a sickbed face. The fish still needed salting before noon."

Ruksana laughed, open and unashamed. Even Noorjahan smiled for half a second.

"Go to Babul," Noorjahan said. "If the bag's still hanging where he put it, that's the last useful thing you're likely to get. Then eat something before you fall over. If you go home empty, at least go home standing."

The pump yard lay near the cut where the channel widened and the bank had been hardened with sandbags that were already splitting at the seams. Two engines sat open under a shade roof, each ribbed with hoses, bolts, and rags black with grease. Men moved around them in the detached hurry of workers on someone else's schedule. The dredger itself was farther out, a rusted hulk moored in brown water, its pipe arcing to the bank like a stiff neck.

Babul was easy to pick out. He was short and square, with one ear folded in on itself from some old infection and a pair of cracked eyeglasses he kept pushing back up with the inside of his wrist because both hands were slick with oil. He listened to Shamima's question while tightening a nut with a wrench so worn it no longer had any paint on it.

"Monir," he said, and spat to the side. "Which Monir?"

Shamima gave up first. "The useless one with the blue bag."

Babul barked out a laugh. A younger worker behind him, all elbows and beard fluff, said, "That narrows nothing."

Nura stepped forward. "Thin. Used to work this cut years ago. Stayed on the char behind the dry pond. Noorjahan said he left papers here."

Babul's laugh faded. He squinted at her over the top of his glasses. "You're kin?"

"He's on my papers," Nura said again, and hated the phrase more each time.

Babul wiped his hands on a rag already too dirty to help. "Ah."

He scrubbed the rag across each palm and kept his eyes on the certificate copy peeking from Nura's bag. "Who told you about the bag?"

"Noorjahan."

"She should've let the mud keep its mouth shut."

He stood a moment longer, as if deciding whether Nura's trouble was worth dirtying his hands twice. Then he crossed to a shelf under the shade roof, shoved aside a coil of hose, and dragged down a small blue canvas bag gone nearly black at the seams with old oil. The zipper had furred red with rust. He set it on an upturned drum and worked the teeth apart with the flat edge of his wrench until the cloth gave with a sound like skin tearing.

Inside were a broken comb, a bidi tin with no lid, and three folded papers welded together in cloudy plastic. Babul slit the plastic with a thumbnail and spread the papers flat with greasy fingers. One was half a voter slip, green-black with mildew. The other two were crew-advance chits. On one, Monir had one father. On the other, he had another. Neither father's name matched the line Nura had copied out the night before. The thumbprint at the bottom of both chits was the same smeared oval, pressed harder on the second as if he had already known by then that paper only counted when somebody else believed it.

The younger worker, whose name turned out to be Selim when somebody shouted for him, leaned against the engine frame. He had rolled his lungi up to one thigh and was using a blade to scrape mud from under his toenails with complete concentration.

"Told you he had one," Selim said.

Babul tapped the papers with a blackened nail. "Same face. Same scar under the chin. Different father whenever a clerk wanted one and there wasn't a beating attached to getting it right. Contractor didn't care. Arms lift sand the same whatever father name a man borrows."

Babul said, "If you need him for some government thing, stop now. Even alive, he'd be no use. Those floating crews change names the way they change landing points. Papers get wet. Cards go missing. Half the fathers in those registers could be one man wearing three stories."

Nura kept looking at the two father names until the letters blurred into the same damp smear. Her jaw had locked so hard the hinge hurt. Even alive, he'd be no use.

She had crossed for a signature, a witness, one fact she could carry home in her bag.

She felt suddenly embarrassed by the careful fold of her certificate copy in her bag, by the neatness of her handwriting around the name Monir, by the private little corner of hope she had protected from Halima's contempt and her own intelligence. She had thought perhaps she could arrive, ask the right questions, and walk back onto Kadam Char with something solid: a workplace, an ID number, a witness, a man gone old enough to be sorry. Instead she had found thirteen days of tea, a kerosene bottle, fever water, and a blue bag full of papers that could not keep one father straight from the next.

Shamima thanked them because Nura had gone quiet. On the way back from the yard she bought two guavas from a boy under a tarp and cut them with her thumbnail, salting the slices from a twist of newspaper she kept in her bag.

"Eat," she said, handing one to Nura.

"I'm not hungry."

"That's not what I asked."

Nura bit into the guava because speaking would have taken more effort. It was hard and almost sour. Salt stung the split place inside her lip where old tenderness still lived from the day men had shoved her in Halima's doorway. The sky had brightened to the bleached white that came before noon heat turned mean. Nearby, Momena's stall clattered with cups. Across the lane, Noorjahan had already gone back to her fish basin, spoon scraping silver skin into a metal bowl while flies worried the rim. Nobody paused for Nura to swallow.

Before they headed to the ghat, Ruksana came hurrying after them with a twist of puffed rice in a page torn from a school copybook. "For the ride," she said, pressing it into Nura's hand. The baby was asleep on her shoulder now, drooling onto the printed sunflowers of her housecoat. "You'll get a headache on an empty stomach. And don't go back asking old people for fairy tales. Ask what got written and who's ready to swear to it."

Nura stared at her.

Ruksana shrugged, suddenly almost shy under the force of her own bluntness. "What are you staring at? You think you're the first girl to learn a man can fill the father box and still not buy you one fist of rice?"

She turned away before Nura could answer, calling to Juthi to come inside before the crows got the fish eyes. Shamima watched her go and said, "Well. That saved us one speech."

The return boat left later than Mannan had promised because Mannan had found an extra passenger with two sacks of duck feed and was not a man who respected anybody's urgency except his own. By the time they pushed off, the sun sat overhead like a hand holding the whole river down. The channel looked flatter now, crueler for being so bright. Diesel fumes thickened in the boat well. One duck-feed sack leaked a sweet dusty smell that made Nura think of school storerooms and damp grain.

Shamima sat with her knees up and the sewing-machine belt looped around her wrist like a black bracelet. She waited until the boat cleared the bank before speaking.

"You got what you came for?"

Nura looked at the water shredding off the bow. "I got enough."

"That's not the same thing."

"No."

Shamima let the answer sit. She knew when not to nag a wound. After a while she said, "You know your grandmother will say she was trying to save you from this."

Nura laughed once, and it came out ugly. "Maybe. She still spent my name before I was old enough to keep it myself."

Nura unfolded the slip with Monir's name and smoothed it on her thigh. Sweat had dampened the edges. The pencil line was still dark. The paper had survived by being dried, recopied, and carried carefully from one danger to the next. Monir's own trail had not survived even him. That was the turn in it. Halima had not only hidden a father from her. She had taken a man who could not keep one father name on two greasy chits and fixed him into Nura's future hard enough that an office might treat him as truth.

The boat slammed down from a chop of current and spray hit her calves. Mannan cursed the engine, then patted the skullcap back into place as if decorum could steady the river. The extra passenger fell asleep sitting up with his cheek against the duck-feed sack. Shamima chewed guava seeds and watched Nura from the corner of her eye.

"So what now?" she asked.

Nura folded the slip once, then again. She did not tear it.

"Now I stop looking for him," she said. "I go home and make her tell me the whole thing. What she wrote. Who heard it. Who'll swear to the house when the magistrate asks."

Shamima tipped her head. "That will go well."

"It doesn't have to go well. It has to work."

The river widened. Kadam Char came back into view in pieces: a run of tin roofs, the school wall with its pasted notices lifting at the corners, the mosque courtyard gone pale in noon glare, children no bigger than fingers on the bank. The sight of it brought no relief. Those roofs held Halima, the shelf of papers, and the questions Nura had stacked in her head like forms waiting for a stamp. Under the readiness sat a harder anger than the one that had sent her out in the dark. Before, she had been angry because Halima had denied her choice. Now she was angry because Halima had made the choice first and then kept the method hidden, as if gratitude should cover the theft.

Mannan nosed the boat into the mud. Shamima stood, slung her bag over one shoulder, and said, "If she starts shouting, come get me after. Not during. I don't fight old women before lunch."

The split in Nura's lip pulled when her mouth finally gave. "Coward."

"Practitioner," Shamima said, stepping off.

Nura followed her into the sucking mud at the bank. The puffed rice crackled in the copybook paper under her arm. By the time she had climbed the path toward the house, she had the questions in order: what name Halima had put into the father line, what relation she meant to defend with it, who could still say how many years the house had held them, and who would stand in a schoolroom and swear to it so Nura's future stayed on its feet when the magistrate asked.


# Chapter 13

From the eastern side of the char came, now and then, the low collapsing sound of bank giving way somewhere out of sight, the scrape and slump of a huge wet mat dragged over earth. Halima heard it crossing the yard to the schoolroom. Inside, the questions Nura had gone home with were already waiting on the blackboard in four chalk headings: NAME, RELATION, YEARS IN HOUSE, WITNESSES. Aminul had pushed the children's benches into two hard ranks beneath them. The room smelled of old dust, damp jute, and the sour chalk children sucked when the teacher's back was turned. There had been no rain since yesterday, but the river still made itself known.

Nura sat at the teacher's table with ruled paper stacked by her elbow, the small burn mark on her wrist showing each time she dipped her pen. She had tied her hair back too tight. Since the trip to the neighboring char she had moved with a colder economy. A day spent looking for a father had burned out whatever use she still had for softness. She did not look up when Halima came in. She only said, "He's made groups," and slid her chin toward Aminul.

Aminul stood near the board with his folders held a little off his shirtfront to save them from sweat. Even in this heat he had managed a pressed collar. Chalk dust sat on one cuff. "The clerk sent word from the mainland," he said. "The magistrate may come the day after tomorrow. If not then, the next morning. We don't have room for confusion now."

Halima put her palm on the nearest bench. The wood still held the sticky warmth of children. "A house isn't an exam answer," she said.

"No," Aminul said. "But if five people from one yard give me five versions, the affidavit will tear in the first question."

He had arranged the first pile already, with weak papers on top and difficult dependencies underneath, the households most likely to split open when asked to sound official sitting at the very front. He did not say that part aloud, but Halima saw it in the order of the names. He had learned the island well enough to sort danger. That, more than his folders or his cautious beard, made her distrust him.

The first group came in with their hesitation still on them: Rashid with net tar dried black on his cuffs, his wife Parvin carrying their younger boy on one hip, and Khodeja fupu a half step behind them, counting the tablets knotted in the corner of her sari by touch. Khodeja had no business in a schoolroom this early; she usually spent the morning on Rashid's veranda sorting lentils with her bad eyes close to the grain, pausing every few minutes to spit clove into the yard. Today she had oiled the white hair at her temples flat and put on a blouse with one intact hook. She had come with that one good hook fastened, hoping appearance might keep her from being discussed like a cracked pot.

"Sit," Aminul said, then corrected himself when he saw Khodeja searching for the bench with her feet. "Please. Slowly."

Khodeja lowered herself with both hands and a small animal sound she tried to hide inside her throat. Halima had heard that sound from women laboring, from old men with water in the lungs, from goats dragged by a broken leg. It was the body's unwilling truth. No paper ever held it.

Aminul glanced at the page. "We'll start plain. Give full names, then the relation, then how long you've stayed under one roof. After that I ask the questions the magistrate is likely to ask."

Rashid said his own name first, then Parvin's, then the two boys. When he got to Khodeja he paused just long enough for Halima to feel it in the bench under her hand.

"She's my father's sister," he said.

"And she lives with you?" Aminul asked.

"She stays there," Rashid said.

Parvin shifted the child higher on her hip. "Mostly," she said. "Sometimes she goes west to Khaleka's place."

Khodeja's fingers stopped moving over the medicine knot.

Halima turned her head. "When did she last go west?"

Parvin did not look at her. "I mean, she could. If needed."

Aminul kept his voice level. He was careful like this when he knew a floorboard was rotten. "Listen to me properly. An adult dependent with no clear paper line creates questions. I'm not making a moral point. I'm telling you the risk. If you include her, you need a clean answer for why she's part of the household and who can witness it. If you don't include her, then don't include her anywhere. Don't wobble."

The younger boy had found the chalk tray and was scraping at it with one dirty fingernail. The sound set Halima's teeth on edge.

Rashid said, "The share gets thinner if the household gets bigger. Everyone knows that."

No one answered. The sentence lay in the schoolroom like a bad smell.

Khodeja spoke before Parvin could. Her voice had the rubbed-down grain of an old broom. "Leave me out, then. I can sit with Khaleka till the list goes through. I'm old. I don't need much."

Parvin said too quickly, "No one said that."

"You said enough," Halima said.

Nura's pen stopped. Even Aminul looked up from the page.

Halima stepped closer to Khodeja. She could smell clove, stale cough syrup, and the faint sourness of old cloth dried too slowly. Last winter she had wrapped the old woman's shin after a fall from the veranda step; the skin there had torn like wet paper and healed shiny. Rashid's wife had changed the bandage each morning before cooking. Halima had seen the yellow medicine stains on the family's washing line for a week. Now Parvin stood three feet away pretending this woman floated in and out like weather.

"Where does she sleep?" Halima asked.

Rashid stared at the floor.

"Tell it," Halima said.

"Near the rice drum," he muttered.

"Who gives her tablets?"

Parvin swallowed. "I do."

"Who washed her when she couldn't bend after the fall?"

Parvin's face tightened. "I did."

Halima put her palm flat on the table, not hard, but enough to stop the room from sliding further into cowardice. "Then don't sit here teaching your own mouth to forget her."

Aminul rubbed the chalk dust from his thumb onto his trousers. "And if the magistrate asks why she has no supporting line through husband or sons?"

Halima did not turn toward him. "Then they say the truth. Her husband died. Her son went under in flood season and came back bloated to another char. She has eaten from this house since before that boy there stopped pissing the bed."

The younger boy looked up at the word and grinned. Khodeja did not. Her eyes had gone watery, but she was too proud to let tears fall in front of the others. She only pinched the knot of tablets until the pills made a hard little shape against the cloth.

Aminul said, "Truth isn't the only issue. Sequence is. If one witness says she arrived after the son died and another says before, the clerk will start digging for contradiction. That's what I'm trying to prevent."

"Then prevent contradiction," Halima said. "Don't prevent the woman."

For a moment Aminul looked tired enough to drop the official courtesy from his face. Then he nodded once. "Fine. Again." He lifted his pen. "Rashid. Start from the top. Full names."

This time Rashid said Khodeja's name without swallowing it. The words were not warmer for being repeated, but they stayed in the room. Halima took what she could get.

When the group stood to leave, Khodeja gripped Halima's wrist with fingers dry as fishbone. "Dai-ma," she whispered, keeping her mouth turned away so the others would not hear, "don't waste the government's tin on me."

Halima bent close enough for the old woman to smell the onion she had cut before leaving home. "You hush. You've taken enough less in this life."

Khodeja made a face that might have been agreement or phlegm and shuffled out, one hand still pressed to the medicine knot like she could count herself there if nowhere else.

The next households came faster. Once people understood the rehearsal was real, they did not treat it like advice. They treated it like weather. They braced.

By midmorning the schoolroom had grown hot enough that the chalk lines on the board began to sweat into the paint. Aminul called names, Nura copied statements in a cleaner hand than anyone else on the char could manage, and Halima listened to lives being pressed flatter and flatter so they would slide more easily into official ears.

Bilkis came in with her son Samu on her lap, smelling faintly of stale milk because she had weaned him too quickly in hopes of shrinking herself back down before the hearing. The boy banged a spoon against his own ankle and laughed each time the bone answered. Aminul asked for the marriage date. The room shifted the way rooms always did when sex had to be turned into calendar language in front of men. Bilkis picked at the loose thread on her sleeve.

"He was born early," she said.

From the doorway her mother-in-law said, "Not that early."

Aminul closed his eyes briefly. "Please don't help from the doorway unless asked."

"I'm asked every day by the village," the older woman said, but she stayed put.

Halima watched Bilkis's face. Shame had put a shine on it that was not sweat. She remembered the birth: the child slippery and furious, the husband not yet fully hers in the eyes of the papers, the mother-in-law counting months more angrily than she had counted prayers. Back then Halima had done what she always did. She had wrapped the baby, cut the cord, and dared the household to love him faster than the arithmetic.

"Say this," Halima told Bilkis, not unkindly. "The marriage paper came late because the imam was marooned by flood. You entered the household before the paper, not after the man. That's common enough that no one can prove it strange."

Aminul's mouth tightened, but he wrote it. Then he looked up at Bilkis's mother-in-law. "If that's the line, it has to be yours too."

The older woman sniffed once, like something rotten had been pushed under her nose. Still, she repeated it. Not gracefully. Not kindly. But by the time she had said flood, paper, marriage in the required order, the boy was inside the household on the page, and Halima saw the women waiting at the door register the lesson. Shame could be timed. A body could be kept if the sentence held.

After Bilkis left, the people outside stopped bringing whole stories. They came in carrying only their sharpest risk, waiting to hear whether the room would solve it with a sentence or by cutting someone loose.

Jalal's household came just before noon and left a worse taste. Jalal himself wore a lungi with engine grease at the knee and kept cracking his knuckles, trying to harden himself with the sound. His wife Mina had painted her toenails red once for Eid and the color still clung in stubborn islands. With them came Mina's younger sister Munni and Munni's daughter Tuli, who carried a school slate wrapped in a scarf and stared at the room with the tense stillness of a child waiting to be tested on letters.

Munni had a habit of smoothing the child's hairline with a damp thumb every few breaths, even when it was already flat. The girl tolerated it with the patient contempt of children who knew their mothers were frightened.

Aminul asked the questions in order: names, relations, years in the house, witnesses.

Jalal answered for everyone until Aminul stopped him. "Let each person speak for themselves," he said.

Mina said Munni had been with them since her husband disappeared downriver on dredging work. She said it in a thin voice, turning disappearance into a technical term instead of the shape of half the men's futures on the chars. When Aminul asked whether Munni and the child were part of the household claim, Mina's mouth pinched.

"She helps with work," Mina said. "She stays because where else will she go? But it's not permanent in that sense."

Munni nodded too fast. "I can stay elsewhere till the hearing if that helps."

Tuli looked up at her mother. "Where?"

The fly kept worrying the window grille. Mina stared at the floorboards. Jalal fixed on the chalk headings, waiting for one of them to answer for him.

Aminul said, careful again, "If they're in, they're in. If they're out, they're out. If I present her as a temporary guest and the child as outside the claimant household, you all have to say the same thing without breaking under questions. Understand me."

Nura read back what he had written, her voice flat with disgust she kept tucked inside grammar. "Munni Begum. Temporary guest. Supports herself through domestic help. Child not economically dependent on claimant household."

The sentence made Tuli frown. "I eat there," she said.

Jalal snapped, "Quiet."

But Tuli had already stepped into the crack. She hugged the slate to her chest and looked at Nura instead of the adults. "I sleep by Khala, near the wall where lizards fall from the tin."

Even Aminul's pen paused.

Jalal's fingers stopped on his knuckles. Mina looked down at the wet line of ink on Nura's page. Munni's damp thumb froze against Tuli's hairline, and the child's slate tapped once against the bench leg, small and sharp in the hush.

Munni laughed once, a dry wrong sound. "She's just saying whatever she sees."

"She sees where she sleeps," Halima said.

Mina rounded on her, all the strain of the last weeks showing through the careful wife-face. "And if we list every hungry person who has slept on our floor this year, what then? Will the government give us another room at Nabinpara? Another cooking pot? More rice? Or do we all stand in line for one smaller mercy?"

Jalal did not crack another knuckle. Mina kept both hands braced on the bench, holding back the extra room, the extra pot, the extra scoop of rice with her bare palms. Halima opened her mouth and found nothing in it that would feed them.

Aminul took it as permission to keep going. "The magistrate won't reward confusion. He won't reward generosity either, if it can't be defended. If Munni and the child are permanent dependents, then say that and stand by it. If not, don't improvise tenderness in the hearing and expect the file to carry it."

Munni stared at the board. Nura's copy lay on the table, the words temporary guest still wet enough to shine. Halima remembered Munni three months earlier with blood up to both wrists, helping press Mina's belly after the youngest was born, steady as a trained hand though no one had ever paid her for the steadiness. She remembered Tuli asleep under the same mosquito net as Mina's sons while the rain beat the tin all night. Temporary, Aminul had written. Storm seasons did not measure cleanly from inside an office.

"Cross that out," Halima said.

Aminul's pen hung over the page. Jalal kept his eyes on the tabletop. Tuli's slate edge clicked once against the bench and went still.

"Cross it out," she said again, this time to Nura.

Nura met her eyes for a second, then drew a hard line through temporary guest so deep the nib nearly tore the paper.

Jalal looked from one face to the next and found no rescue. "If we claim them," he said, "and the share breaks smaller, who's feeding everybody after?"

"You already are," Halima said.

"Not under a clerk's eye."

Nura lifted the page to keep the wet ink from smearing. The line through temporary guest had bitten through the paper and ghosted onto the blank form beneath. After that she stopped cushioning Aminul's language. When a line cut someone smaller, she read it back exactly, eyes on the person it cut. The room began to flinch before the words landed.

The call to noon prayer drifted thinly from the mosque, and for a few minutes the schoolroom emptied enough for breath. People stood outside in clumps under the hard white light, arguing in low voices they imagined the room could not hear. It heard everything. Children passed at the doorway and peered in at the blackboard. To them it was a game. One of them had drawn a boat in the corner of the last page Nura had copied. She blotted it out with the side of her hand before Aminul saw.

When Majed and Jamila came after the break, they brought the opposite lesson. Jamila had dressed for seriousness in a washed sari with a narrow dark border and sat so straight her spine looked nailed together. Majed smelled of engine oil even after scrubbing. He kept running his thumb over the split skin beside his nail, counting how much work waited at home.

"Please write it exactly as the papers say," Jamila told Aminul. Her voice was calm enough to cut skin. "There won't be any confusion from our side."

Aminul asked one careful follow-up about who, beyond the named pair, used their roof as a matter of right. Jamila answered before Majed could.

"Write what stands in the papers and what can be witnessed cleanly," she said. "Nothing extra."

Majed's mouth opened, then shut. Aminul set their packet on the ready pile by his left elbow, the one closest to the magistrate's visit. The people waiting at the doorway tracked that movement the way hungry people tracked a ladle. Halima heard the lesson settle over the room: the fuller the life, the shakier the file; the cleaner the file, the quicker the mercy.

By afternoon Aminul's blackboard had turned into a map of subtraction. A sister who had slept for eleven years in her brother-in-law's house became, on Nura's page, a temporary guest. A widowed aunt who had raised three children through two flood seasons became not a regular dependent. Men went home to count cooking pots and sleeping corners and came back with smaller households in their mouths.

Halima had spent thirty years doing the opposite work. A girl abandoned after marriage came back with a swollen belly and no safe father's name for the child, so Halima found one that would hold. A nephew whose brother died on a boat began eating from his uncle's pot, and she taught the household to stop saying guest. A widow moved her mat closer to the kitchen and became, by repetition, part of the family. It had never been clean work. It had been stitched from fear, compromise, pity, and the practical knowledge that hunger did not wait for legal elegance. Still, it had pulled people inward. What Aminul called rehearsal was teaching the island a different hand movement entirely.

Late in the afternoon Bashir came in with the boy everyone called his stepson. The boy had already rolled his bedding; the jute rope bit into the web of his hand. He carried an enamel plate hooked through two fingers and an old mosquito net over one shoulder. Fish scales glittered on his calves.

Aminul looked from the bundle to the boy. "You're changing the line?"

Bashir kept his eyes on the board. "Write him under his mother's brother till after the hearing. He'll sleep on my boat. That's cleaner."

The boy shifted the bedding higher against his hip. "The boat leaks from the stern."

"Then sleep in the bow," Bashir said. "You've done it before."

Halima heard Nura stop writing.

"Can I take the thicker quilt?" the boy asked.

"No," Bashir said. He pulled the better quilt out of the roll himself, set it aside on the bench for his younger children, and pushed the reed mat and torn mosquito net back at the boy. "Take those. You'll manage two nights."

The boy said nothing after that. He slid the enamel plate between the mat and the net, bent his shoulder under the bundle, and went down the school steps before anyone dismissed him. Halima watched the rope saw at his palm as he crossed the yard toward the ghat, leaning his whole small body against the load to keep it from slipping.

The room stayed quiet long enough to hear his feet on the hard ground outside.

When the line moved again, Halima stepped out to the veranda because the air inside had become unbreathable. The schoolyard was white with late light. Goats nosed at the fence posts. Down by the water the boy was shoving the bedding into a fishing boat one-handed because the other palm had gone red where the rope had burned it.

Aminul followed after a minute with the board duster still in his hand, forgotten there. Up close the care in his appearance had come undone. Sweat had darkened the collar seams. Chalk had settled in the trim of his moustache.

"You think I don't see what this is doing," he said.

Halima leaned one shoulder against the wall. The rough plaster caught at her blouse. "You see it and keep teaching it."

"I'm not teaching cruelty."

"No? That boy just lost his bed to a sentence."

He set the duster on the windowsill with more force than necessary. Dust rose from it and drifted back into the room. "We prepared people for the kind of questions they're going to face whether you like it or not. If I don't force consistency here, the magistrate will force it there, in public, with no patience and less mercy."

Halima looked past him into the schoolroom. Nura was gathering the copied pages into piles, aligning their corners with sharp taps against the table. Each tap sounded to Halima like a door being shut.

"Mercy," she said. "You call this mercy."

"I call it triage."

The word hit wrong in her ear. It belonged to clinics, to road accidents, to choices made over bleeding people because there were not enough hands. Aminul had meant to sound practical. Instead he sounded like a man who had spent too long among categories and begun trusting them more than flesh.

"These aren't broken limbs on a floor," she said. "They're households."

"And households can still be excluded entirely." His own temper had frayed now; the balance in his sentences was gone. "Tell me I'm wrong. Tell me the magistrate will sit there all day and listen to every cousin, every foster child, every woman who came back from a vanished husband and never left again. He won't. He wants a list he can sign before the launch schedule shifts. I'm trying to get people through the mouth of a bottle."

Halima thought of Khodeja pinching her tablets through the sari knot, of Tuli saying where lizards fell from the tin, of Bilkis's mother-in-law swallowing the flood story because it saved the child, of Bashir's boy carrying the torn net toward the boat. The schoolroom had stripped ordinary acts of care and made them beg for classification.

"And to make them fit," she said, "you want them to practice leaving each other out before anyone even asks."

"They were doing that already."

"Not like this."

Aminul dragged a hand over his face and came away with chalk on the side of his thumb. "You think your way doesn't cut? It does. By the time it reaches the table, it still has to fit under one witness line, one thumbprint, one file. Then I still have to mark one household paid and another sent to appeals."

She heard the fatigue in him then, the sympathy his job never allowed him to display except as irritation. That was the worst of him and the best: he was not blind. He simply kept going after seeing.

"I know it cuts," she said. Her voice had dropped without her meaning it to. "I've been cutting and stitching this island together since before you learned to shave. But don't stand there and make it sound like you're only sorting paper. You're moving Khodeja's pills away from Rashid's rice drum. You're putting that child off the wall where the lizards drop and into a leaking boat so your page stays neat. That kind of cutting is easy to teach."

He did look at her then. Not as an officer. As a man who had perhaps been waiting for someone to say the ugliest thing plainly.

"That kind of cutting may still get a family housed," he said.

"At whose door does the old woman then die? In whose yard does the child return when the cousin gets tired of feeding her? When does the boy stop being temporary and just become a body everyone got used to outside?" Halima pushed herself off the wall. "You can clear a file by moving a mat. You can't clear the hunger that follows it."

For a second she thought he might answer with the hard institutional sentence, the one about limits and regulations and what could or could not be entertained. Instead he looked out toward the western huts where the poorer houses crouched low and separate. Distance was the only camouflage the school wall ever offered them.

"What would you have me do?" he asked, almost quietly. "Take every contradiction in raw and watch half the claims collapse?"

Halima did not answer at once. The truth was that she had no clean method to offer. Her own life disproved cleanliness. She wanted the magistrate to hear the mess of dependency exactly as it was lived: who carried water, who slept by whose wall, who came back hungry and was not turned away. She also knew what happened when men in offices met mess. They called it fraud and kept their pens dry.

Nura came to the doorway with the copied statements stacked against her chest. "It's spreading," she said. Her voice was brisk enough to sound almost detached, but Halima knew the signs of strain in the girl now. She overcontrolled when frightened. "Khodeja fupu's bedding has been sent to Khaleka's cousin's hut on the west side. Munni and Tuli are going to stay near the tea stall. Bashir's boy is already on the boat."

She handed the papers to Aminul and went back inside before either of them replied.

Halima stood with chalk grit on her palm.

Across the yard, Khaleka's grandson crossed with Khodeja fupu's rolled mat on one shoulder and her pill tin tied into the bedding so it knocked against the bamboo frame at each step. Near the gate, Munni had Tuli by the wrist, the child's slate wrapped in the same scarf bundle, both of them already turned toward the tea stalls. Down at the ghat, Bashir's stepson shoved the bedding farther under the thwart with his good hand and sat in the bow with his knees pulled up. He did not look back toward the houses.

From inside, Aminul called the next household. Halima turned from the yard and went back in and sat on the mat.


# Chapter 14

Halima stayed on the mat after the last witness left, watching Aminul rub chalk off his fingers onto his trousers. The schoolroom still smelled of old sweat, damp jute, and chalk. All afternoon people had stood in front of the blackboard and practiced saying shame in ways that sounded administrative. Before the room emptied, the first rolled mats had already started crossing the yard toward the roofs those sentences favored. A sister who had slept for eleven years in her brother-in-law's house had been taught to call herself a temporary guest. An aunt who had raised three children through two flood seasons had become, on Aminul's page, "not a regular dependent." Halima had sat through it with her bad eye watering from dust and rage, listening to lived years narrowed until they could pass under a magistrate's pen.

Sufia came in near the end with a kettle hooked in one cloth-thickened hand and six glasses trapped between the fingers of the other. She always sorted the glasses by cracks, tapping each rim with her thumbnail before she poured, as if she could hear which one would split under hot tea and which one would last another week. The boys at the ghat called her stingy for that. They did not know what six broken glasses cost a stall that already sold more on promise than on cash.

She poured for Aminul first, because he was the man with files. Then for the two men from the eastern side who had spent an hour deciding whether an old widowed aunt ate enough in their yard to count. Then for Nura, who had been copying names at the edge of the desk with the quick, angry neatness she used when she wanted not to speak. When Sufia reached Halima she tilted the glass wrong and the tea slopped over her own knuckles.

"Your hand's gone blind before your eye has," Halima said.

Sufia sucked tea off the base of her thumb and gave the little dry smile she used when she wanted a sentence to stay neighborly. "It's been a bad day for all body parts. The ferry boys broke one spoon. Your Nura wrote through two nibs. That old Karim uncle farted in God's own face and blamed the bench."

Nura snorted despite herself. Aminul looked up from his papers, not sure whether he was allowed to hear that. Halima kept her eyes on Sufia. The woman had missed her look twice already. That was unlike her. Sufia lived by noticing what other people wished she would miss.

Halima took the glass but did not drink. "Come to the house after evening prayer."

Sufia set the kettle down too hard. The glasses chimed against one another. "House? For what?"

"To answer something."

"Everything needs answering these days." Sufia bent to gather empty saucers that did not need gathering. "I've got a stall to run, Halima-dai. Men still drink tea while their households collapse. Maybe more because of it."

"Then answer here."

That made Sufia look up for a breath, but it was long enough.

The schoolroom had thinned. One of the eastern men was still arguing with Aminul about whether a daughter's marriage counted as household exit if she kept coming back to eat. Nura was stacking used papers and blowing grit off the top sheet. Outside, children were dragging a broken bench across the yard. The sound went through the wall like a saw.

Sufia lowered her voice. "Not in front of chalk and boys."

Halima set the tea untouched on the desk. "Then where?"

Sufia wiped her hands down the front of her sari, leaving two darker lines in the cloth. "When I close the stall. Behind it. After the last boat."

"What am I waiting to hear?"

Sufia gave a short laugh with no air in it. "If I knew how to say it fast, I'd have said it already."

That was when Halima understood the thing had shape. This was not rumor or some boy's theft or some party man's rummaging through other people's bedding. It belonged to someone whose face she knew.

She left Sufia to her kettle and stayed through the end of the rehearsal because leaving early would have started ten new stories. Aminul ran the final household again, trying to get a husband to say his stepdaughter had lived with him since age six instead of age eight because the school record would hold better. The man kept shaving the girl smaller with every sentence. Halima stopped him only when he got down to four.

"She wasn't born in your fishing basket," she said. "Stop cutting her to fit your mouth."

No one answered. Nura's pen stopped. Aminul made a mark in the margin and kept his face tipped toward the page, as if the paper could shelter him from Halima.

By dusk the schoolyard had gone copper with dust and low sun. The air was drier now than it had been a week ago, but the char still held its smell of wet silt underneath everything, as if the mud had merely pulled a thin skin over itself and was waiting to be stepped through. Halima walked toward the ghat with the taste of unsipped tea and anger sitting together in her mouth.

Sufia's stall stood where it always did, just above the landing slope, half bamboo, half tin, its front bench listing because one leg had sunk farther into the earth than the others. She had folded an old election leaflet under that leg months ago and kept kicking it back into place whenever customers rocked the bench loose. She did it now without looking, heel finding paper by habit. Her limp was worse when she was tired. She hid it in company by moving faster.

Boatmen were taking their last tea before dark. A boy in a school shirt chewed betel and pretended he was old enough to spit red. Sufia worked the kettle, cut limes, counted coins, and kept angling her body toward the customers as if business itself could hold Halima off. She filled silence with business.

"The milk turned sour by noon," she said to no one and everyone. "Whoever brought that can must hate my father's soul. You, pay now or drink river water tomorrow. No, not tomorrow, tonight. Your debt already has grandchildren."

The men laughed. Sufia laughed with them. Her hands stayed quick. Only the skin at the base of her throat moved too fast.

Halima waited. She had delivered babies in smaller spaces than that stall, had stood between women's knees while mothers-in-law, goats, rain, and screaming children crowded the same room. Waiting did not tire her. It tired other people. At last the final boat pushed off and the last two men drained their glasses and went up the slope still arguing about rope prices. Sufia rinsed the kettle once, wiped the counter, closed the biscuit tin, and dropped the day's coins into a rusted tobacco box.

"Come then," she said without turning. "If I'm going to ruin my own evening, I might as well get on with it."

She led Halima behind the stall to the narrow strip where Sufia kept firewood under a bent sheet of corrugated tin. A torn fishing net hung there to dry. Beyond it the river took up the dark, flat and wide and never still. Water slapped the moored boats below in small patient blows. Somewhere farther down the bank a child was crying in the thin, affronted way of a child who had not yet understood that nobody was coming quickly.

Sufia lowered herself onto an upturned crate and rubbed once at the bad ankle through her sari. Halima stayed standing. The tin behind them still held the day's heat.

"Say it," Halima said.

Sufia looked at the river first, not at her. "You remember that flood year when the water took the goat sheds from the west side and everybody had to lift trunks onto charpoys? The year you went house to house telling women to sleep with the papers under their blouses if they had to?"

"I remember every year. Pick one."

"The year after Saleha died."

The name hit Halima low and hard, the way an elbow caught by accident in a crowd could hurt more than a planned blow. Sufia saw it land and did not look away this time.

"I copied them," she said.

The river kept slapping the boat hull. A rope strained and eased. Halima heard each sound separately, too clearly.

"What did you copy?"

"You know what."

"Make your mouth do the work."

Sufia swallowed. When she was frightened she usually packed the air with ordinary detail until the fear lost its edges. Halima could hear her trying now.

"The kettle cracked that season. Remember? I was boiling tea in that black pot with no handle. My boy had fever. Mofiz was bringing men from the union office around because of that survey on the eastern side. Everybody was saying land lines would be measured proper this time, not guessed from whoever shouted loudest. You had that fever after the twins. You were talking in your sleep. I sat there and thought if you died, half this char would wake up without names."

She stopped. Halima let the silence sit. Sufia had used the wrong road on purpose, walking around the thing until shame tired her out. Let it.

"I copied selected pages," Sufia said at last. "I couldn't manage the whole ledger. My hand cramped and my spelling was already ugly enough to insult the dead. I took the pages that touched the eastern high ground and the households always arguing over who belonged under which roof. I made copies at night when I helped you sort things. Sometimes when you were bathing. Once while you slept."

The last sentence came out small, though it cut deepest. It named the moment Sufia had crossed the mat while Halima slept within arm's reach.

Halima felt the old labor calluses in her palms as if she were gripping someone's knees. She could see Sufia at nineteen, a thin-armed girl with lamp smoke in her hair, holding hot water while Halima cut cords. She could see Sufia later, broadening, slowing in one leg, still good with frightened mothers and still quicker than most women with a clean cloth. She had let this woman into blood rooms, into the hour when a woman's insides were still partly outside her, into the little stupid jokes women made while waiting for pain. She had not let her into the ledger.

"Why those pages?" Halima asked.

"Because those were the ones Mofiz said would matter if the survey men came back. He said the higher ground would decide who got pushed west and who kept their bamboo. He said if there wasn't a second trace anywhere, one bad leak in your roof and whole households would disappear into mud."

"So you handed him a second trace."

"I handed him what I thought was a backup."

Halima laughed once, harsh enough to startle even herself. "To Mofiz."

"Yes. To Mofiz." Sufia's chin lifted, not in pride but in refusal to beg yet. "He had a steel trunk then. He had dealings with offices. Men listened when he said paper should be kept dry. You had one oilcloth bundle under a roof every gossiping fool on the char could point to."

"I kept it out of men's hands for a reason."

"I know why you kept it." Sufia's voice sharpened for the first time. "Don't talk to me like I found it under a bush. I was there when you wrote half those lines. I held the lamp. I know which father was made from mercy and which from fear and which from the need to keep a girl out of a house that would've eaten her. I know."

She breathed through her nose, once, twice. When she spoke again the sideways humor was gone.

"I also knew one woman was holding too much."

That hit differently. Halima had been ready for accusation, for apology, even for some miserable little confession that Sufia had taken money. This was worse because it had bone in it.

"You think I wanted it?" Halima said. "You think I sat up nights rubbing my hands because the whole char had to come through my door?"

Sufia shook her head. "Wanting it isn't the only way. If every woman has to duck under one eave and sit on one mat till you lift your head, that's still a grip."

The tin at Halima's back gave off stored heat. Sweat slid once down her spine and stopped under the waistband of her petticoat. She locked her knees and let it cool there.

Sufia went on, and now that she had stepped onto the straight road she stayed there.

"When women came to you, they came because they had to. A husband gone to debt work, a child born too soon after marriage, a girl who needed a father line so some bastard wouldn't bargain for her, a widow who needed a brother's name so the rice pot stayed on. You helped them. I'm not spitting on that. But they still had to come through your door, sit on your mat, and watch your face while you decided what could bend and what couldn't. If the roof leaked and the book turned to paste, all of it went with you. That scared me. More because you talked like being scared of that made a person small."

Halima opened her mouth, then shut it. What came first was not a rebuttal but a row of bodies. Women crouched on her floor with wet hems. Men who could not meet her eye until they needed her to make a child belong somewhere. Grandmothers pressing coins into her hand to ask whether a date might move by two months if it saved a daughter. She had called it work because it was work. Blood work. Paper work. Women's after-midnight work done while officials slept. But every one of those people had still sat and waited while she decided what could be altered and what could not bear touch.

She had never taken bribes the way Mofiz did. She had never turned a widow out to enlarge a land strip. She had never made a line for profit. All of that remained true. Truth could sit beside another truth without canceling it. Her hand opened and closed once against her thigh.

"What else?" she said.

Sufia picked a splinter from the edge of the crate and rolled it between her fingers. "He used them."

Halima had known that was coming. Knowing did not soften it.

"Tell it properly."

"Whenever there was a quarrel over the eastern side, he had those copies in plastic. He never showed the whole page, only enough to feed whichever side needed feeding. This child was born under that roof, so the mother had entered that household before the old man died. This daughter had already married out, so she couldn't split a homestead later. This younger boy had no separate father line worth counting, so he stayed folded into the bigger house. Little lines. You know how much a little line can hold."

Halima knew.

"He started thickening his own people with them," Sufia said. "He didn't always do it by name. He did it by arrangement, by who had been attached to him long enough that he could sound inevitable. A widow here, a nephew there, a tenant turned kin when kin was useful. He made old improvisations sit under his hand like they were law. And because some of those lines came from your mercy, they looked old and respectable, already lived in."

The river smell thickened as the evening cooled, damp metal and fish oil and silt. Halima could almost see the copied pages, not as paper but as the births behind them. A baby rubbed blue to pink while rain hammered tin. A girl child registered under a safer uncle because the real father drank through every wage and had already promised one daughter to a creditor's son. A boy slid into a brother's household on paper because otherwise the dead man's debts would have reached him before his teeth were all in. Those were the kinds of bends she had made. Many had been necessary, though none of them had been clean.

And Mofiz had taken the bends and turned them into walls.

"He couldn't have done that without wanting it already," Halima said.

"No." Sufia looked at her then, direct enough to hurt. "But he couldn't have done it without me either."

That, finally, sounded like confession rather than argument.

Halima thought of the search at her house. The stomp of sandals, the young men's hands in bedding and rice tins, Nura's split lip. She had spent days thinking of theft as a sudden act, something taken in one afternoon by men who smelled blood. Sufia was showing her the slower theft underneath it, the one done years ago in lamplight by a woman with tired hands who thought she was preventing a future disaster and was, in fact, storing one.

"Did he pay you?" Halima asked.

Sufia flinched as if slapped, then recovered enough to answer straight. "Not like that."

"That means he paid you some way."

"He kept my stall where it was when the ghat got shifted. He talked to the ferry boys when they got drunk and started taking tea on credit they'd never settle. He made me feel like I wasn't just passing cups, like I had a place in matters bigger than milk and lime." Her mouth twisted. "There. That's uglier than money. Happy?"

Halima was not happy. The answer, though, made sense in the sour ordinary way human failure usually made sense. Fear had one pocket. Vanity had another. Sufia worried the edge of her sari so hard the cloth gave a faint dry squeak.

"Why tell me now?"

Sufia spat neatly into the mud away from the firewood, red betel darkening to brown almost at once. When she answered, her voice had gone flat.

"Because he's getting ready to use them at the hearing if the room turns against him. He showed me yesterday, not to confess but to frighten me. He had three copied sheets in a plastic sleeve with the corners taped. He said if you started preaching household truth in front of the magistrate, he'd remind the room what kind of truth you wrote with. He'll say your book shifts to suit whoever is in front of you. He'll stain the ledger enough to keep his own pieces useful."

Halima held her jaw shut until it ached.

"He thinks because I gave them, I'll stand where he tells me if he needs witness. I won't." Sufia swallowed again. "I came to you instead. Late. Cowardly late. But not that far."

For a moment Halima wanted the easy thing: to call her rotten, call her sold, call her the kind of woman who could stand beside births for years and still miss what mercy cost. The words rose. None of them stayed true long enough to say.

"What did you think a backup was?" Halima asked quietly. "Paper doesn't stay innocent because you make a second copy."

Sufia put both hands flat on her knees. The tea-stall hands. Quick, scarred, strong at the thumb web from lifting kettles and pounding betel. Once those same hands had pressed a slippery newborn against a mother's breast while Halima stitched a tear. Once they had turned a breech baby a little lower when Halima told her to brace. There was nothing villainous about them. That was the worst of it.

"I thought a second copy meant the truth wouldn't drown with one old woman," Sufia said. "I thought if your roof went or rats got into the loft, there'd still be something dry in that steel trunk of his, under plastic, close to an office stamp. I didn't think hard enough about which truth I'd put there, or whose hand would break the seal first. I was stupid. And I was frightened. Both are true."

Halima leaned one shoulder against the tin at last. It burned through the damp cloth of her blouse. Beyond Sufia the black water opened and shut around the anchored boats. Somewhere uphill somebody was pounding chili on stone. Each strike carried clean in the thinning air.

"I used to think hiding it was the clean part," Halima said, mostly to herself.

"Was it?" Sufia asked.

Halima kept looking past Sufia at the river-dark gap beyond the torn net. She saw her own yard instead: the low stool by the door, women crossing it with wet hems and old newspaper bundles tucked under an arm, babies burning against a shoulder. She had made them come one by one because paper bent differently under a single pair of hands; she needed to hear the breathing, see whose knuckles shook, judge whether a date could move or a father could. Still, one by one meant through her doorway, across her mat, under her eye. She had called that protection, and often it was. It was also a kind of power with no stamp on it except her own say.

She straightened from the tin.

"Who else knows you copied them?"

"No one I told. Maybe people guessed. Mofiz knows. One or two men he showed pieces to over the years, maybe. Not the whole thing."

"Does Nura know?"

Sufia shook her head hard. "No."

"Good."

The word came out harder than Halima intended. Nura's false father line was not some abstract household inconsistency. It was Saleha's death stretched forward through paper and into the girl's own mouth. Once the ledger went public, Nura's name would not stay clean at the edges. Halima knew that. She knew Jamila's would not. Kashem's would not. Rahima's would not. None of the careful silences would.

Sufia watched her thinking and seemed to understand enough not to interrupt.

At last Halima said, "If I keep it hidden now, he chooses which pieces of us get dragged out."

"He'll pick the ugliest bits and make them sound like the whole book."

"If I bring it out myself, he still drags."

"Then make him say it with the full pages in the room," Sufia said. "Not from three corners he's kept in plastic."

"Then there's no protected version left."

Sufia's face changed at that, not into relief but into the expression of a person hearing a sentence she had feared and expected both. "No," she said. "There isn't."

Halima pushed away from the tin. Her knees cracked. "You won't stand for him."

"I won't."

"You'll say what you told me if asked."

Sufia's chin went down, then up. "I will."

"And if you try to turn sideways again-"

"I know what your hand still weighs," Sufia said. There was almost humor in it, and the humor hurt too. "Go. Before I start crying like an idiot and make you hate me less."

"I don't hate you less."

"I didn't ask for less. I said before."

That, at least, sounded like Sufia. Halima left her there among the firewood and torn net and the stale tea smell sunk into bamboo.

The path home took her past the school first. The blackboard was still visible through the open door, a ghost rectangle in the darkening room. On it Aminul had written three witness headings in careful Bangla and rubbed out two of them so often the chalk dust had ground into the paint. Household head. Duration of residence. Supporting relation. All day they had been trying to shove lives under those three headings until people started trimming one another to fit. An aunt removed. A stepchild blurred. A married daughter pushed farther away from the cooking fire that had fed her half her life.

The rehearsals had felt cruel already. Sufia's confession made them look worse: the chalk headings on Aminul's board, the names shaved down to fit beneath them, the aunt moved off the cooking fire by a single word. Halima had thought she was deciding between hidden truth and public harm. That choice was gone now. The harm had already seeped out through copied sheets and plastic sleeves into Mofiz's hands. Keeping the ledger under cloth had not stopped him; it had only let him choose which scraps to wave in other people's faces.

By the time she reached the house, the cooking fire was lit. Nura was squatting by the stove with her dupatta tucked tight at the waist, blowing under the pot ring. The rice water hissed over and ran into the ash. She looked up once, quickly.

"You're late."

Halima set her sandals by the wall. "I had talk."

"With who?"

"With Sufia."

That made Nura stop. Not because Sufia mattered in herself, maybe, but because nobody sought Sufia privately unless news had gone poisonous.

"What now?" Nura asked.

Halima stood listening to the rice water hiss into ash before she answered. The house smelled of rice starch, smoke, old paper, and the medicinal oil Nura had been rubbing into her wrist where the old burn tightened in damp weather. Above them, on the loft shelf, the oilcloth bundle sat where it had sat through storms, searches, and nights when Halima had sworn she would rather let it rot than let it rule the house again.

She climbed onto the low stool, reached up, and brought it down.

"What happened?" Nura said.

"Sufia made the copies."

"The missing pages?"

"Years ago."

Nura's face sharpened at once, every line in it going thin. "For Mofiz?"

"At first, she says, for backup. To him, yes."

Nura laughed once, bitter as neem. "A backup in his pocket. That's clever."

"She thought one hidden book was too much in one place."

The sentence had come out before Halima had decided whether to give Sufia that much fairness. Nura heard it anyway.

"Was she wrong?" Nura asked.

Halima laid the bundle on the mat and unwrapped the outer cloth. The ledger inside smelled faintly of mildew, lamp soot, and the old mustard oil from Halima's hands. Alongside it were the loose copied sheets still remaining, their edges softened from years of handling. Gaps showed where others had once sat.

"She was wrong about him," Halima said.

"That wasn't what I asked."

Nura's voice had changed in the last two weeks. It had not deepened, but it had sharpened into something more exact. She had stopped asking for comfort in ways that could be mistaken for obedience. Halima touched the ledger cover with two fingers. The board underneath was warped from damp.

"No," she said after a while. "She wasn't wrong about the weight of it."

Nura let out the breath she'd been holding. The sound carried no triumph or forgiveness, only recognition.

"What did he do with the copies?" she asked.

"Used lines from them to stiffen his own claims. Eastern side households. Land talk. Dependents folded where he wanted them. He plans to use them again if the hearing turns."

Nura sank back onto her heels. The fire snapped behind her. "Then he'll do it anyway. Whether you speak or not."

"He'll do it dirtier if I stay quiet."

"And if you speak, he'll say you forged it all."

"He's already decided what to call me. Silence just gives him the cleaner lie."

Nura looked at the open ledger, then at Halima. In the firelight the burn mark on her wrist shone pale and tight. "If you take that to the hearing, you're taking me too."

There was no theatricality in it. That was what made it hard. Nura was only stating a fact: a girl's father line, already rotten, was about to be handled in public by men with notebooks.

Halima sat down opposite her. The mat pressed the knobs of bamboo through her knees.

"I know."

Nura gave her a flat look. "Do you? The minute that book opens, Mofiz will call me bastard in front of half the char. He'll drag my mother through the yard, say you stitched a father onto me after the fact, and the men will sit there enjoying it because shame is free entertainment if it belongs to somebody else."

Halima took that without interrupting. Smoke from the stove had crept into the room and was needling her bad eye again.

"And if the magistrate hears father-line fraud before he hears anything else," Nura said, "my clinic road dies right there on the table. No one takes a trainee whose papers turn into a public joke."

Halima looked at the ledger between them, at the warped cover that had once seemed like protection because it stayed under cloth. "Yes," she said. "He may call you that. The magistrate may mark your file dirty before he marks anything else. I'm not going to lie to you about tomorrow."

Nura's jaw set, though whether from relief or anger Halima could not tell.

"But if I keep this hidden," Halima said, tapping the cover once, "Mofiz still gets to spit those same words. He just gets to do it from three stolen scraps while the magistrate hears only his version first. I'd rather put the whole wound on the table than let him keep cutting you with corners."

Nura's mouth moved once more. This time the cruel thing came out tired instead. "So I get shamed properly, with full documentation."

Halima almost said don't talk like that. Waste of breath. Instead she said, "You get the full household in the room with you. The years people fed you, kept you in school, and answered for you. It may still hurt you. It will. But it won't be only his plastic sleeve doing the speaking."

"Did you decide already," Nura asked, "or were you going to sit here till dawn and call that thinking?"

Halima almost told her not to use that tone. Waste of breath. The girl was right.

"I decided walking home."

Nura nodded once. "Then call Aminul before he finishes cleaning everybody into lies."

Halima looked up sharply.

Nura shrugged, not insolent, just tired. "What? You think I didn't hear today? If one more man says his aunt only eats with him during festivals, I'll feed him his own pen."

A laugh tried to rise in Halima and failed halfway. It left ache behind.

She rewrapped the ledger, not tightly, not for hiding. She left the knot loose and set the bundle by the door instead of on the shelf. Nura watched that more closely than any spoken sentence. On this char, where walls were thin and hands quick and mud got into everything, placement was declaration. The book by the door was not a secret anymore. It was cargo.

Aminul was still at the school when Halima arrived. He had moved one bench under the hanging bulb and spread the witness sheets there in orderly stacks. Even at that hour he held his folder away from his body as if sweat were a form of contamination he could out-discipline. The room was quieter without the day's fear in it, but not kind. Chalk dust had settled into the grain of the desks. A gecko clicked from behind the calendar pinned to the wall. Outside, the river kept speaking to itself in the dark.

Aminul looked up when she came in. His eyes went immediately to the bundle in her hands.

"You brought it out," he said.

"Sit properly," Halima said. "I didn't come here to watch surprise crawl over your face."

He sat, not because she outranked him in any official way but because by now he knew when not to waste motion. "What happened?"

"Sufia copied selected pages years ago and gave them to Mofiz. He used them to strengthen his eastern-side claims. He means to use them again at the hearing if he starts losing ground."

Aminul's expression changed in stages: disbelief first, then calculation, then the tight carefulness he put on when a fact threatened his existing plan. "Are you certain?"

"Certain enough to bring the book here."

"Did she explain how much he has?"

"Enough."

"Can she testify to it?"

"Yes, if she doesn't crawl back into fear before morning."

Aminul rubbed his moustache once. "This complicates-"

Halima set the bundle down on top of his neat stacks hard enough that the pages shifted. "Don't say complicates to me tonight."

The rest of the sentence stayed behind his teeth.

For a moment none of them spoke: not Aminul, not Halima, not the gecko behind the calendar. The hanging bulb buzzed. Somewhere outside a bicycle bell rang twice and faded.

At last Aminul said, more carefully, "If Mofiz has excerpts, then he can challenge authenticity selectively. If we rely on witness summaries alone, he can use the copies to create doubt around any household you support."

"He already has doubt in his pocket," Halima said. "I'm done letting him spend it alone."

"If you produce the full ledger, he'll attack the whole document."

"Then he can come at the whole book in front of the room," she said. "Not keep cutting us with scraps."

Aminul's gaze stayed on the oilcloth. "And if the whole document is attacked, every altered line in it becomes potential ammunition. Including your own household."

He was not warning her about honor. He was warning her about consequence, which was his better quality. Halima respected that enough not to pretend otherwise.

"I know whose lines are in my house," she said.

He let that sit. "Then what are you asking me to do?"

Halima felt suddenly, vividly, the weight of the book when it was wet. The drag of paper that had taken on river air over years. The way it had grown heavier not because ink weighed much but because lives did. She had spent three decades deciding when that weight should remain under cloth and when it should be brought to light for one person, two people, never the room. That control was gone. Sufia had broken it years ago. Mofiz had been using the broken pieces while Halima still told herself the center held.

"No more rehearsals that trim people smaller," she said. "No more teaching men which old woman to forget and which child to half-claim. Tomorrow the magistrate gets the ledger with the households, the lies, and the reasons buried in them. If Mofiz wants dirt, he can stand in the full field, not pocket handfuls."

Aminul looked at her for a long moment. In the bulb light his shirt collar had finally wilted. He looked less like an official then and more like a tired man who had spent weeks trying to make an institution behave like it had ears.

"That's not a small decision," he said.

"Nothing small is left."

He almost corrected the wording, then thought better of it. "If you read openly, you lose control of what happens next."

Halima looked at the blackboard, at the three headings still ghosting there from the day's work. Household head. Duration of residence. Supporting relation. So clean. So hungry.

"I lost it the day those copied pages left my house," she said. "Tonight I just had to admit it."

Aminul exhaled through his nose. "If we do this, I need to change the order. I need Sufia held near the front if she's willing. I need the magistrate prepared for contested evidence rather than polished witness statements. And I need you to understand that once the ledger enters the record, there's no returning it to private status. Not legally. Not socially."

Halima almost said, It already left private status when men tore through my bedding. But that was only the loud version. The quieter leaving had happened when Sufia bent over copied sheets by lamplight and told herself she was making safety.

"Good," Halima said. "Then it can stop pretending."

Aminul gave one short nod. He was still afraid of the choice. So was she. Fear was not argument. He pulled the top witness sheet from under the bundle, read the first line, and then tore it once down the middle. He did it without theater and without looking at her for approval. The paper had become the wrong kind of paper.

He tore the next one too.

The sound was small but clean.

Halima laid a palm on the ledger. The cover board was rough where damp had raised the grain. Under her hand were shifted dates, borrowed fathers, rescued daughters, protected bastards, hidden mothers, children folded into households that had fed them if not birthed them. Under her hand were also openings for men like Mofiz, because paper kept its lines long after it forgot why they had been drawn. The leaf beneath her palm gave a dry little crack.

She had known that in pieces. Tonight it sat whole in her chest, heavy as wet paper.

When she left the school, the night had thickened. A strip of moon sat low over the river, too thin to light much. The ghat sounds had gone down to chain clink and water against wood. From somewhere west came a woman calling a child home by the full length of his name. The call crossed the char, struck tin, and came back smaller.

At her door Nura was waiting with the lamp turned low. She looked at Halima's empty hands first, then at her face, and nodded as if she had read the answer there already.

"Tomorrow?" she asked.

"Tomorrow."

Nura stepped aside. Halima went in. The shelf above the bedding stood bare where the bundle had sat for years, a rectangle of cleaner dust marking its old place. The sight of that empty patch hurt her more than she expected. She could still see the oilcloth there, damp at the corners in monsoon, smelling of mildew and her own hands. For years she had looked at that shelf and told herself the hidden bundle was keeping other people safe. The clean dust showed her something meaner: the book had also been keeping its power inside her room.

She lay down without undressing fully. The mat smelled of smoke and old reed. Through the wall the river kept moving its dark body past the char whether anybody's papers survived it or not. Sleep took its time. When it came, it carried no kindness in it: only page edges, wet fingerprints, the sound of a kettle being set down too hard, and the knowledge that by morning she would carry the oilcloth ledger into the schoolyard and it would no longer belong to her alone.


# Chapter 15
